10021{_}2{_}While he was at Carlsbad he heard the news.
10021{_}3{_}Then he received a letter from Edith, speaking with deference and solicitude of Bruce's rheumatism, entreating him to do the cure thoroughly, and suggesting that they should call the little girl Matilda, after a rich and sainted--though still living--aunt of Edith's.
10021{_}4{_}It might be an advantage to the child's future (in every sense) to have a godmother so wealthy and so religious.
10021{_}5{_}It appeared from the detailed description that the new daughter had, as a matter of course (and at two days old), long golden hair, far below her waist, sweeping lashes and pencilled brows, a rosebud mouth, an intellectual forehead, chiselled features and a tall, elegant figure.
10021{_}6{_}She was a magnificent, regal-looking creature and was a superb beauty of the classic type, and yet with it she was dainty and winsome.
10021{_}7{_}She had great talent for music.
10021{_}8{_}This, it appeared, was shown by the breadth between the eyes and the timbre of her voice.
10021{_}9{_}Overwhelmed with joy at the advent of such a paragon, and horrified at Edith's choice of a name, Bruce had replied at once by wire, impulsively:  _'Certainly not Matilda I would rather she were called Aspasia.
10021{_}10{_}'_  Edith read this expression of feeling on a colourless telegraph form, and as she was, at Knightsbridge, unable to hear the ironical tone of the message she took it literally.
10021{_}11{_}She criticised the name, but was easily persuaded by her mother-in-law to make no objection.
10021{_}12{_}The elder Mrs Ottley pointed out that it might have been very much worse.
10021{_}13{_}'But it's not a pretty name,' objected Edith.
10021{_}14{_}'If it wasn't to be Matilda, I should rather have called her something out of Maeterlinck--Ygraine, or Ysolyn--something like that.'
10021{_}15{_}'Yes, dear, Mygraine's a nice name, too,' said Mrs Ottley, in her humouring way, 'and so is Vaselyn.
10021{_}16{_}But what does it really matter?
10021{_}17{_}I shouldn't hold out on a point like this.
10021{_}18{_}One gets used to a name.
10021{_}19{_}Let the poor child be called Asparagus if he wishes it, and let him feel he has got his own way.'
10021{_}20{_}So the young girl was named Aspasia Matilda Ottley.
10021{_}21{_}It was characteristic of Edith that she kept to her own point, though not aggressively.
10021{_}22{_}When Bruce returned after his after-cure, it was too late to do anything but pretend he had meant it seriously.
10021{_}23{_}Archie called his sister Dilly.
10021{_}24{_}Archie had been rather hurt at the--as it seemed to him--unnecessary excitement about Dilly.
10021{_}25{_}Not that he was jealous in any way.
10021{_}26{_}It was rather that he was afraid it would spoil her to be made so much of at her age; make her, perhaps, egotistical and vain.
10021{_}27{_}But it was not Archie's way to show these fears openly.
10021{_}28{_}He did not weep loudly or throw things about as many boys might have done.
10021{_}29{_}His methods were more roundabout, more subtle.
10021{_}30{_}He gave hints and suggestions of his views that should have been understood by the intelligent.
10021{_}31{_}He said one morning with some indirectness:  'I had such a lovely dream last night, Mother.'
10021{_}32{_}'Did you, pet?
10021{_}33{_}How sweet of you.
10021{_}34{_}What was it?'
10021{_}35{_}'Oh, nothing much.
10021{_}36{_}It was all right.
10021{_}37{_}Very nice.
10021{_}38{_}It was a lovely dream.
10021{_}39{_}I dreamt I was in heaven.'
10021{_}40{_}'Really!
10021{_}41{_}How delightful.
10021{_}42{_}Who was there?'
10021{_}43{_}This is always a woman's first question.
10021{_}44{_}'Oh, you were there, of course.
10021{_}45{_}And father.
10021{_}46{_}Nurse, too.
10021{_}47{_}It was a lovely dream.
10021{_}48{_}Such a nice place.'
10021{_}49{_}'Was Dilly there?'
10021{_}50{_}'Dilly?
10021{_}51{_}Er--no--no--she wasn't.
10021{_}52{_}She was in the night nursery, with Satan.'
10021{_}53{_}Sometimes Edith thought that her daughter's names were decidedly a failure--Aspasia by mistake, Matilda through obstinacy, and Dilly by accident.
10021{_}54{_}However the child herself was a success.
10021{_}55{_}She was four years old when the incident occurred about the Mitchells.
10021{_}56{_}The whole of this story turns eventually on the Mitchells.
10021{_}57{_}The Ottleys lived in a concise white flat at Knightsbridge.
10021{_}58{_}Bruce's father had some time ago left him a good income on certain conditions; one was that he was not to leave the Foreign Office before he was fifty.
10021{_}59{_}One afternoon Edith was talking to the telephone in a voice of agonised entreaty that would have melted the hardest of hearts, but did not seem to have much effect on the Exchange, which, evidently, was not responsive to pathos that day.
10021{_}60{_}'Oh!
10021{_}61{_}Exchange, _why_ are you ringing off?
10021{_}62{_}_Please_ try again.... Do I want any number?
10021{_}63{_}Yes, I do want any number, of course, or why should I ring up?...
10021{_}64{_}I want 6375 Gerrard.'
10021{_}65{_}Here Archie interposed.
10021{_}66{_}'Mother, can I have your long buttonhook?'
10021{_}67{_}'No, Archie, you can't just now, dear.... Go away Archie....
10021{_}68{_}Yes, I said 6375 Gerrard.
10021{_}69{_}Only 6375 Gerrard!...
10021{_}70{_}Are you there?
10021{_}71{_}Oh, don't keep on asking me if I've got them!...
10021{_}72{_}No, they haven't answered.... Are you 6375?...
10021{_}73{_}Oh--wrong number--sorry.... 6375 Gerrard?
10021{_}74{_}Only six--are you there?...
10021{_}75{_}Not 6375 Gerrard?...
10021{_}76{_}Are you anyone else?...
10021{_}77{_}Oh, is it you, Vincy?...
10021{_}78{_}I want to tell you--'  'Mother, can I have your long buttonhook?'
10021{_}79{_}Here Bruce came in.
10021{_}80{_}Edith rang off.
10021{_}81{_}Archie disappeared.
10021{_}82{_}'It's really rather wonderful, Edith, what that Sandow exerciser has done for me!
10021{_}83{_}You laughed at me at first, but I've improved marvellously.'
10021{_}84{_}Bruce was walking about doing very mild gymnastics, and occasionally hitting himself on the left arm with the right fist.'
10021{_}85{_}Look at my muscle--look at it--and all in such a short time!'
10021{_}86{_}'Wonderful!'
10021{_}87{_}said Edith.
10021{_}88{_}'The reason I know what an extraordinary effect these few days have had on me is something I have just done which I couldn't have done before.
10021{_}89{_}Of course I'm naturally a very powerful man, and only need a little--'  'What have you done?'
10021{_}90{_}'Why--you know that great ridiculous old wooden chest that your awful Aunt Matilda sent you for your birthday--absurd present I call it--mere lumber.'
10021{_}91{_}'Yes?'
10021{_}92{_}'When it came I could barely push it from one side of the room to the other.
10021{_}93{_}Now I've lifted it from your room to the box-room.
10021{_}94{_}Quite easily.
10021{_}95{_}Pretty good, isn't it?'
10021{_}96{_}'Yes, of course it's very good for you to do all these exercises; no doubt it's capital.... Er--you know I've had all the things taken out of the chest since you tried it before, don't you?'
10021{_}97{_}'Things--what things?
10021{_}98{_}I didn't know there was anything in it.'
10021{_}99{_}'Only a silver tea-service, and a couple of salvers,' said Edith, in a low voice....
10021{_}100{_}...He calmed down fairly soon and said: 'Edith, I have some news for you.
10021{_}101{_}You know the Mitchells?'
10021{_}102{_}'Do I know the _Mitchells_?
10021{_}103{_}Mitchell, your hero in your office, that you're always being offended with--at _least_ I know the Mitchells by _name_.
10021{_}104{_}I ought to.'
10021{_}105{_}'Well, what do you think they've done?
10021{_}106{_}They've asked us to dinner.'
10021{_}107{_}'Have they?
10021{_}108{_}Fancy!'
10021{_}109{_}'Yes, and what I thought was so particularly jolly of him was that it was a verbal invitation.
10021{_}110{_}Mitchell said to me, just like this, 'Ottley, old chap, are you doing anything on Sunday evening?''
10021{_}111{_}Here Archie came to the door and said, 'Mother, can I have your long buttonhook?'
10021{_}112{_}Edith shook her head and frowned.
10021{_}113{_}''Ottley, old chap,'' continued Bruce, ''are you and your wife doing anything on Sunday?
10021{_}114{_}If not, I do wish you would waive ceremony and come and dine with us.
10021{_}115{_}Would Mrs Ottley excuse a verbal invitation, do you think?'
10021{_}116{_}I said, 'Well, Mitchell, as a matter of fact I don't believe we have got anything on.
10021{_}117{_}Yes, old boy, we shall be delighted.'
10021{_}118{_}I accepted, you see.
10021{_}119{_}I accepted straight out.
10021{_}120{_}When you're treated in a friendly way, I always say why be unfriendly?
10021{_}121{_}And Mrs Mitchell is a charming little woman--I'm sure you'd like her.
10021{_}122{_}It seems she's been dying to know you.'
10021{_}123{_}'Fancy!
10021{_}124{_}I wonder she's still alive, then, because you and Mitchell have known each other for eight years, and I've never met her yet.'
10021{_}125{_}'Well, you will now.
10021{_}126{_}Let bygones be bygones.
10021{_}127{_}They live in Hamilton Place.'
10021{_}128{_}'Oh yes....Park Lane?'
10021{_}129{_}'I told you he was doing very well, and his wife has private means.'
10021{_}130{_}'Mother,' Archie began again, like a litany, 'can I have your long buttonhook?
10021{_}131{_}I know where it is.'
10021{_}132{_}'No, Archie, certainly not; you can't fasten laced boots with a buttonhook.... Well, that will be fun, Bruce.'
10021{_}133{_}'I believe they're going to have games after dinner,' said Bruce.
10021{_}134{_}'All very jolly--musical crambo--that sort of thing.... What shall you wear, Edith?'
10021{_}135{_}'Mother, do let me have your long buttonhook.
10021{_}136{_}I want it.
10021{_}137{_}It isn't for my boots.'
10021{_}138{_}'_Certainly_ not.
10021{_}139{_}What a nuisance you are!
10021{_}140{_}Do go away....
10021{_}141{_}I think I shall wear my salmon-coloured dress with the sort of mayonnaise- coloured sash.... (No, you're not to have it, Archie).'
10021{_}142{_}'But, Mother, I've got it....
10021{_}143{_}I can soon mend it, Mother.'
10021{_}144{_}On Sunday evening Bruce's high spirits seemed to flag; he had one of his sudden reactions.
10021{_}145{_}He looked at everything on its dark side.
10021{_}146{_}'What on earth's that thing in your hair, Edith?'
10021{_}147{_}'It's a bandeau.'
10021{_}148{_}'I don't like it.
10021{_}149{_}Your hair looks very nice without it.
10021{_}150{_}What on _earth_ did you get it for?'
10021{_}151{_}'For about six-and-eleven, I think.'
10021{_}152{_}'Don't be trivial, Edith.
10021{_}153{_}We shall be late.
10021{_}154{_}Ah!
10021{_}155{_}It really does seem rather a pity, the very first time one dines with people like the Mitchells.'
10021{_}156{_}'We sha'n't be late, Bruce.
10021{_}157{_}It's eight o'clock, and eight o'clock I suppose means--well, eight.
10021{_}158{_}Sure you've got the number right?'
10021{_}159{_}'Really.
10021{_}160{_}Edith!...
10021{_}161{_}My memory is unerring, dear.
10021{_}162{_}I never make a mistake.
10021{_}163{_}Haven't you ever noticed it?'
10021{_}164{_}'A--oh yes--I think I have.'
10021{_}165{_}'Well, it's 168 Hamilton Place.
10021{_}166{_}Look sharp, dear.'
10021{_}167{_}On their way in the taxi he gave her a good many instructions and advised her to be perfectly at her ease and _absolutely natural_; there was nothing to make one otherwise, in either Mr or Mrs Mitchell.
10021{_}168{_}Also, he said, it didn't matter a bit what she wore, as long as she had put on her _best_ dress.
10021{_}169{_}It seemed a pity she had not got a new one, but this couldn't be helped, as there was now no time.
10021{_}170{_}Edith agreed that she knew of no really suitable place where she could buy a new evening dress at eight-thirty on Sunday evening.
10021{_}171{_}And, anyhow, he said, she looked quite nice, really very smart; besides, Mrs Mitchell was not the sort of person who would think any the less of a pretty woman for being a little dowdy and out of fashion.
10021{_}172{_}When they drove up to what house agents call in their emotional way a superb, desirable, magnificent town mansion, they saw that a large dinner-party was evidently going on.
10021{_}173{_}A hall porter and four powdered footmen were in evidence.
10021{_}174{_}'By Jove!'
10021{_}175{_}said Bruce, as he got out, 'I'd no idea old Mitchell did himself so well as this.'...
10021{_}176{_}The butler had never heard of the Mitchells.
10021{_}177{_}The house belonged to Lord Rosenberg.
10021{_}178{_}'Confound it!
10021{_}179{_}'said Bruce, as he flung himself into the taxi.
10021{_}180{_}'Well!
10021{_}181{_}I've made a mistake for once in my life.
10021{_}182{_}I admit it.
10021{_}183{_}Of course, it's really Hamilton Gardens.
10021{_}184{_}Sorry.
10021{_}185{_}Yet somehow I'm rather glad Mitchell doesn't live in that house.'
10021{_}186{_}'You are perfectly right,' said Edith: 'the bankruptcy of an old friend and colleague could be no satisfaction to any man.'
10021{_}187{_}Hamilton Gardens was a gloomy little place, like a tenement building out of Marylebone Road.
10021{_}188{_}Bruce, in trying to ring the bell, unfortunately turned out all the electric light in the house, and was standing alone in despair in the dark when, fortunately the porter, who had been out to post a letter, ran back, and turned up the light again.... 'I shouldn't have thought they could play musical crambo here, 'he called out to Edith while he was waiting.
10021{_}189{_}'And now isn't it odd?
10021{_}190{_}I have a funny kind of feeling that the right address is Hamilton House.'
10021{_}191{_}'I suppose you're perfectly certain they don't live at a private idiot asylum?'
10021{_}192{_}Edith suggested doubtfully.
10021{_}193{_}On inquiry it appeared the Mitchells did not live at Hamilton Gardens.
10021{_}194{_}An idea occurred to Edith, and she asked for a directory.
10021{_}195{_}The Winthrop Mitchells lived at Hamilton Terrace, St John's Wood.
10021{_}196{_}'At last!'
10021{_}197{_}said Bruce.
10021{_}198{_}'Now we shall be too disgracefully late for the first time.
10021{_}199{_}But be perfectly at your ease, dear.
10021{_}200{_}Promise me that.
10021{_}201{_}Go in quite naturally.'
10021{_}202{_}'How else can I go in?'
10021{_}203{_}'I mean as if nothing had happened.'
10021{_}204{_}'I think we'd better tell them what _has_ happened,' said Edith; 'it will make them laugh.
10021{_}205{_}I hope they will have begun their dinner.'
10021{_}206{_}'Surely they will have finished it.'
10021{_}207{_}'Perhaps we may find them at their games!'
10021{_}208{_}'Now, now, don't be bitter, Edith dear--never be bitter--life has its ups and downs.... Well!
10021{_}209{_}I'm rather glad, after all, that Mitchell doesn't live in that horrid little hole.'
10021{_}210{_}'I'm sure you are,' said Edith; 'it could be no possible satisfaction to you to know that a friend and colleague of yours is either distressingly hard up or painfully penurious.'
10021{_}211{_}They arrived at the house, but there were no lights, and no sign of life.
10021{_}212{_}The Mitchells lived here all right, but they were out.
10021{_}213{_}The parlourmaid explained.
10021{_}214{_}The dinner-party had been Saturday, the night before....  'Strange,' said Bruce, as he got in again.
10021{_}215{_}'I had a curious presentiment that something was going wrong about this dinner at the Mitchells'.'
10021{_}216{_}'What dinner at the Mitchells'?
10021{_}217{_}There doesn't seem to be any.'
10021{_}218{_}'Do you know,' Bruce continued his train of thought, 'I felt certain somehow that it would be a failure.
10021{_}219{_}Wasn't it odd?
10021{_}220{_}I often think I'm a pessimist, and yet look how well I'm taking it.
10021{_}221{_}I'm more like a fatalist--sometimes I hardly know what I am.'
10021{_}222{_}'I could tell you what you are,' said Edith, 'but I won't, because now you must take me to the Carlton.
10021{_}223{_}We shall get there before it's closed.'
10021{_}224{_}CHAPTER II  Opera Glasses  Whether to behave with some coolness to Mitchell, and be stand-offish, as though it had been all his fault, or to be lavishly apologetic, was the question.
10021{_}225{_}Bruce could not make up his mind which attitude to take.
10021{_}226{_}In a way, it was all the Mitchells' fault.
10021{_}227{_}They oughtn't to have given him a verbal invitation.
10021{_}228{_}It was rude, Bohemian, wanting in good form; it showed an absolute and complete ignorance of the most ordinary and elementary usages of society.
10021{_}229{_}It was wanting in common courtesy; really, when one came to think about it, it was an insult.
10021{_}230{_}On the other hand, technically, Bruce was in the wrong.
10021{_}231{_}Having accepted he ought to have turned up on the right night.
10021{_}232{_}It may have served them right (as he said), but the fact of going on the wrong night being a lesson to them seemed a little obscure.
10021{_}233{_}Edith found it difficult to see the point.
10021{_}234{_}Then he had a more brilliant idea; to go into the office as cheerily as ever, and say to Mitchell pleasantly, 'We're looking forward to next Saturday, old chap,' pretending to have believed from the first that the invitation had been for the Saturday week; and that the dinner was still to come....
10021{_}235{_}This, Edith said, would have been excellent, provided that the parlourmaid hadn't told them that she and Bruce had arrived about a quarter to ten on Sunday evening and asked if the Mitchells had begun dinner.
10021{_}236{_}The chances against the servant having kept this curious incident to herself were almost too great.
10021{_}237{_}After long argument and great indecision the matter was settled by a cordial letter from Mrs Mitchell, asking them to dinner on the following Thursday, and saying she feared there had been some mistake.
10021{_}238{_}So that was all right.
10021{_}239{_}Bruce was in good spirits again; he was pleased too, because he was going to the theatre that evening with Edith and Vincy, to see a play that he thought wouldn't be very good.
10021{_}240{_}He had almost beforehand settled what he thought of it, and practically what he intended to say.
10021{_}241{_}But when he came in that evening he was overheard to have a strenuous and increasingly violent argument with Archie in the hall.
10021{_}242{_}Edith opened the door and wanted to know what the row was about.
10021{_}243{_}'Will you tell me, Edith, where your son learns such language?
10021{_}244{_}He keeps on worrying me to take him to the Zoological Gardens to see the--well--you'll hear what he says.
10021{_}245{_}The child's a perfect nuisance.
10021{_}246{_}Who put it into his head to want to go and see this animal?
10021{_}247{_}I was obliged to speak quite firmly to him about it.'
10021{_}248{_}Edith was not alarmed that Bruce had been severe.
10021{_}249{_}She thought it much more likely that Archie had spoken very firmly to him.
10021{_}250{_}He was always strict with his father, and when he was good Bruce found fault with him.
10021{_}251{_}As soon as he grew really tiresome his father became abjectly apologetic.
10021{_}252{_}Archie was called and came in, dragging his feet, and pouting, in tears that he was making a strenuous effort to encourage.
10021{_}253{_}'You must be firm with him,' continued Bruce.
10021{_}254{_}'Hang it!
10021{_}255{_}Good heavens!
10021{_}256{_}Am I master in my own house or am I not?'
10021{_}257{_}There was no reply to this rhetorical question.
10021{_}258{_}He turned to Archie and said in a gentle, conciliating voice:  'Archie, old chap, tell your mother what it is you want to see.
10021{_}259{_}Don't cry, dear.'
10021{_}260{_}'Want to see the damned chameleon,' said Archie, with his hands in his eyes.
10021{_}261{_}'Want father to take me to the Zoo.'
10021{_}262{_}'You can't go to the Zoo this time of the evening.
10021{_}263{_}What do you mean?'
10021{_}264{_}'I want to see the damned chameleon.'
10021{_}265{_}'You hear!'
10021{_}266{_}exclaimed Bruce to Edith.
10021{_}267{_}'Who taught you this language?'
10021{_}268{_}'Miss Townsend taught it me.'
10021{_}269{_}'There!
10021{_}270{_}It's dreadful, Edith; he's becoming a reckless liar.
10021{_}271{_}Fancy her dreaming of teaching him such things!
10021{_}272{_}If she did, of course she must be mad, and you must send her away at once.
10021{_}273{_}But I'm quite sure she didn't.'
10021{_}274{_}'Come, Archie, you know Miss Townsend never taught you to say that.
10021{_}275{_}What have you got into your head?'
10021{_}276{_}'Well, she didn't exactly teach me to say it--she didn't give me lessons in it--but she says it herself.
10021{_}277{_}She said the damned chameleon was lovely; and I want to see it.
10021{_}278{_}She didn't say I ought to see it.
10021{_}279{_}But I want to.
10021{_}280{_}I've been wanting to ever since.
10021{_}281{_}She said it at lunch today, and I do want to.
10021{_}282{_}Lots of other boys go to the Zoo, and why shouldn't I?
10021{_}283{_}I want to see it so much.'
10021{_}284{_}'Edith, I must speak to Miss Townsend about this very seriously.
10021{_}285{_}In the first place, people have got no right to talk about queer animals to the boy at all--we all know what he is--and in such language!
10021{_}286{_}I should have thought a girl like Miss Townsend, who has passed examinations in Germany, and so forth, would have had more sense of her responsibility--more tact.
10021{_}287{_}It shows a dreadful want of--I hardly know what to think of it--the daughter of a clergyman, too!'
10021{_}288{_}'It's all right, Bruce,' Edith laughed.
10021{_}289{_}'Miss Townsend told me she had been to see the _Dame aux Camelias_ some time ago.
10021{_}290{_}She was enthusiastic about it.
10021{_}291{_}Archie dear, I'll take you to the Zoological Gardens and we'll see lots of other animals.
10021{_}292{_}And don't use that expression.'
10021{_}293{_}'What!
10021{_}294{_}Can't I see the da--'  'Mr Vincy,' announced the servant.
10021{_}295{_}'I must go and dress,' said Bruce.
10021{_}296{_}Vincy Wenham Vincy was always called by everyone simply Vincy.
10021{_}297{_}Applied to him it seemed like a pet name.
10021{_}298{_}He had arrived at the right moment, as he always did.
10021{_}299{_}He was very devoted to both Edith and Bruce, and he was a confidant of both.
10021{_}300{_}He sometimes said to Edith that he felt he was just what was wanted in the little home; an intimate stranger coming in occasionally with a fresh atmosphere was often of great value (as, for instance, now) in calming or averting storms.
10021{_}301{_}Had anyone asked Vincy exactly what he was he would probably have said he was an Observer, and really he did very little else, though after he left Oxford he had taken to writing a little, and painting less.
10021{_}302{_}He was very fair, the fairest person one could imagine over five years old.
10021{_}303{_}He had pale silky hair, a minute fair moustache, very good features, a single eyeglass, and the appearance, always, of having been very recently taken out of a bandbox.
10021{_}304{_}But when people fancied from this look of his that he was an empty-headed fop they soon found themselves immensely mistaken.
10021{_}305{_}He was thirty-eight, but looked a gilded youth of twenty; and _was_ sufficiently gilded (as he said), not perhaps exactly to be comfortable, but to enable him to get about comfortably, and see those who were.
10021{_}306{_}He had a number of relatives in high places, who bored him, and were always trying to get him married.
10021{_}307{_}He had taken up various occupations and travelled a good deal.
10021{_}308{_}But his greatest pleasure was the study of people.
10021{_}309{_}There was nothing cold in his observation, nothing of the cynical analyst.
10021{_}310{_}He was impulsive, though very quiet, immensely and ardently sympathetic and almost too impressionable and enthusiastic.
10021{_}311{_}It was not surprising that he was immensely popular generally, as well as specially; he was so interested in everyone except himself.
10021{_}312{_}No-one was ever a greater general favourite.
10021{_}313{_}There seemed to be no type of person on whom he jarred.
10021{_}314{_}People who disagreed on every other subject agreed in liking Vincy.
10021{_}315{_}But he did not care in the least for acquaintances, and spent much ingenuity in trying to avoid them; he only liked intimate friends, and of all he had perhaps the Ottleys were his greatest favourites.
10021{_}316{_}His affection for them dated from a summer they had spent in the same hotel in France.
10021{_}317{_}He had become extraordinarily interested in them.
10021{_}318{_}He delighted in Bruce, but had with Edith, of course, more mutual understanding and intellectual sympathy, and though they met constantly, his friendship with her had never been misunderstood.
10021{_}319{_}Frivolous friends of his who did not know her might amuse themselves by being humorous and flippant about Vincy's little Ottleys, but no-one who had ever seen them together could possibly make a mistake.
10021{_}320{_}They were an example of the absurdity of a tradition--'the world's' proneness to calumny.
10021{_}321{_}Such friendships, when genuine, are never misconstrued.
10021{_}322{_}Perhaps society is more often taken in the other way.
10021{_}323{_}But as a matter of fact the truth on this subject, as on most others, is always known in time.
10021{_}324{_}No-one had ever even tried to explain away the intimacy, though Bruce had all the air of being unable to do without Vincy's society sometimes cynically attributed to husbands in a different position.
10021{_}325{_}Vincy was pleased with the story of the Mitchells that Edith told him, and she was glad to hear that he knew the Mitchells and had been to the house.
10021{_}326{_}'How like you to know everyone.
10021{_}327{_}What did they do?'
10021{_}328{_}'The night I was there they played games,' said Vincy.
10021{_}329{_}He spoke in a soft, even voice.
10021{_}330{_}'It was just a little--well--perhaps just a _tiny_ bit ghastly, I thought; but don't tell Bruce.
10021{_}331{_}That evening I thought the people weren't quite young enough, and when they played 'Oranges and Lemons, and the Bells of St Clements,' and so on--their bones seemed to--well, sort of rattle, if you know what I mean.
10021{_}332{_}But still perhaps it was only my fancy.
10021{_}333{_}Mitchell has such very high spirits, you see, and is determined to make everything go.
10021{_}334{_}He won't have conventional parties, and insists on plenty of verve; so, of course, one's forced to have it.'
10021{_}335{_}He sighed.
10021{_}336{_}'They haven't any children, and they make a kind of hobby of entertaining in an unconventional way.'
10021{_}337{_}'It sounds rather fun.
10021{_}338{_}Perhaps you will be asked next Thursday.
10021{_}339{_}Try.'
10021{_}340{_}'I'll try.
10021{_}341{_}I'll call, and remind her of me.
10021{_}342{_}I daresay she'll ask me.
10021{_}343{_}She's very good-natured.
10021{_}344{_}She believes in spiritualism, too.'
10021{_}345{_}'I wonder who'll be there?'
10021{_}346{_}'Anyone might be there, or anyone else.
10021{_}347{_}As they say of marriage, it's a lottery.
10021{_}348{_}They might have roulette, or a spiritual seance, or Kubelik, or fancy dress heads.'
10021{_}349{_}'Fancy dress heads!'
10021{_}350{_}'Yes.
10021{_}351{_}Or a cotillion, or just bridge.
10021{_}352{_}You never know.
10021{_}353{_}The house is rather like a country house, and they behave accordingly.
10021{_}354{_}Even hide-and-seek, I believe, sometimes.
10021{_}355{_}And Mitchell adores unpractical jokes, too.'
10021{_}356{_}'I see.
10021{_}357{_}It's rather exciting that I'm going to the Mitchells at last.'
10021{_}358{_}'Yes, perhaps it will be the turning-point of your life,' said Vincy.
10021{_}359{_}'Ah!
10021{_}360{_}here's Bruce.'
10021{_}361{_}'I don't think much of that opera glass your mother gave you,' Bruce remarked to his wife, soon after the curtain rose.
10021{_}362{_}'It's the fashion,' said Edith.
10021{_}363{_}'It's jade--the latest thing.'
10021{_}364{_}'I don't care if it is the fashion.
10021{_}365{_}It's no use.
10021{_}366{_}Here, try it, Vincy.'
10021{_}367{_}He handed it to Vincy, who gave Bruce a quick look, and then tried it.
10021{_}368{_}'Rather quaint and pretty, I think.
10021{_}369{_}I like the effect,' he said, handing it back to Bruce.
10021{_}370{_}'It may be quaint and pretty, and it may be the latest thing, and it may be jade,' said Bruce rather sarcastically, 'but I'm not a slave to fashion.
10021{_}371{_}I never was.
10021{_}372{_}And I don't see any use whatever in an opera glass that makes everything look smaller instead of larger, and at a greater distance instead of nearer.
10021{_}373{_}I call it rot.
10021{_}374{_}I always say what I think.
10021{_}375{_}And you can tell your mother what I said if you like.'
10021{_}376{_}'You're looking through it the wrong side, dear,' said Edith.
10021{_}377{_}CHAPTER III  The Golden Quoribus  Edith had been very pretty at twenty, but at twenty-eight her prettiness had immensely increased; she had really become a beauty of a particularly troubling type.
10021{_}378{_}She had long, deep blue eyes, clearly-cut features, hair of that soft, fine light brown just tinged with red called by the French chatain clair; and a flower-like complexion.
10021{_}379{_}She was slim, but not angular, and had a reposeful grace and a decided attraction for both men and women.
10021{_}380{_}They generally tried to express this fascination by discovering resemblances in her to various well-known pictures of celebrated artists.
10021{_}381{_}She had been compared to almost every type of all the great painters: Botticelli, Sir Peter Lely, Gainsborough, Burne-Jones.
10021{_}382{_}Some people said she was like a Sargent, others called her a post-impressionist type; there was no end to the old and new masters of whom she seemed to remind people; and she certainly had the rather insidious charm of somehow recalling the past while suggesting something undiscovered in the future.
10021{_}383{_}There was a good deal that was enigmatic about her.
10021{_}384{_}It was natural, not assumed as a pose of mysteriousness.
10021{_}385{_}She was not all on the surface: not obvious.
10021{_}386{_}One wondered.
10021{_}387{_}Was she capable of any depth of feeling?
10021{_}388{_}Was she always just sweet and tactful and clever, or could there be another side to her character?
10021{_}389{_}Had she (for instance) a temperament?
10021{_}390{_}This question was considered one of interest,--so Edith had a great many admirers.
10021{_}391{_}Some were new and fickle, others were old and faithful.
10021{_}392{_}She had never yet shown more than a conversational interest in any of them, but always seemed to be laughing with a soft mockery at her own success.
10021{_}393{_}Edith was not a vain woman, not even much interested in dress, though she had a quick eye and a sure impressionistic gift for it.
10021{_}394{_}She was always an immense favourite with women, who felt subconsciously grateful to her for her wonderful forbearance.
10021{_}395{_}To have the power and not to use it!
10021{_}396{_}To be so pretty, yet never _to take_ _anyone away_!--not even coldly display her conquests.
10021{_}397{_}But this liking she did not, as a rule, return in any decided fashion.
10021{_}398{_}She had dreadfully little to say to the average woman, except to a few intimate friends, and frankly preferred the society of the average man, although she had not as yet developed a taste for coquetry, for which she had, however, many natural gifts.
10021{_}399{_}She was much taken up by Bruce, by Archie and Dilly, and was fond of losing herself in ideas and in books, and in various artistic movements and fads in which her interest was cultivated and perhaps inspired by Vincy.
10021{_}400{_}Vincy was her greatest friend and confidant.
10021{_}401{_}He was really a great safety-valve, and she told him nearly every thought.
10021{_}402{_}Still, Archie was, so far, her greatest interest.
10021{_}403{_}He was a particularly pretty boy, and she was justified in thinking him rather unusual.
10021{_}404{_}At this period he spent a considerable amount of his leisure time not only in longing to see real animals, but in inventing and drawing pictures of non-existent ones--horrible creatures, or quaint creatures, for which he found the strangest names.
10021{_}405{_}He told Dilly about them, but Dilly was not his audience--she was rather his confidante and literary adviser; or even sometimes his collaborator.
10021{_}406{_}His public consisted principally of his mother.
10021{_}407{_}It was a convention that Edith should be frightened, shocked and horrified at the creatures of his imagination, while Dilly privately revelled in their success.
10021{_}408{_}Miss Townsend, the governess, was rather coldly ignored in this matter.
10021{_}409{_}She had a way of speaking of the animals with a smile, as a nice occupation to keep the children quiet.
10021{_}410{_}She did not understand.
10021{_}411{_}'Please, Madam, would you kindly go into the nursery; Master Archie wishes you to come and hear about the golden--something he's just made up like,' said Dilly's nurse with an expression of resignation.
10021{_}412{_}Edith jumped up at once.
10021{_}413{_}'Oh dear!
10021{_}414{_}Tell Master Archie I'm coming.'
10021{_}415{_}She ran into the nursery and found Archie and Dilly both looking rather excited; Archie, fairly self-controlled, with a paper in his hand on which was a rough sketch which he would not let her see, and hid behind him.
10021{_}416{_}'Mother,' Archie began in a low, solemn voice, rather slowly, 'the golden quoribus is the most horrible animal, the most awful-looking animal, you ever heard of in _your_ life!'
10021{_}417{_}'Oh-h-h!
10021{_}418{_}How awful!'
10021{_}419{_}said Edith, beginning to shiver.
10021{_}420{_}'Wait a moment--let me sit down quietly and hear about it.'
10021{_}421{_}She sat down by the fire and clasped her hands, looking at him with a terrified expression which was part of the ritual.
10021{_}422{_}Dilly giggled, and put her thumb in her mouth, watching the effect with widely opened eyes.
10021{_}423{_}'Much more awful than the gazeka, of course, I suppose?'
10021{_}424{_}Edith said rather rashly.
10021{_}425{_}'Much,' said Dilly.
10021{_}426{_}'(Be quiet, Dilly!)
10021{_}427{_}Mother!'
10021{_}428{_}he was reproachful, 'what do you mean?
10021{_}429{_}The gazeka?
10021{_}430{_}Why--the gazeka's nothing at all--it's a rotten little animal.
10021{_}431{_}It doesn't count.
10021{_}432{_}Besides, it isn't real--it never was real.
10021{_}433{_}Gazeka, indeed!'
10021{_}434{_}'Oh, I beg your pardon,' said Edith repentantly; 'do go on.'
10021{_}435{_}'No... the golden quoribus is far-ar-r-r-r more frightening even than the jilbery.
10021{_}436{_}Do you remember how awful _that_ was?
10021{_}437{_}And much larger.'
10021{_}438{_}'What!
10021{_}439{_}Worse than the jilbery!
10021{_}440{_}Oh, good gracious!
10021{_}441{_}How dreadful!
10021{_}442{_}What's it like?'
10021{_}443{_}'First of all--it's as long as from here to Brighton,' said Archie.
10021{_}444{_}'A little longer,' said Dilly.
10021{_}445{_}'(Shut up, miss!)
10021{_}446{_}As long.
10021{_}447{_}It's called the golden quoribus because it's bright gold, except the bumps; and the bumps are green.'
10021{_}448{_}'Bright green,' said Dilly.
10021{_}449{_}'(Oh, will you hold your tongue, Dilly?)
10021{_}450{_}Green.'
10021{_}451{_}'How terrible!...
10021{_}452{_}And what shape is it?'
10021{_}453{_}'All pointed and sharp, and three-cornered.'
10021{_}454{_}'Does it breathe fire?'
10021{_}455{_}asked Edith.
10021{_}456{_}Archie smiled contemptuously.
10021{_}457{_}'Breathe fire!
10021{_}458{_}Oh, Mother!
10021{_}459{_}Do you think it's a silly dragon in a fairy story?
10021{_}460{_}Of course it doesn't.
10021{_}461{_}How can it breathe fire?'
10021{_}462{_}'Sorry,' said Edith apologetically.
10021{_}463{_}'Go on.'
10021{_}464{_}'_But_, the peculiar thing about it, besides that it lives entirely on muffins and mutton and the frightening part, I'm coming to now.'
10021{_}465{_}He became emphatic, and spoke slowly.
10021{_}466{_}'The golden quoribus has more claws than any... other... animal... in the whole world!'
10021{_}467{_}'Oh-h-h,' she shuddered.
10021{_}468{_}'Yes,' said Archie solemnly.
10021{_}469{_}'It has large claws coming out of its head.'
10021{_}470{_}'Its head!
10021{_}471{_}Good gracious!'
10021{_}472{_}'It has claws here and claws there; claws coming out of the eyes; and claws coming out of the ears; and claws coming out of its shoulders; and claws coming out of the forehead!'
10021{_}473{_}Edith shivered with fright and held up her hands in front of her eyes to ward off the picture.
10021{_}474{_}'And claws coming out of the mouth,' said Archie, coming a step nearer to her and raising his voice.
10021{_}475{_}Edith jumped.
10021{_}476{_}'And claws coming out of the hands, and claws coming out of the feet!'
10021{_}477{_}'Yes,' said Dilly, wildly and recklessly and jumping up and down, 'and claws on the ceiling, and claws on the floor, and claws all over the world!'
10021{_}478{_}With one violent slap she was sent sprawling.
10021{_}479{_}Shrieks, sobs and tears filled the quiet nursery.
10021{_}480{_}'I know,' said Archie, when he had been persuaded to apologise, 'of course I know a gentleman oughtn't to hit a lady, not even--I mean, especially not if she's his little sister.
10021{_}481{_}But oh, Mother, ought a lady to interrupt a story?'
10021{_}482{_}When Edith told Vincy he entirely took Archie's side.
10021{_}483{_}Suppose Sargent were painting a beautiful picture, and one of his pupils, snatching the paint-brush from him, insisted on finishing it, and spoiling it--how would he like it?
10021{_}484{_}Imagine a poet who had just written a great poem, and been interrupted in reciting it by someone who quickly finished it off all wrong!
10021{_}485{_}The author might be forgiven under such circumstances if in his irritation he took a strong line.
10021{_}486{_}In Vincy's opinion it served Dilly jolly well right.
10021{_}487{_}Young?
10021{_}488{_}Of course she was young, but four (he said) was not a day too soon to begin to learn to respect the work of the artist.
10021{_}489{_}Edith owned that Archie was not easily exasperated and was as a rule very patient with the child.
10021{_}490{_}Bruce took an entirely different view.
10021{_}491{_}He was quite gloomy about it and feared that Archie showed every sign of growing up to be an Apache.
10021{_}492{_}CHAPTER IV  The Mitchells  The Mitchells were, as Vincy had said, extremely hospitable; they had a perfect mania for receiving; they practically lived for it, and the big house at Hampstead, with its large garden covered in, and a sort of studio built out, was scarcely ever without guests.
10021{_}493{_}When they didn't have some sort of party they invariably went out.
10021{_}494{_}Mitchell's great joy was to make his parties different from others by some childish fantasy or other.
10021{_}495{_}He especially delighted in a surprise.
10021{_}496{_}He often took the trouble (for instance) to have a telegram sent to every one of his guests during the course of the evening.
10021{_}497{_}Each of these wires contained some personal chaff or practical joke.
10021{_}498{_}At other times he would give everyone little presents, concealed in some way.
10021{_}499{_}Christmas didn't come once a year to the Mitchells; it seemed never to go away.
10021{_}500{_}One was always surprised not to find a Christmas tree and crackers.
10021{_}501{_}These entertainments, always splendidly done materially, and curiously erratic socially, were sometimes extremely amusing; at others, of course, a frost; it was rather a toss-up.
10021{_}502{_}And the guests were, without exception, the most extraordinary mixture in London.
10021{_}503{_}They included delightful people, absurd people, average people; people who were smart and people who were dowdy, some who were respectable and nothing else, some who were deplorable, others beautiful, and many merely dull.
10021{_}504{_}There was never the slightest attempt at any sort of harmonising, or of suitability; there was a great deal of kindness to the hard-up, and a wild and extravagant delight in any novelty.
10021{_}505{_}In fact, the Mitchells were everything except exclusive, and as they were not guided by any sort of rule, they really lived, in St John's Wood, superior to suburban or indeed any other restrictions.
10021{_}506{_}They would ask the same guests to dinner time after time, six or seven times in succession.
10021{_}507{_}They would invite cordially a person of no attraction whatsoever whom they had only just met, and they would behave with casual coolness to desirable acquaintances or favourite friends whom they had known all their lives.
10021{_}508{_}However, there was no doubt that their parties had got the name for being funny, and that was quite enough.
10021{_}509{_}London people in every set are so desperate for something out of the ordinary way, for variety and oddness, that the Mitchells were frequently asked for invitations by most distinguished persons who hoped, in their blase fatigue, to meet something new and queer.
10021{_}510{_}For the real Londoner is a good deal of a child, and loves Punch and Judy shows, and conjuring tricks (symbolically speaking)--and is also often dreaming of the chance of meeting some spring novelty, in the way of romance.
10021{_}511{_}Although the Mitchells were proud of these successes they were as free from snobbishness as almost anyone could be.
10021{_}512{_}On the whole Mrs Mitchell had a slight weakness for celebrities, while Mr Mitchell preferred pretty women, or people who romped.
10021{_}513{_}It was merely from carelessness that the Ottleys had never been asked before.
10021{_}514{_}When Edith and Bruce found themselves in the large square country-house-looking hall, with its oak beams and early English fireplace, about twenty people had arrived, and as many more were expected.
10021{_}515{_}A lively chatter had already begun; for each woman had been offered on her arrival a basket from which she had to choose a brightly coloured ribbon.
10021{_}516{_}These ribbons matched the rosettes presented in an equally haphazard way to every man.
10021{_}517{_}As Vincy observed, it gave one the rather ghastly impression that there was going to be a cotillion at once, on sight, before dinner; which was a little frightening.
10021{_}518{_}In reality it was merely so that the partners for the meal should be chosen by chance.
10021{_}519{_}Mitchell thought this more fun than arranging guests; but there was an element of gambling about it that made wary people nervous.
10021{_}520{_}Everyone present would have cheated had it been possible.
10021{_}521{_}But it was not.
10021{_}522{_}Mrs Mitchell was a tiny brown-eyed creature, who looked absurdly young; she was kind, sprightly, and rather like a grouse.
10021{_}523{_}Mitchell was a jovial-looking man, with a high forehead, almost too much ease of manner, and a twinkling eye.
10021{_}524{_}The chief guests tonight consisted of Lord Rye, a middle-aged suffraget, who was known for his habit of barking before he spoke and for his wonderful ear for music--he could play all Richard, Oscar and Johann Strauss's compositions by ear on the piano, and never mixed them up; Aylmer Ross, the handsome barrister; Myra Mooney, who had been on the stage; and an intelligent foreigner from the embassy, with a decoration, a goat-like beard, and an Armenian accent.
10021{_}525{_}Mrs Mitchell said he was the minister from some place with a name like Ruritania.
10021{_}526{_}She had a vague memory.
10021{_}527{_}There was also a Mr Cricker, a very young man of whom it was said that he could dance like Nijinsky, but never would; and the rest were chiefly Foreign Office clerks (like Mitchell and Bruce), more barristers and their wives, a soldier or two, some undergraduates, a lady photographer, a few pretty girls, and vague people.
10021{_}528{_}There were to be forty guests for dinner and a few more in the evening.
10021{_}529{_}Almost immediately on her arrival Edith noticed a tall, clean-shaven man, with smooth fair hair, observant blue eyes, and a rather humorous expression, and she instantly decided that she would try to will him to take her to dinner.
10021{_}530{_}(Rather a superfluous effort of magnetism, since it must have been settled already by fate and the ribbons.)
10021{_}531{_}It was obvious from one quick glance that he shared the wish.
10021{_}532{_}To their absurdly great mutual disappointment (a lot of ground was covered very quickly at the Mitchells), their ribbons didn't match, and she was taken to dinner by Captain Willis, who looked dull.
10021{_}533{_}Fortune, however, favoured her.
10021{_}534{_}On her other side she found the man who looked amusing.
10021{_}535{_}He was introduced to her across the table by Mrs Mitchell, with _empressement_, as Mr Aylmer Ross.
10021{_}536{_}Edith felt happy tonight; her spirits were raised by what she felt to be an atmosphere _tiede_, as the French say; full of indulgence, sympathetic, relaxing, in which either cleverness or stupidity could float equally at its ease.
10021{_}537{_}The puerility of the silly little arrangements to amuse removed all sense of ceremony.
10021{_}538{_}The note is always struck by the hostess, and she was everything that was amiable, without effort or affectation.
10021{_}539{_}No-one was ever afraid of her.
10021{_}540{_}Bruce's neighbour at dinner was the delicate, battered-looking actress, in a Royal fringe and a tight bodice with short sleeves, who had once been a celebrity, though no-one remembered for what.
10021{_}541{_}Miss Myra Mooney, formerly a beauty, had known her days of success.
10021{_}542{_}She had been the supreme performer of ladylike parts.
10021{_}543{_}She had been known as the very quintessence of refinement.
10021{_}544{_}It was assumed when she first came out that a duke would go to the devil for her in her youth, and that in her late maturity she would tour the provinces with _The Three Musketeers_.
10021{_}545{_}Neither of these prophecies had, however, been fulfilled.
10021{_}546{_}She still occasionally took small middle-aged titled parts in repertoire matinees.
10021{_}547{_}She was unable to help referring constantly to the hit she made in _Peril_ at Manchester in 1887; nor could she ever resist speaking of the young man who sent her red carnations every day of his blighted existence for fifteen years; a pure romance, indeed, for, as she owned, he never even wished to be introduced to her.
10021{_}548{_}She still called him poor boy, oblivious of the fact that he was now sixty-eight, and, according to the illustrated papers, spent his entire time in giving away a numberless succession of daughters in brilliant marriage at St George's, Hanover Square.
10021{_}549{_}In this way Miss Mooney lived a good deal in the past, but she was not unaware of the present, and was always particularly nice to people generally regarded as bores.
10021{_}550{_}So she was never without plenty of invitations.
10021{_}551{_}Mitchell had had formerly a slight _tendre_ for her, and in his good nature pretended to think she had not altered a bit.
10021{_}552{_}She was still refined _comme cela ne se fait plus_; it was practically no longer possible to find such a perfect lady, even on the stage.
10021{_}553{_}As she also had all the easy good nature of the artist, and made herself extremely agreeable, Bruce was delighted with her, and evidently thought he had drawn a prize.
10021{_}554{_}'I wondered,' Aylmer Ross said, 'whether this could possibly happen.
10021{_}555{_}First I half hoped it might; then I gave it up in despair.'
10021{_}556{_}'So did I,' said Edith; 'and yet I generally know.
10021{_}557{_}I've a touch of second sight, I think--at dinner-parties.'
10021{_}558{_}'Oh, well, I have second sight too--any amount; only it's always wrong.
10021{_}559{_}However!...'
10021{_}560{_}'Aren't the Mitchells dears?'
10021{_}561{_}said Edith.
10021{_}562{_}'Oh, quite.
10021{_}563{_}Do you know them well?'
10021{_}564{_}'Very well, indeed.
10021{_}565{_}But I've never seen them before.'
10021{_}566{_}'Ah, I see.
10021{_}567{_}Well, now we've found our way here--broken the ice and that sort of thing--we must often come and dine with them, mustn't we, Mrs Ottley?
10021{_}568{_}Can't we come again next week?'
10021{_}569{_}'Very sweet of you to ask us, I'm sure.'
10021{_}570{_}'Not at all; very jolly of us to turn up.
10021{_}571{_}The boot is on the other leg, or whatever the phrase is.
10021{_}572{_}By the way, I'm sure you know everything, Mrs Ottley, tell me, did people ever wear only one boot at a time, do you think, or how did this expression originate?'
10021{_}573{_}'I wonder.'
10021{_}574{_}Something in his suave manner of taking everything for granted seemed to make them know each other almost too quickly, and gave her an odd sort of self-consciousness.
10021{_}575{_}She turned to Captain Willis on her other side.
10021{_}576{_}'I say,' he said querulously, 'isn't this a bit off?
10021{_}577{_}We've got the same coloured ribbons and you haven't said a word to me yet!
10021{_}578{_}Rather rot, isn't it, what?'
10021{_}579{_}'Oh, haven't I?
10021{_}580{_}I will now.'
10021{_}581{_}Captain Willis lowered his voice to a confidential tone and said: 'Do you know, what I always say is--live and let live and let it go at that; what?'
10021{_}582{_}'That's a dark saying,' said Edith.
10021{_}583{_}'Have a burnt almond,' said Captain Willis inconsequently, as though it would help her to understand.
10021{_}584{_}'Yes, Mrs Ottley, that's what I always say....
10021{_}585{_}But people won't, you know--they won't--and there it is.'
10021{_}586{_}He seemed resigned.
10021{_}587{_}'Good chap, Mitchell, isn't he?
10021{_}588{_}Musical chairs, I believe--that's what we're to play this evening; or bridge, whichever we like.
10021{_}589{_}I shall go in for bridge.
10021{_}590{_}I'm not musical.'

10021{_}593{_}He had evidently been listening.

10021{_}595{_}'We'll talk then, shall we?

10021{_}597{_}I didn't notice when you came in.'
10021{_}598{_}'Over there, opposite; the left-hand corner.'
10021{_}599{_}'Good-looking chap with the light moustache--next to Myra Mooney?'
10021{_}600{_}'That's it,' she said.
10021{_}601{_}'He seems to be enjoying himself.
10021{_}602{_}I'm glad he's got Miss Mooney.
10021{_}603{_}He's lucky.'
10021{_}604{_}'He is indeed,' said Aylmer.
10021{_}605{_}'She's a wonderful-looking woman--like an old photograph, or someone in a book,' said Edith.
10021{_}606{_}'Do you care for books?'

10021{_}608{_}I've just been discovering Bourget.
10021{_}609{_}Fancy, I didn't know about him!
10021{_}610{_}I've just read _Mensonges_ for the first time.'
10021{_}611{_}'Oh yes.
10021{_}612{_}Rather a pompous chap, isn't he?
10021{_}613{_}But you could do worse than read _Mensonges_ for the first time.'
10021{_}614{_}'I _have_ done worse.
10021{_}615{_}I've been reading Rudyard Kipling for the last time.'
10021{_}616{_}'Really!
10021{_}617{_}Don't you like him?
10021{_}618{_}Why?'
10021{_}619{_}'I feel all the time, somehow, as if he were calling me by my Christian name without an introduction, or as if he wanted me to exchange hats with him,' she said.
10021{_}620{_}'He's so fearfully familiar with his readers.'
10021{_}621{_}'But you think he keeps at a respectful distance from his characters?
10021{_}622{_}However--why worry about books at all, Mrs Ottley?
10021{_}623{_}Flowers, lilies of the field, and so forth, don't toil or spin; why should they belong to libraries?
10021{_}624{_}I don't think you ever ought to read--except perhaps sometimes a little poetry, or romance.... You see, that is what you are, rather, isn't it?'
10021{_}625{_}'Don't you care for books?'
10021{_}626{_}she answered, ignoring the compliment.
10021{_}627{_}'I should have thought you loved them, and knew everything about them.
10021{_}628{_}I'm not sure that I know.'
10021{_}629{_}'You know quite enough, believe me,' he answered earnestly.
10021{_}630{_}'Oh, don't be cultured--don't talk about Lloyd George!
10021{_}631{_}Don't take an intelligent interest in the subjects of the day!'
10021{_}632{_}'All right; I'll try not.'
10021{_}633{_}She turned with a laugh to Captain Willis, who seemed very depressed.
10021{_}634{_}'I say, you know,' he said complainingly, 'this is all very well.
10021{_}635{_}It's all very well no doubt.
10021{_}636{_}But I only ask one thing--just one.
10021{_}637{_}Is this cricket?
10021{_}638{_}I merely ask, you know.
10021{_}639{_}Just that--is it cricket; what?'
10021{_}640{_}'It isn't meant to be.
10021{_}641{_}What's the matter?'
10021{_}642{_}'Why, I'm simply fed up and broken-hearted, you know.
10021{_}643{_}Hardly two words have I had with you tonight, Mrs Ottley....
10021{_}644{_}I suppose that chap's awfully amusing, what?
10021{_}645{_}I'm not amusing....
10021{_}646{_}I know that.'
10021{_}647{_}'Oh, don't say that.
10021{_}648{_}Indeed you are.'
10021{_}649{_}she consoled him.
10021{_}650{_}'Am I though?'
10021{_}651{_}'Well, you amuse _me_!'
10021{_}652{_}'Right!'
10021{_}653{_}He laughed cheerily.
10021{_}654{_}He always filled up pauses with a laugh.
10021{_}655{_}CHAPTER V  The Surprise  Certainly Mrs Mitchell on one side and Captain Willis on the other had suffered neglect.
10021{_}656{_}But they seemed to become hardened to it towards the end of dinner....  'I have a boy, too,' Aylmer remarked irrelevantly, 'rather a nice chap.
10021{_}657{_}Just ten.'
10021{_}658{_}Though only by the merest, slightest movement of an eyelash Edith could not avoid showing her surprise.
10021{_}659{_}No-one ever had less the air of a married man.
10021{_}660{_}Also, she was quite ridiculously disappointed.
10021{_}661{_}One can't say why, but one doesn't talk to a married man quite in the same way or so frankly as to a bachelor--if one is a married woman.
10021{_}662{_}She did not ask about his wife, but said:  'Fancy!
10021{_}663{_}Boys are rather nice things to have about, aren't they?'
10021{_}664{_}She was looking round the table, trying to divine which was Mrs Aylmer Ross.
10021{_}665{_}No, she wasn't there.
10021{_}666{_}Edith felt sure of it.
10021{_}667{_}It was an unaccountable satisfaction.
10021{_}668{_}'Yes; he's all right.
10021{_}669{_}And now give me a detailed description of _your_ children.'
10021{_}670{_}'I can't.
10021{_}671{_}I never could talk about them.'
10021{_}672{_}'I see....
10021{_}673{_}I should like to see them....
10021{_}674{_}I saw you speak to Vincy.
10021{_}675{_}Dear little fellow, isn't he?'
10021{_}676{_}'He's a great friend of mine.'
10021{_}677{_}'I'm tremendously devoted to him, too.
10021{_}678{_}He's what used to be called an exquisite.
10021{_}679{_}And he _is_ exquisite; he has an exquisite mind.
10021{_}680{_}But, of course, you know what a good sort he is.'
10021{_}681{_}'Rather.'
10021{_}682{_}'He seems rather to look at life than to act in it, doesn't he?'
10021{_}683{_}continued Aylmer.
10021{_}684{_}'He's a brilliant sort of spectator.
10021{_}685{_}Vincy thinks that all the world's a stage, but _he's_ always in the front row of the stalls.
10021{_}686{_}I never could be like that ...
10021{_}687{_}I always want to be right in the thick of it, on in every scene, and always performing!'
10021{_}688{_}'To an audience?'
10021{_}689{_}said Edith.
10021{_}690{_}He smiled and went on.
10021{_}691{_}'What's so jolly about him is that though he's so quiet, yet he's genial; not chilly and reserved.
10021{_}692{_}He's frank, I mean--and confiding.
10021{_}693{_}Without ever saying much.
10021{_}694{_}He expresses himself in his own way.'
10021{_}695{_}'That's quite true.'
10021{_}696{_}'And, after all, it's really only expression that makes things real.
10021{_}697{_}'If you don't talk about a thing, it has never happened.''
10021{_}698{_}'But it doesn't always follow that a thing has happened because you do talk about it,' said Edith.
10021{_}699{_}'Ah, Mrs Mitchell's going !'
10021{_}700{_}She floated away.
10021{_}701{_}He remained in a rather ecstatic state of absence of mind.
10021{_}702{_}*       *       *       *       *  Mrs Mitchell gladly told Edith all about Aylmer Ross, how clever he was, how nice, how devoted to his little boy.
10021{_}703{_}He had married very young, it seemed, and had lost his wife two years after.
10021{_}704{_}This was ten years ago, and according to Mrs Mitchell he had never looked at another woman since.
10021{_}705{_}Women love to simplify in this sentimental way.
10021{_}706{_}'However,' she said consolingly, 'he's still quite young, under forty, and he's sure to fall in love and marry again.'
10021{_}707{_}'No doubt,' said Edith, wishing the first wife had remained alive.
10021{_}708{_}She disliked the non-existent second one.
10021{_}709{_}*       *       *       *       *  Nearly all the men had now joined the ladies in the studio, with the exception of Bruce and of Aylmer Ross.
10021{_}710{_}Mrs Mitchell had taken an immense fancy to Edith and showed it by telling her all about a wonderful little tailor who made coats and skirts better than Lucile for next to nothing, and by introducing to her Lord Rye and the embassy man, and Mr Cricker.
10021{_}711{_}Edith was sitting in a becoming corner under a shaded light from which she could watch the door, when Vincy came up to talk to her.
10021{_}712{_}'You seemed to get on rather well at dinner,' he said.
10021{_}713{_}'Yes; isn't Captain Willis a dear?'
10021{_}714{_}'Oh, simply sweet.
10021{_}715{_}So bright and clever.
10021{_}716{_}I was sure you'd like him, Edith.'
10021{_}717{_}Captain Willis here came up and said, a shade more jovially than he had spoken at dinner, with his laugh:  'Well, you know, Mrs Ottley, what I always say is--live and let live and let it go at that; what?
10021{_}718{_}But they never _do_, you know!
10021{_}719{_}They won't--and there it is!'
10021{_}720{_}Edith now did a thing she had never done in her life before and which was entirely unlike her.
10021{_}721{_}She tried her utmost to retain the group round her, and to hold their attention.
10021{_}722{_}For a reason of which she was hardly conscious, she wanted Aylmer Ross to see her surrounded.
10021{_}723{_}The minister from the place with a name like Ruritania was so immensely bowled over that he was already murmuring in a low voice (almost a hiss, as they say in melodrama): 'Vous etes chez vous, quand?
10021{_}724{_}Dites un mot, un mot seulement, et je me precipiterai a vos pieds_,' while at the same time, in her other ear, Lord Rye was explaining (to her pretended intense interest) how he could play the whole of _Elektra, The Chocolate Soldier_ and _Nightbirds_ by ear without a single mistake.
10021{_}725{_}('Perfectly sound!'
10021{_}726{_}grumbled Captain Willis, 'but why do it?')
10021{_}727{_}Vincy was listening, enjoying himself.
10021{_}728{_}Bruce came in at last, evidently engaged in an absorbed and intimate conversation with Aylmer Ross.
10021{_}729{_}They seemed so much interested in their talk that they went to the other end of the room and sat down there together.
10021{_}730{_}Aylmer gave her one glance only.
10021{_}731{_}Edith was unreasonably annoyed.
10021{_}732{_}What on earth could he and Bruce find to talk about?
10021{_}733{_}At length, growing tired of her position, she got up, and walked across the room to look at a picture on the wall, turning her graceful back to the room.
10021{_}734{_}Bruce had now at last left his companion, but still Aylmer Ross did not go and speak to her, though he was sitting alone.
10021{_}735{_}Musical chairs began in the studio.
10021{_}736{_}Someone was playing 'Baby, look-a-here,' stopping suddenly in the middle to shouts of laughter and shrieks from the romping players.
10021{_}737{_}In the drawing-room some of the people were playing bridge.
10021{_}738{_}How dull the rest of the evening was!
10021{_}739{_}Just before the party practically broke up, Edith had an opportunity of saying as she passed Aylmer:  'I thought we were going to have a talk instead of playing games?'
10021{_}740{_}'I saw you were occupied,' he answered ceremoniously.
10021{_}741{_}'I didn't like--to interrupt.'
10021{_}742{_}She laughed.
10021{_}743{_}'Is this a jealous scene, Mr Ross?'
10021{_}744{_}'I wonder,' he said, smiling, 'and if so, whose.
10021{_}745{_}Well, I hope to see you again soon.'
10021{_}746{_}'_What_ a success your charming wife has had tonight,' said Mrs Mitchell to Bruce, as they took leave.
10021{_}747{_}'Everyone is quite wild about her.
10021{_}748{_}How pretty she is!
10021{_}749{_}You _must_ be proud of her.'
10021{_}750{_}They were nearly the last.
10021{_}751{_}Mr Cricker, who had firmly refused the whole evening, in spite of abject entreaties, to dance like Nijinsky, suddenly relented when everyone had forgotten all about it, and was leaping alone in the studio, while Lord Rye, always a great lingerer, was playing Richard Strauss to himself on the baby Grand, and smoking a huge cigar.
10021{_}752{_}'Edith,' said Bruce solemnly, as they drove away, 'I've made a friend tonight.
10021{_}753{_}There was one really charming man there--he took an immense fancy to me.'
10021{_}754{_}'Oh--who was that?'
10021{_}755{_}'Who was that?'

10021{_}758{_}Why, Aylmer Ross, the chap who sat next to you at dinner!
10021{_}759{_}I suppose you didn't appreciate him.
10021{_}760{_}Very clever, very interesting.
10021{_}761{_}He was anxious to know several things which I was glad to be in a position to tell him.
10021{_}762{_}Yes--an awfully good sort.

10021{_}768{_}You can come too, dear.

10021{_}772{_}Once in a way!

10021{_}784{_}She was attracted and did not know why, and for the first time hoped she had made an impression.
10021{_}785{_}It had been a long evening, and her headache, she said, necessitated solitude and darkness at once.
10021{_}786{_}'All right.

10021{_}788{_}Don't be anxious, dear!

10021{_}794{_}'Why, of course, it's only courteous.
10021{_}795{_}It's all right.
10021{_}796{_}One must return their hospitality.'
10021{_}797{_}CHAPTER VI  The Visit  The following afternoon Edith was standing by the piano in her condensed white drawing-room, trying over a song, which she was accompanying with one hand, when to her surprise the maid announced 'Mr Aylmer Ross.'
10021{_}798{_}It was a warm day, and though there was a fire the windows were open, letting in the scent of the mauve and pink hyacinths in the little window-boxes.
10021{_}799{_}She thought as she came forward to meet him that he seemed entirely different from last night.
10021{_}800{_}Her first impression was that he was too big for the room, her second that he was very handsome, and also a little agitated.
10021{_}801{_}'I really hardly know how to apologise, Mrs Ottley.
10021{_}802{_}I oughtn't to have turned up in this cool way.
10021{_}803{_}But your husband has kindly asked me to dine with you tonight, and I wasn't sure of the time.
10021{_}804{_}I thought I'd come and ask you.'
10021{_}805{_}He waited a minute.
10021{_}806{_}'Of course, if I hadn't been so fortunate as to find you in, I should just have left a note.'
10021{_}807{_}He looked round the room.
10021{_}808{_}*       *       *       *       *  Obviously it was quite unnecessary for him to have called; he could have sent the note that he had brought with him.
10021{_}809{_}She was flattered.
10021{_}810{_}She thought that she liked his voice and the flash of his white teeth when he smiled.
10021{_}811{_}'Oh, I'm glad I'm at home,' she said, in a gentle way that put him at his ease, and yet at an immense distance.
10021{_}812{_}'I felt in the mood to stop at home and play the piano today.
10021{_}813{_}I'm delighted to see you.'
10021{_}814{_}They sat down by the fire.
10021{_}815{_}'It's at eight tonight.
10021{_}816{_}Shall we have tea?'
10021{_}817{_}'Oh no, thanks; isn't it too early?
10021{_}818{_}I sha'n't keep you a moment.
10021{_}819{_}Thanks very much.... You were playing something when I came in.
10021{_}820{_}I wish you'd play it to me over again.'
10021{_}821{_}*       *       *       *       *  Nine women out of ten would have refused, saying they knew nothing of music, or that they were out of practice, or that they never played except for their own amusement, or something of the kind; especially if they took no pride whatever in that accomplishment.
10021{_}822{_}But Edith went back to the piano at once, and went on trying over the song that she didn't know, without making any excuse for the faltering notes.
10021{_}823{_}'That's charming,' he said.
10021{_}824{_}'Thanks.
10021{_}825{_}Tosti, of course.'
10021{_}826{_}She came back to the fireplace.
10021{_}827{_}'Of course.
10021{_}828{_}We had great fun last night, didn't we?'
10021{_}829{_}'Oh, _I_ enjoyed myself immensely; part of the time at least.'
10021{_}830{_}'But after dinner you were rather horrid, Mr Ross.
10021{_}831{_}You wouldn't come and talk to me, would you?'
10021{_}832{_}'Wouldn't I?
10021{_}833{_}I was afraid.
10021{_}834{_}Tell me, do I seem many years older since last night?'
10021{_}835{_}he asked.
10021{_}836{_}'I don't see any difference.
10021{_}837{_}Why?'
10021{_}838{_}'Because I've lived months--almost years--since I saw you last.
10021{_}839{_}Time doesn't go by hours, does it?...
10021{_}840{_}What a charming little room this is.
10021{_}841{_}It suits you.
10021{_}842{_}There's hardly anything in it, but everything is right.'
10021{_}843{_}'I don't like to have many things in a room,' said Edith, holding out her delicate hands to the fire.
10021{_}844{_}'It makes me nervous.
10021{_}845{_}I have gradually accustomed Bruce to my idea by removing one thing at a time --photographs, pictures, horrid old wedding presents, all the little things people have.
10021{_}846{_}They suggest too many different trains of thought.
10021{_}847{_}They worry me.
10021{_}848{_}He's getting used to it now.
10021{_}849{_}He says, soon there'll be nothing left but a couple of chairs and a bookcase!'
10021{_}850{_}'And how right!
10021{_}851{_}I've had rather the same idea in my house, but I couldn't keep it up.
10021{_}852{_}It's different for a man alone; things seem to accumulate; especially pictures.
10021{_}853{_}I know such a lot of artists.
10021{_}854{_}I'm very unfortunate in that respect....
10021{_}855{_}I really feel I oughtn't to have turned up like this, Mrs Ottley.'
10021{_}856{_}'Why not?'
10021{_}857{_}'You're very kind....
10021{_}858{_}Excuse my country manners, but how nice your husband is.
10021{_}859{_}He was very kind to me.'
10021{_}860{_}'He liked _you_ very much, too.'

10021{_}862{_}'Oh no.
10021{_}863{_}Never.
10021{_}864{_}He couldn't.
10021{_}865{_}He amuses me,' Edith replied softly.
10021{_}866{_}'Oh, does he?...
10021{_}867{_}If I had the opportunity I wonder if I should _amuse_ you,' he spoke thoughtfully.
10021{_}868{_}'No; I don't think you would at all,' said Edith, looking him straight in the face.
10021{_}869{_}'That's quite fair,' he laughed, and seemed rather pleased.
10021{_}870{_}'You mean I should bore you to death!
10021{_}871{_}Do forgive me, Mrs Ottley.
10021{_}872{_}Let's go on with our talk of last night....
10021{_}873{_}I feel it's rather like the Palace of Truth here; I don't know why.

10021{_}879{_}He was going to ask one or two other kindred spirits--as I think they're called.
10021{_}880{_}To see something--some collection.

10021{_}886{_}'At eight.'
10021{_}887{_}'Yes.'

10021{_}894{_}'It was very jolly.
10021{_}895{_}I liked it.
