10021{_}897{_}'Oh no!
10021{_}898{_}It was pink!'
10021{_}899{_}she answered.
10021{_}900{_}Then there was an extraordinary pause, in which neither of them seemed able to think of anything to say.
10021{_}901{_}There was a curious sort of vibration in the air.
10021{_}902{_}'Isn't it getting quite springy?'
10021{_}903{_}said Edith, as she glanced at the window.
10021{_}904{_}'It's one of those sort of warm days that seem to have got mixed up by mistake with the winter.'
10021{_}905{_}'Very,' was his reply, which was not very relevant.
10021{_}906{_}Another pause was beginning.
10021{_}907{_}'Mr Vincy,' announced the servant.
10021{_}908{_}He was received with enthusiasm, and Aylmer Ross now recovered his ease and soon went away.
10021{_}909{_}'Edith!'
10021{_}910{_}said Vincy, in a reproving tone.
10021{_}911{_}'_Really_!
10021{_}912{_}How _very_ soon!'
10021{_}913{_}'He came to know what time we dine.
10021{_}914{_}He was just passing.'
10021{_}915{_}'Oh, yes.
10021{_}916{_}He would want to know.
10021{_}917{_}He lives in Jermyn Street.
10021{_}918{_}I suppose Knightsbridge is on his way to there.'
10021{_}919{_}'From where?'
10021{_}920{_}she asked.
10021{_}921{_}'From here,' said Vincy.
10021{_}922{_}'What happened after we left?'
10021{_}923{_}said Edith.
10021{_}924{_}'I saw the Cricker man beginning to dance with hardly anyone looking at him.'
10021{_}925{_}'Isn't his imitation of Nijinsky wonderful?'
10021{_}926{_}asked Vincy.
10021{_}927{_}'Simply marvellous!
10021{_}928{_}I thought he was imitating George Grossmith.
10021{_}929{_}Do you know, I love the Mitchells, Vincy.
10021{_}930{_}It's really great fun there.
10021{_}931{_}Fancy, Bruce seems so delighted with Aylmer Ross and Miss Mooney that he insisted on their both dining with us tonight.'
10021{_}932{_}'He seemed rather carried away, I thought.
10021{_}933{_}There's a fascination about Aylmer.
10021{_}934{_}There are so many things he's not,' said Vincy.
10021{_}935{_}'Tell me some of them.'
10021{_}936{_}'Well, for one thing, he's not fatuous, though he's so good-looking.
10021{_}937{_}He's not a lady-killing sort of person or anything else tedious.'
10021{_}938{_}She was delighted at this especially.
10021{_}939{_}'If he took a fancy to a person--well, it might be rather serious, if you take my meaning,' said Vincy.
10021{_}940{_}'How sweet of him!
10021{_}941{_}So unusual.
10021{_}942{_}Do you like Myra Mooney?'
10021{_}943{_}'Me?
10021{_}944{_}Oh, rather; I'm devoted to her.
10021{_}945{_}She's a delightful type.
10021{_}946{_}Get her on to the subject of the red carnations.
10021{_}947{_}She's splendid about them.... She received them every day at breakfast-time for fifteen years.
10021{_}948{_}Another jolly thing about Aylmer is that he has none of that awful old-fashioned modernness, thank goodness!'
10021{_}949{_}'Ah, I noticed that.'
10021{_}950{_}'I suppose he wasn't brilliant today.
10021{_}951{_}He was too thrilled.
10021{_}952{_}But, do be just a teeny bit careful, Edith dear, because when he is at all he's very much so.
10021{_}953{_}Do you see?'
10021{_}954{_}'What a lot you seem to think of one little visit, Vincy!
10021{_}955{_}After all, it was only one.'
10021{_}956{_}'There hasn't been time yet for many more, has there, Edith dear?
10021{_}957{_}He could hardly call twice the same day, on the first day, too....
10021{_}958{_}Yes, I come over quite queer and you might have knocked me down with a feather, in a manner of speaking, when I clapped eyes on him setting here.'
10021{_}959{_}Edith liked Vincy to talk in his favourite Cockney strain.
10021{_}960{_}It contrasted pleasantly with his soft, even voice and _raffine_ appearance.
10021{_}961{_}'Here's Bruce,' she said.
10021{_}962{_}Bruce came in carrying an enormous basket of gilded straw.
10021{_}963{_}It was filled with white heather, violets, lilies, jonquils, gardenias and mimosa.
10021{_}964{_}The handle was trimmed with mauve ribbon.
10021{_}965{_}'Oh, Bruce!
10021{_}966{_}How angelic of you!'
10021{_}967{_}'Don't be in such a hurry, dear.
10021{_}968{_}These are not from me.
10021{_}969{_}They arrived just at the same time that I did.
10021{_}970{_}Brought by a commissionaire.
10021{_}971{_}There was hardly room for it in the lift.'
10021{_}972{_}Edith looked quickly at the card.
10021{_}973{_}It bore the name of the minister of the place with a name like Ruritania.
10021{_}974{_}'What cheek!'
10021{_}975{_}exclaimed Bruce, who was really flattered.
10021{_}976{_}'What infernal impertinence.
10021{_}977{_}Upon my word I've more than half a mind to go and tell him what I think of him--straight from the shoulder.
10021{_}978{_}What's the address?'
10021{_}979{_}'Grosvenor Square.'
10021{_}980{_}'Well, I don't care.
10021{_}981{_}I shall go straight to the embassy,' said Bruce.
10021{_}982{_}'No, I sha'n't.
10021{_}983{_}I'll send them back and write him a line--tell him that Englishwomen are not in the habit of accepting presents from undesirable aliens....
10021{_}984{_}I consider it a great liberty.
10021{_}985{_}Aren't I right, Vincy?'
10021{_}986{_}'Quite.
10021{_}987{_}But perhaps he means no harm, Bruce.
10021{_}988{_}I daresay it's the custom in the place with the funny name.
10021{_}989{_}You see, you never know, in a place like that.'
10021{_}990{_}'Then you don't think I ought to take it up?'
10021{_}991{_}'I don't want them.
10021{_}992{_}It's a very oppressive basket,' Edith said.
10021{_}993{_}'How like you, Edith!
10021{_}994{_}I thought you were fond of flowers.'
10021{_}995{_}'So I am, but I like one at a time.
10021{_}996{_}This is too miscellaneous and crowded.'
10021{_}997{_}'Some women are never satisfied.
10021{_}998{_}It's very rude and ungrateful to the poor old man, who meant to be nice, no doubt, and to show his respect for Englishwomen.
10021{_}999{_}I think you ought to write and thank him,' said Bruce.
10021{_}1000{_}'And let me see the letter before it goes.'
10021{_}1001{_}CHAPTER VII  Coup de Foudre  When Aylmer Ross got back to the little brown house in Jermyn Street he went to his library, and took from a certain drawer an ivory miniature framed in black.
10021{_}1002{_}He looked at it for some time.
10021{_}1003{_}It had a sweet, old-fashioned face, with a very high forehead, blue eyes, and dark hair arranged in two festoons of plaits, turned up at the sides.
10021{_}1004{_}It represented his mother in the early sixties and he thought it was like Edith.
10021{_}1005{_}He had a great devotion and cult for the memory of his mother.
10021{_}1006{_}When he was charmed with a woman he always imagined her to be like his mother.
10021{_}1007{_}He had never thought this about his wife People had said how extraordinarily Aylmer must have been in love to have married that uninteresting girl, no-one in particular, not pretty and a little second-rate.
10021{_}1008{_}As a matter of fact the marriage had happened entirely by accident.
10021{_}1009{_}It had occurred through a misunderstanding during a game of consequences in a country house.
10021{_}1010{_}She was terribly literal.
10021{_}1011{_}Having taken some joke of his seriously, she had sent him a touchingly coy letter saying she was overwhelmed at his offer (feeling she was hardly worthy to be his wife) and must think it over.
10021{_}1012{_}He did not like to hurt her feelings by explaining, and when she relented and accepted him he couldn't bear to tell her the truth.
10021{_}1013{_}He was absurdly tender-hearted, and he thought that, after all, it didn't matter so very much.
10021{_}1014{_}The little house left him by his mother needed a mistress; he would probably marry somebody or other, anyhow; and she seemed such a harmless little thing.
10021{_}1015{_}It would please her so much!
10021{_}1016{_}When the hurried marriage had come to a pathetic end by her early death everyone was tragic about it except Aylmer.
10021{_}1017{_}All his friends declared he was heart-broken and lonely and would never marry again.
10021{_}1018{_}He had indeed been shocked and grieved at her death, but only for her--not at being left alone.
10021{_}1019{_}That part, was a relief.
10021{_}1020{_}The poor little late Mrs Aylmer Ross had turned out a terrible mistake.
10021{_}1021{_}She had said the wrong thing from morning till night, and, combining a prim, refined manner with a vulgar point of view, had been in every way dreadfully impossible.
10021{_}1022{_}He had really been patience and unselfishness itself to her, but he had suffered.
10021{_}1023{_}The fact was, he had never even liked her.
10021{_}1024{_}That was the reason he had not married again.
10021{_}1025{_}But he was devoted to his boy in a quiet way.
10021{_}1026{_}He was the sort of man who is adored by children, animals, servants and women.
10021{_}1027{_}Tall, strong and handsome, with intelligence beyond the average, yet with nothing alarming about him, good-humoured about trifles, jealous in matters of love--perhaps that is, after all, the type women really like best.
10021{_}1028{_}It is sheer nonsense to say that women enjoy being tyrannised over.
10021{_}1029{_}No doubt there are some who would rather be bullied than ignored.
10021{_}1030{_}But the hectoring man is, with few exceptions, secretly detested.
10021{_}1031{_}In so far as one can generalise (always a dangerous thing to do) it may be said that women like best a kind, clever man who can be always trusted; and occasionally (if necessary) deceived.
10021{_}1032{_}Aylmer hardly ever got angry except in an argument about ideas.
10021{_}1033{_}Yet his feelings were violent; he was impulsive, and under his suave and easy-going manner emotional.
10021{_}1034{_}He was certainly good-looking, but had he not been he would have pleased all the same.
10021{_}1035{_}He seemed to radiate warmth, life, a certain careless good-humour.
10021{_}1036{_}To be near him was like warming one's hands at a warm fire.
10021{_}1037{_}Superficially susceptible and inclined to be experimental he had not the instinct of the collector and was devoid of fatuousness.
10021{_}1038{_}But he could have had more genuine successes than all the Don Juans and Romeos and Fausts who ever climbed rope ladders.
10021{_}1039{_}Besides his physical attraction he inspired a feeling of reliance.
10021{_}1040{_}Women felt safe with him; he would never treat anyone badly.
10021{_}1041{_}He inspired that kind of trust enormously in men also, and his house was constantly filled with people asking his advice and begging him to do things--sometimes not very easy ones.
10021{_}1042{_}He was always being left guardian to young persons who would never require one, and said himself he had become almost a professional trustee.
10021{_}1043{_}As Aylmer was generous and very extravagant in a way of his own (though he cared nothing for show), he really worked hard at the bar to add to his already large income.
10021{_}1044{_}He always wanted a great deal of money.
10021{_}1045{_}He required ease, margin and elbow-room.
10021{_}1046{_}He had no special hobbies, but he needed luxury in general of a kind, and especially the luxury of getting things in a hurry, his theory being that everything comes to the man who won't wait.
10021{_}1047{_}He was not above detesting little material hardships.
10021{_}1048{_}He was not the sort of man, for instance, even in his youngest days, who would go by omnibus to the gallery to the opera, to hear a favourite singer or a special performance; not that he had the faintest tinge of snobbishness, but simply because such trifling drawbacks irritated him, and spoilt his pleasure.
10021{_}1049{_}Impressionistic as he was in life, on the other hand, curiously, Aylmer's real taste in art and decoration was Pre-Raphaelite; delicate, detailed and meticulous almost to preciousness.
10021{_}1050{_}He often had delightful things in his house, but never for long.
10021{_}1051{_}He had no pleasure in property; valuable possessions worried him, and after any amount of trouble to get some object of art he would often give it away the next week.
10021{_}1052{_}For he really liked money only for freedom and ease.
10021{_}1053{_}The general look of the house was, consequently, distinguished, sincere and extremely comfortable.
10021{_}1054{_}It was neither hackneyed nor bizarre, and, while it contained some interesting things, had no superfluities.
10021{_}1055{_}Aylmer had been spoilt as a boy and was still wilful and a little impatient.
10021{_}1056{_}For instance he could never wait even for a boy-messenger, but always sent his notes by taxi to wait for an answer.
10021{_}1057{_}And now he wanted something in a hurry, and was very much afraid he would never get it.
10021{_}1058{_}Aylmer was, as I have said, often a little susceptible.
10021{_}1059{_}This time he felt completely bowled over.
10021{_}1060{_}He had only seen her twice.
10021{_}1061{_}That made no difference.
10021{_}1062{_}The truth was--it sounds romantic, but is really scientific, all romance being, perhaps, based on science--that Edith's appearance corresponded in every particular with an ideal that had grown up with him.
10021{_}1063{_}Whether he had seen some picture as a child that had left a vague and lasting impression, or whatever the reason was, the moment he saw her he felt, with a curious mental sensation, as of something that fell into its place with a click ('Ca y est!
10021{_}1064{_}'), that she realised some half-forgotten dream.
10021{_}1065{_}In fact, it was a rare and genuine case of _coup de foudre_.
10021{_}1066{_}Had she been a girl he would have proposed to her the next day, and they might quite possibly have married in a month, and lived happily ever after.
10021{_}1067{_}These things occasionally happen.
10021{_}1068{_}But she was married already.
10021{_}1069{_}Had she been a fool, or a bore, a silly little idiot or a fisher of men, a social sham who prattled of duchesses or a strenuous feminine politician who babbled of votes; a Christian Scientist bent on converting, an adventuress without adventures (the worst kind), a mind-healer or a body-snatcher, a hockey-player or even a lady novelist, it would have been exactly the same; whatever she had been, mentally or morally, he would undoubtedly have fallen in love with her physically, at first sight.
10021{_}1070{_}But it was very much worse than that.
10021{_}1071{_}He found her delightful, and clever; he was certain she was an angel.
10021{_}1072{_}She was married to Ottley.
10021{_}1073{_}Ottley was all right.... Rather an ass ... rather ridiculous; apparently in every way but one.
10021{_}1074{_}*       *       *       *       *  So absurdly hard hit was Aylmer that it seemed to him as if to see her again as soon as possible was already the sole object in his life.
10021{_}1075{_}Did she like him?
10021{_}1076{_}Intuitively he felt that during his little visit his intense feeling had radiated, and not displeased--perhaps a little impressed--her.
10021{_}1077{_}He could easily, he knew, form a friendship with them; arrange to see her often.
10021{_}1078{_}He was going to meet her tonight, through his own arrangement.
10021{_}1079{_}He would get them to come and dine with him soon--no, the next day.
10021{_}1080{_}What was the good?
10021{_}1081{_}Well, where was the harm?
10021{_}1082{_}Aylmer had about the same code of morals as the best of his numerous friends in Bohemia, in clubland and in social London.
10021{_}1083{_}He was no more scrupulous on most subjects than the ordinary man of his own class.
10021{_}1084{_}Still, _he had been married himself_.
10021{_}1085{_}That made an immense difference, for he was positively capable of seeing (and with sympathy) from the husband's point of view.
10021{_}1086{_}Even now, indifferent as he had been to his own wife, and after ten years, it would have caused him pain and fury had he found out that she had ever tried to play him false.
10021{_}1087{_}Of course, cases varied.
10021{_}1088{_}He knew that if Edith had been free his one thought would have been to marry her.
10021{_}1089{_}Had she been different, and differently placed, he would have blindly tried for anything he could get, in any possible way.
10021{_}1090{_}But, as she was?...
10021{_}1091{_}He felt convinced he could never succeed in making her care for him; there was not the slightest chance of it.
10021{_}1092{_}And, supposing even that he could?
10021{_}1093{_}And here came in the delicacy and scruple of the man who had been married himself.
10021{_}1094{_}He thought he wouldn't even wish to spoil, by the vulgarity of compromising, or by the shadow of a secret, the serenity of her face, the gay prettiness of that life.
10021{_}1095{_}No, he wouldn't if he could.
10021{_}1096{_}And yet how exciting it would be to rouse her from that cool composure.
10021{_}1097{_}She was rather enigmatic.
10021{_}1098{_}But he thought she could be roused.
10021{_}1099{_}And she was so clever.
10021{_}1100{_}How well she would carry it off!
10021{_}1101{_}How she would never bore a man!
10021{_}1102{_}And he suddenly imagined a day with her in the country.... Then he thought that his imagination was flying on far too fast.
10021{_}1103{_}He decided not to be a hopeless fool, but just to go ahead, and talk to her, and get to know her; not to think too much about her.
10021{_}1104{_}She needn't even know how he felt.
10021{_}1105{_}To idolise her from a distance would be quite delightful enough.
10021{_}1106{_}When a passion is not realised, he thought, it fades away, or becomes ideal worship --Dante--Petrarch--that sort of thing!
10021{_}1107{_}It could never fade away in this case, he was sure.
10021{_}1108{_}How pretty she was, how lovely her mouth was when she smiled!
10021{_}1109{_}She had no prejudices, apparently; no affectations; how she played and sang that song again when he asked her!
10021{_}1110{_}With what a delightful sense of humour she had dealt with him, and also with Bruce, at the Mitchells.
10021{_}1111{_}Ottley must be a little difficult sometimes.
10021{_}1112{_}She had read and thought; she had the same tastes as he.
10021{_}1113{_}He wondered if she would have liked that thing in _The Academy_, on Gardens, that he had just read.
10021{_}1114{_}He began looking for it.
10021{_}1115{_}He thought he would send it to her, asking her opinion; then he would get an answer, and see her handwriting.
10021{_}1116{_}You don't know a woman until you have had a letter from her.
10021{_}1117{_}But no--what a fool he would look!
10021{_}1118{_}Besides he was going to see her tonight.
10021{_}1119{_}It was about time to get ready.... Knowing subconsciously that he had made some slight favourable impression--at any rate that he hadn't repelled or bored her--he dressed with all the anxiety, joy and thrills of excitement of a boy of twenty; and no boy of twenty can ever feel these things as keenly or half as elaborately as a man nearly twice that age, since all the added experiences, disillusions, practice, knowledge and life of the additional years help to form a part of the same emotion, making it infinitely deeper, and all the stronger because so much more _averti_ and conscious of itself.
10021{_}1120{_}He seemed so nervous while dressing that Soames, the valet, to whom he was a hero, ventured respectfully to hope there was nothing wrong.
10021{_}1121{_}'No.
10021{_}1122{_}I'm all right,' said Aylmer.
10021{_}1123{_}'I'm never ill.
10021{_}1124{_}I think, Soames, I shall probably die of middle age.'
10021{_}1125{_}He went out laughing, leaving the valet smiling coldly out of politeness.
10021{_}1126{_}*       *       *       *       *  Soames never understood any kind of jest.
10021{_}1127{_}He took himself and everyone else seriously.
10021{_}1128{_}But he already knew perfectly well that his master had fallen in love last night, and he disapproved very strongly.
10021{_}1129{_}He thought all that sort of thing ought to be put a stop to.
10021{_}1130{_}CHAPTER VIII  Archie's Essay  'Mrs Ottley,' said Miss Townsend,' do you mind looking at this essay of Archie's?
10021{_}1131{_}I really don't know what to think of it.
10021{_}1132{_}I think it shows talent, except the spelling.
10021{_}1133{_}But it's _very_ naughty of him to have written what is at the end.'
10021{_}1134{_}Edith took the paper and read:  'TRAYS OF CHARACTER  trays of character will always show threw how ever much you may polish it up trays of character will always show threw the grane of the wood.
10021{_}1135{_}A burd will keep on singing because he wants to and they can't help doing what it wants this is instinkt.
10021{_}1136{_}and it is the same with trays of charicter.
10021{_}1137{_}having thus shown my theory that trays of carocter will always show threw in spite of all trubble and in any circemstances whatever I will conclude Archibald Bruce Ottley please t.o.'
10021{_}1138{_}On the other side of the paper was written very neatly, still in Archie's writing:  '3 LINDEN MANSIONS, CADOGAN SQUARE, KNIGHTSBRIDGE.
10021{_}1139{_}_Second Floor_  1.
10021{_}1140{_}Mr Bruce Ottley (FO) 2.
10021{_}1141{_}Mrs Bruce Ottley 3.
10021{_}1142{_}Master Archibald Bruce Ottley 4.
10021{_}1143{_}Little _beast_ 5.
10021{_}1144{_}Mary Johnson housemaid 6.
10021{_}1145{_}Miss Thrupp Cook 7.
10021{_}1146{_}Marie maid  8.
10021{_}1147{_}Dorothy Margaret Miss Townsend governess 9.
10021{_}1148{_}Ellen Maud Parrot nurse.'
10021{_}1149{_}'Do you see?'
10021{_}1150{_}said Miss Townsend.
10021{_}1151{_}'It's his way of slyly calling poor Dilly a beast, because he's angry with her.
10021{_}1152{_}Isn't it a shame?
10021{_}1153{_}What shall I do?'
10021{_}1154{_}Both of them laughed and enjoyed it.
10021{_}1155{_}'Archie, what is the meaning of this?
10021{_}1156{_}Why did you make this census of your home?'
10021{_}1157{_}Edith asked him gently.
10021{_}1158{_}'Why, I didn't make senses of my home; I just wrote down who lived here.'
10021{_}1159{_}Edith looked at him reproachfully.
10021{_}1160{_}'Well, I didn't call Dilly a beast.
10021{_}1161{_}I haven't broken Miss Townsend's rules.
10021{_}1162{_}She made a new rule I wasn't to call her a beast before breakfast--'  'What, you're allowed to call her these awful names after breakfast?'
10021{_}1163{_}'No.
10021{_}1164{_}She made a rule before breakfast I wasn't to call Dilly a beast, and I haven't.
10021{_}1165{_}How did you know it meant her anyway?
10021{_}1166{_}It might have meant somebody else.'
10021{_}1167{_}'That's prevaricating; it's mean--not like you, Archie.'
10021{_}1168{_}'Well, I never called her a beast.
10021{_}1169{_}No-one can say I did.
10021{_}1170{_}And besides, anybody would have called her a beast after how she went on.'
10021{_}1171{_}'What are you angry with the child for?'
10021{_}1172{_}'Oh, she bothers so.
10021{_}1173{_}The moment I imitate the man with the German accent she begins to cry.
10021{_}1174{_}She says she doesn't like me to do it.
10021{_}1175{_}She says she can't bear me to.
10021{_}1176{_}Then she goes and tells Miss Townsend I slapped her, and Miss Townsend blames me.'
10021{_}1177{_}'Then you shouldn't have slapped her; it was horrid of you; you ought to remember she's a little girl and weaker than you.'
10021{_}1178{_}'I did remember...'  'Oh, Archie!'
10021{_}1179{_}'Well, I'll make it up if she begs my pardon; not unless she does I sha'n't,' said Archie magnanimously.
10021{_}1180{_}'I shall certainly not allow her to do anything of the kind.'
10021{_}1181{_}At this moment Dilly came in, with her finger in her tiny mouth, and went up to Archie, drawling with a pout, and in a whining voice:  'I didn't mean to.'
10021{_}1182{_}Archie beamed at once.
10021{_}1183{_}'That's all right, Dilly,' he said forgivingly.
10021{_}1184{_}Then he turned to his mother.
10021{_}1185{_}'Mother, have you got that paper?'
10021{_}1186{_}'Yes, I have indeed!'
10021{_}1187{_}'Well, cross out--that, and put in Aspasia Matilda Ottley.
10021{_}1188{_}Sorry, Dilly!'
10021{_}1189{_}He kissed her, and they ran off together hand in hand; looking like cherubs, and laughing musically.
10021{_}1190{_}CHAPTER IX  Aylmer  At the Carlton Aylmer had easily persuaded Bruce and Edith to dine with him next day, although they were engaged to the elder Mrs Ottley already.
10021{_}1191{_}He said he expected two or three friends, and he convinced them they must come too.
10021{_}1192{_}It is only in London that people meet for the first time at a friend's house, and then, if they take to each other, practically live together for weeks after.
10021{_}1193{_}No matter what social engagements they may happen to have, these are all thrown aside for the new friend.
10021{_}1194{_}London people, with all their correctness, are really more unconventional than any other people in the world.
10021{_}1195{_}For instance, in Paris such a thing could never happen in any kind of _monde_, unless, perhaps, it were among artists and Bohemians; and even then it would be their great object to prove to one another that they were not wanting in distractions and were very much in demand; the lady, especially, would make the man wait for an opportunity of seeing her again, from calculation, to make herself seem of more value.
10021{_}1196{_}Such second-rate solicitudes would never even occur to Edith.
10021{_}1197{_}But she had a scruple about throwing over old Mrs Ottley.
10021{_}1198{_}*       *       *       *       *  'Won't your mother be disappointed?'
10021{_}1199{_}Edith asked.
10021{_}1200{_}'My dear Edith, you can safely leave that to me.
10021{_}1201{_}Of course she'll be disappointed, but you can go round and see her, and speak to her nicely and tell her that after all we can't come because we've got another engagement.'
10021{_}1202{_}'And am I to tell her it's a subsequent one?
10021{_}1203{_}Otherwise she'll wonder we didn't mention it before.'
10021{_}1204{_}'Don't be in a hurry, dear.
10021{_}1205{_}Don't rush things; remember... she's my mother.
10021{_}1206{_}Perhaps to you, Edith, it seems a rather old-fashioned idea, and I daresay you think it's rot, but to me there's something very sacred about the idea of a mother.'
10021{_}1207{_}He lit a cigarette and looked in the glass.
10021{_}1208{_}'Yes, dear.
10021{_}1209{_}Then, don't you think we really ought to have kept our promise to dine with her?
10021{_}1210{_}She'll probably be looking forward to it.
10021{_}1211{_}I daresay she's asked one or two people she thinks we like, to meet us.'
10021{_}1212{_}'Circumstances alter cases, Edith.
10021{_}1213{_}If it comes to that, Aylmer Ross has got two or three people coming to dine with him whom he thinks we might like.
10021{_}1214{_}He said so himself.
10021{_}1215{_}That's why he's asked us.'
10021{_}1216{_}'Yes, but he can't have asked them on purpose, Bruce, because, you see, we didn't know him on Thursday.'
10021{_}1217{_}'Well, why should he have asked them on purpose?
10021{_}1218{_}_How_ you argue!
10021{_}1219{_}_How_ you go on!
10021{_}1220{_}It really seems to me you're getting absurdly exacting and touchy, Edith dear.
10021{_}1221{_}I believe all those flowers from the embassy have positively turned your head.
10021{_}1222{_}_Why_ should he have asked them on purpose.
10021{_}1223{_}You admit yourself that we didn't even know the man last Thursday, and yet you expect--' Bruce stopped.
10021{_}1224{_}He had got into a slight tangle.
10021{_}1225{_}Edith looked away.
10021{_}1226{_}She had not quite mastered the art of the inward smile.
10021{_}1227{_}'Far better, in my opinion,' continued Bruce, walking up and down the room.--'Now, don't interrupt me in your impulsive way, but hear me out--it would be far more kind and sensible in every way for you to sit right down at that little writing-table, take out your stylographic pen and write and tell my mother that I have a bad attack of influenza....
10021{_}1228{_}Yes; one should always be considerate to one's parents.
10021{_}1229{_}I suppose it really is the way I was brought up that makes me feel this so keenly,' he explained.
10021{_}1230{_}Edith sat down to the writing-table.
10021{_}1231{_}'How bad is your influenza?'
10021{_}1232{_}'Oh, not very bad; because it would worry her: a slight attack.--Stop!
10021{_}1233{_}Not so very slight--we must let her think it's the ordinary kind, and then she'll think it's catching and she won't come here for a few days, and that will avoid our going into the matter in detail, which would be better.'
10021{_}1234{_}'If she thinks it's catching, dear, she'll want Archie and Dilly, and Miss Townsend and Nurse to go and stay with her in South Kensington, and that will be quite an affair.'
10021{_}1235{_}'Right as usual; very thoughtful of you; you're a clever little woman sometimes, Edith.
10021{_}1236{_}Wait!'
10021{_}1237{_}--he put up his hand with a gesture frequent with him, like a policeman stopping the traffic at Hyde Park Corner.
10021{_}1238{_}'Wait!--leave out the influenza altogether, and just say I've caught a slight chill.'
10021{_}1239{_}'Yes.
10021{_}1240{_}Then she'll come over at once, and you'll have to go to bed.'
10021{_}1241{_}'My dear Edith,' said Bruce, 'you're over-anxious; I shall do nothing of the kind.
10021{_}1242{_}There's no need that I should be laid up for this.
10021{_}1243{_}It's not serious.'
10021{_}1244{_}He was beginning to believe in his own illness, as usual.
10021{_}1245{_}'Air!
10021{_}1246{_}(I want to go round to the club)--tonic treatment!--that's the thing!--that's often the very best thing for a chill--this sort of chill.... Ah, that will do very nicely.
10021{_}1247{_}Very neatly written.... Good-bye, dear.'
10021{_}1248{_}*       *       *       *       *  As soon as Bruce had gone out Edith rang up the elder Mrs Ottley on the telephone, and relieved her anxiety in advance.
10021{_}1249{_}They were great friends; the sense of humour possessed by her mother-in-law took the sting out of the relationship.
10021{_}1250{_}*       *       *       *       *  The dinner at Aylmer's house was a great success.
10021{_}1251{_}Bruce enjoyed himself enormously, for he liked nothing better in the world than to give his opinion.
10021{_}1252{_}And Aylmer was specially anxious for his view as to the authenticity of a little Old Master he had acquired, and took notes, also, of a word of advice with regard to electric lighting, admitting he was not a very practical man, and Bruce evidently was.
10021{_}1253{_}Edith was interested and pleased to go to the house of her new friend and to reconstruct the scene as it must have been when Mrs Aylmer Ross had been there.
10021{_}1254{_}Freddy, the boy, was at school, but there was a portrait of him.
10021{_}1255{_}Evidently he resembled his father.
10021{_}1256{_}The sketch represented him with the same broad forehead, smooth, dense light hair, pale blue eyes under eyebrows with a slight frown in them, and the charming mouth rather fully curved, expressing an amiable and pleasure-loving nature.
10021{_}1257{_}The boy was good-looking, but not, Edith thought, as handsome as Aylmer.
10021{_}1258{_}The only other woman present was Lady Everard, a plump, talkative, middle-aged woman in black; the smiling widow of Lord Everard, and well known for her lavish musical hospitality and her vague and indiscriminate good nature.
10021{_}1259{_}She bristled with aigrettes and sparkled with diamonds and determination.
10021{_}1260{_}She was marvellously garrulous about nothing in particular.
10021{_}1261{_}She was a woman who never stopped talking for a single moment, but in a way that resembled leaking rather than laying down the law.
10021{_}1262{_}Tepidly, indifferently and rather amusingly she prattled on without ceasing, on every subject under the sun, and was socially a valuable help because where she was there was never an awkward pause--or any other kind.
10021{_}1263{_}Vincy was there and young Cricker, whose occasional depressed silences were alternated with what he called a certain amount of sparkling chaff.
10021{_}1264{_}Lady Everard told Edith that she felt quite like a sort of mother to Aylmer.
10021{_}1265{_}'Don't you think it's sad, Mrs Ottley,' she said, when they were alone, 'to think that the dear fellow has no wife to look after this dear little house?
10021{_}1266{_}It always seems to me such a pity, but still, I always say, at any rate Aylmer's married once, and that's more than most of them do nowadays.
10021{_}1267{_}It's simply horse's work to get them to do it at all.
10021{_}1268{_}Sometimes I think it's perfectly disgraceful.
10021{_}1269{_}And yet I can't help seeing how sensible it is of them too; you know, when you think of it, what with one thing and another, what does a man of the present day need a wife for?
10021{_}1270{_}What with the flats, where everything on earth is done for them, and the kindness of friends--just think how bachelors are spoilt by their married friends!--and their clubs, and the frightful expense of everything, it seems to me, as a general rule, that the average man must be madly unselfish or a perfect idiot to marry at all--that's what it seems to me--don't you?
10021{_}1271{_}When you think of all the responsibilities they take upon themselves!--and I'm sure there are not many modern wives who expect to do anything on earth but have their bills and bridge debts paid, and their perpetual young men asked to dinner, and one thing and another.
10021{_}1272{_}Of course, though, there are some exceptions.'
10021{_}1273{_}She smiled amiably.
10021{_}1274{_}'Aylmer tells me you have two children; very sweet of you, I'm sure.
10021{_}1275{_}What darling pets they must be!
10021{_}1276{_}Angels!--Angels!
10021{_}1277{_}Oh, I'm so fond of children!
10021{_}1278{_}But, particularly--isn't it funny?--when they're not there, because I can't stand their noise.
10021{_}1279{_}Now my little grandchildren--my daughter Eva's been married ten years--Lady Lindley, you know--hers are perfect pets and heavenly angels, but I can't stand them for more than a few minutes at a time.
10021{_}1280{_}I have nerves, so much so, do you know (partly because I go in a good deal for music and intellect and so on), so much so, that I very nearly had a rest cure at the end of last season, and I should have had, probably, but that new young French singer came over with a letter of introduction to me, and of course I couldn't desert him, but had to do my very best.
10021{_}1281{_}Ever heard him sing?
10021{_}1282{_}Yes, you would, of course.
10021{_}1283{_}Oh, how wonderful it is!'
10021{_}1284{_}Edith waited in vain for a pause to say she didn't know the name of the singer.
10021{_}1285{_}Lady Everard went on, leaning comfortably back in Aylmer's arm-chair.
10021{_}1286{_}'Willie Cricker dances very prettily, too; he came to one of my evenings and had quite a success.
10021{_}1287{_}Only an amateur, of course; but rather nice.
10021{_}1288{_}However, like all amateurs he wants to perform only when people would rather he didn't, and when they want him to he won't; he refuses.
10021{_}1289{_}That's the amateur all over.
10021{_}1290{_}The professional comes up to the scratch when wanted and stops when the performance is not required.
10021{_}1291{_}It's all the difference in the world, isn't it, Mrs Ottley?
10021{_}1292{_}Still, he's a nice boy.
10021{_}1293{_}Are you fond of music?'
10021{_}1294{_}'Very.
10021{_}1295{_}Really fond of it; but I'm only a listener.'
10021{_}1296{_}Lady Everard seemed delighted and brightened up.
10021{_}1297{_}'Oh, you don't sing or play?--you must come to one of my Musical Evenings.
10021{_}1298{_}We have all the stars in the season at times--dear Melba and Caruso--and darling Bemberk and dear Debussy!
10021{_}1299{_}Oh!
10021{_}1300{_}don't laugh at my enthusiasm, my dear; but I'm quite music-mad--and then, of course, we have any amount of amateurs, and all the new young professionals that are coming on.
10021{_}1301{_}In my opinion Paul La France, that's the young man I was telling you about, will be one of the very very best--quite at the top of the tree, and I'm determined he shall.
10021{_}1302{_}But of course, he needs care and encouragement.
10021{_}1303{_}I think of his giving a _Conference_, in which he'll lecture on his own singing.
10021{_}1304{_}I shall be on the platform to make a sort of introductory speech and Monti, of course, will accompany.
10021{_}1305{_}He is the only accompanist that counts.
10021{_}1306{_}But then I suppose he's been accompanying somebody or other ever since he was a little boy, so it's second nature to him.
10021{_}1307{_}And you must come, and bring your husband.
10021{_}1308{_}Does he go with you to places?
10021{_}1309{_}Very nice of him.
10021{_}1310{_}Nowadays if husbands and wives don't occasionally go to the same parties they have hardly any opportunity of meeting at all; that's what I always say.
10021{_}1311{_}But then, of course, _you're_ still almost on your honeymoon, aren't you?
10021{_}1312{_}Charming!'
10021{_}1313{_}In the dining-room Cricker was confiding in Aylmer, while Vincy and Bruce discussed the Old Master.
10021{_}1314{_}'Awful, you know,' Cricker said, in a low voice--' this girl's mania for me!
10021{_}1315{_}I get wires and telephones all day long; she hardly gives me time to shave.
10021{_}1316{_}And she's jolly pretty, so I don't like to chuck it; in fact, I daren't.
10021{_}1317{_}But her one cry is 'Cold; cold; cold!'
10021{_}1318{_}She says I'm as cold as a stone.
10021{_}1319{_}What do you thing of that?'
10021{_}1320{_}'You may be a stone, and a rolling one at that, said Aylmer, 'but there are other pebbles on the beach, I daresay.'
10021{_}1321{_}'I bet not one of them as stony as I am!'
10021{_}1322{_}cried Cricker.
10021{_}1323{_}Cricker came a little nearer, lowering his voice again.
10021{_}1324{_}'It's a very peculiar case,' he said proudly.
10021{_}1325{_}'Of course; it always is.'
10021{_}1326{_}'You see, she's frightfully pretty, on the stage, and married!
10021{_}1327{_}One of the most awkward positions a person can be in.
10021{_}1328{_}Mind you, I'm sorry for her.
10021{_}1329{_}I thought of consulting you about something if you'll give me a minute or two, old chap.'
10021{_}1330{_}He took out a letter-case.
10021{_}1331{_}'I don't mean Ill show you this--oh no, I can't show it--it isn't compromising.'
10021{_}1332{_}'Of course not.
10021{_}1333{_}No-one really likes to show a really lukewarm love letter.
10021{_}1334{_}Besides it would hardly be--'  Cricker put the case back.
10021{_}1335{_}My dear chap!
10021{_}1336{_}I wasn't going to show it to you--I shouldn't dream of such a thing--to anybody; but I was just going to read you out a sentence from which you can form an opinion of my predicament.
10021{_}1337{_}It's no good mincing matters, old boy, the woman is crazy mad about me--there you've got it straight--in a nutshell.--Crazy!'
10021{_}1338{_}'She certainly can't be very sane,' returned Aylmer.
10021{_}1339{_}Before the end of the evening Aylmer had arranged to take the Ottleys to see a play that was having a run.
10021{_}1340{_}After this he dropped in to tea to discuss it and Bruce kept him to dinner.
10021{_}1341{_}Day after day went on, and they saw him continually.
10021{_}1342{_}He had never shown by word or manner any more of his sentiment than on the second occasion when they had met but Edith was growing thoroughly accustomed to this new interest, and it certainly gave a zest to her existence, for she knew, as women do know, or at any rate she believed, that she had an attraction for him, which he didn't intend to give away.
10021{_}1343{_}The situation was pleasant and notwithstanding Vincy's slight anxiety, she persisted in seeing nothing in it to fear in any way.
10021{_}1344{_}Aylmer didn't even flirt.
10021{_}1345{_}One day, at Vincy's rooms, she thought he seemed different.
10021{_}1346{_}Vincy, with all his gentle manner, had in art an extraordinary taste for brutality and violence, and his rooms were covered with pictures by Futurists and Cubists, wild studies by wild men from Tahiti and a curious collection of savage ornaments and weapons.
10021{_}1347{_}'I don't quite see Vincy handling that double-edged Chinese sword, do you?
10021{_}1348{_}said Aylmer, laughing.
10021{_}1349{_}'No, nor do I; but I do like to look at it,' Vincy said.
10021{_}1350{_}They went into the little dining-room, which was curiously furnished with a green marble dining-table, narrow, as in the pictures of the Last Supper, at which the guests could sit on one side only to be waited on from the other.
10021{_}1351{_}On it as decoration (it was laid for two, side by side) were some curious straw mats, a few laurel leaves, a little marble statuette of Pan, and three Tangerine oranges.
10021{_}1352{_}'Oh, Vincy, do tell me--what are you going to eat tonight?'
10021{_}1353{_}Edith exclaimed.
10021{_}1354{_}'Unless you're with other people I can never imagine you sitting down to a proper meal.'
10021{_}1355{_}Eat?
10021{_}1356{_}Oh a nice orange, I think,' said he.
10021{_}1357{_}Sometimes when I'm alone I just have a nice egg and a glass of water, I do myself very well.
10021{_}1358{_}Don't worry about me, Edith.'
10021{_}1359{_}When they were alone for a moment Aylmer looked out of the window.
10021{_}1360{_}It was rather high up, and they looked down on the hustling crowds of people pushing along through the warm air in Victoria Street.
10021{_}1361{_}'It's getting decent weather,' he said.
10021{_}1362{_}'Yes, quite warm.'
10021{_}1363{_}They always suddenly talked commonplaces when they were first left alone.
10021{_}1364{_}'I may be going away pretty soon,' he said.
10021{_}1365{_}'Going away!
10021{_}1366{_}Oh, where?'
10021{_}1367{_}'I'm not quite sure yet.'
10021{_}1368{_}There was a pause.
10021{_}1369{_}'Well, you'll come to tea tomorrow, won't you?
10021{_}1370{_}said Edith.
10021{_}1371{_}'Yes, indeed, thank you--thank you so much.
10021{_}1372{_}I shall look forward to it.
10021{_}1373{_}At five?'
10021{_}1374{_}He spoke formally.
10021{_}1375{_}'At four,' said Edith.
10021{_}1376{_}'I shall be lunching not very far from you tomorrow.'
10021{_}1377{_}'At a quarter to four,' said Edith.
10021{_}1378{_}'I wonder who this other place is laid for,' said Aylmer, looking at the table.
10021{_}1379{_}'How indiscreet of you!
10021{_}1380{_}So do I.
10021{_}1381{_}One must find out.'
10021{_}1382{_}'How?
10021{_}1383{_}By asking?'
10021{_}1384{_}'Good heavens, no!'
10021{_}1385{_}cried Edith.
10021{_}1386{_}'What an extraordinary idea!'
10021{_}1387{_}CHAPTER X  Shopping Chez Soi  Edith was expecting Aylmer to call that afternoon before he went away.
10021{_}1388{_}She was surprised to find how perturbed she was at the idea of his going away.
10021{_}1389{_}He had become almost a part of their daily existence, and seeing him was certainly quite the most amusing and exciting experience she had ever had.
10021{_}1390{_}And now it was coming to an end.
10021{_}1391{_}Some obscure clairvoyance told her that his leaving and telling her of it in this vague way had some reference to her; but perhaps (she thought) she was wrong; perhaps it was simply that, after the pleasant intercourse and semi-intimacy of the last few weeks, he was going to something that interested him more?
10021{_}1392{_}He was a widower; and still a young man.
10021{_}1393{_}Perhaps he was in love with someone.
10021{_}1394{_}This idea was far from agreeable, although except the first and second time they met he had never said a word that could be described even as flirtation.
10021{_}1395{_}He showed admiration for her, and pleasure in her society, but he rarely saw her alone.
10021{_}1396{_}The few visits and _tete-a-tetes_ had always begun by conventional commonplace phrases and embarrassment, and had ended in a delightful sympathy, in animated conversation, in a flowing confidence and gaiety, and in long discussions on books, and art, and principally people.
10021{_}1397{_}That was all.
10021{_}1398{_}In fact he had become, in two or three weeks, in a sense _l'ami de la maison_; they went everywhere with him and met nearly every day, and Bruce appeared to adore him.
10021{_}1399{_}It was entirely different from her long and really intimate friendship with Vincy.
10021{_}1400{_}Vincy was her confidant, her friend.
10021{_}1401{_}She could tell _him_ everything, and she did, and he confided in her and told her all except one side of his life, of which she was aware, but to which she never referred.
10021{_}1402{_}This was his secret romance with a certain girl artist of whom he never spoke, although Edith knew that some day he would tell her about that also.
10021{_}1403{_}But with Aylmer there was, and would always be, less real freedom and impersonal frankness, because there was so much more selfconsciousness; in fact because there was an unacknowledged but very strong mutual physical attraction.
10021{_}1404{_}Edith had, however, felt until now merely the agreeable excitement of knowing that a man she liked, and in whom she was immensely interested, was growing apparently devoted to her, while _she_ had always believed that she would know how to deal with the case in such a way that it could never lead to anything more--that is to say, to more than _she_ wished.
10021{_}1405{_}And now, he was going away.
10021{_}1406{_}Why?
10021{_}1407{_}And where?
10021{_}1408{_}However, the first thing to consider was that she would see him today.
10021{_}1409{_}The result of this consideration was the obvious one.
10021{_}1410{_}She must do some shopping.
10021{_}1411{_}Edith was remarkably feminine in every attribute, in manner, in movement and in appearance; indeed, for a woman of the present day unusually and refreshingly feminine.
10021{_}1412{_}Yet she had certain mental characteristics which were entirely unlike most women.
10021{_}1413{_}One was her extreme aversion for shops, and indeed for going into any concrete little details.
10021{_}1414{_}It has been said that her feeling for dress was sure and unerring.
10021{_}1415{_}But it was entirely that of the artist; it was impressionistic.
10021{_}1416{_}Edith was very clever, indeed, most ingenious, in managing practical affairs, as long as she was the director, the general of the campaign.
10021{_}1417{_}But she did not like carrying out in detail her plans.
10021{_}1418{_}She liked to be the architect, not the workman.
10021{_}1419{_}For example, the small household affairs in the flat went on wheels; everything was almost always perfect.
10021{_}1420{_}But Edith did not rattle her housekeeping keys, or count the coals, nor did she even go through accounts, or into the kitchen every day.
10021{_}1421{_}The secret was simple.
10021{_}1422{_}She had a good cook and housekeeper, who managed all these important but tedious details admirably, under her suggestions.
10021{_}1423{_}In order to do this Edith had to practise a little fraud on Bruce, a justifiable and quite unselfish one.
10021{_}1424{_}She gave the cook and housekeeper a quarter of her dress allowance, in addition to the wages Bruce considered sufficient; because Bruce believed that they could not afford more than a certain amount for a cook, while he admitted that Edith, who had a few hundred pounds a year of her own, might need to spend this on dress.
10021{_}1425{_}Very little of it went on dress, although Edith was not very economical.
10021{_}1426{_}But she had a plan of her own; she knew that to be dressed in a very ordinary style (that is to say, simple, conventional, comme il faut) suited her, by throwing her unusual beauty into relief.
10021{_}1427{_}Occasionally a touch of individuality was added, when she wanted to have a special effect.
10021{_}1428{_}But she never entered a shop; very rarely interviewed a milliner.
10021{_}1429{_}It was always done for her.
10021{_}1430{_}She was easy to dress, being tall, slim and remarkably pretty.
10021{_}1431{_}She thought that most women make a great mistake in allowing dress to be the master instead of the servant of their good looks; many women were, she considered, entirely crushed and made insignificant by the beauty of their clothes.
10021{_}1432{_}The important thing was to have a distinguished appearance, and this cannot, of course, easily be obtained without expensive elegance.
10021{_}1433{_}But Edith was twenty-eight, and looked younger, so she could dress simply.
10021{_}1434{_}This morning Edith had telephoned to her friend, Miss Bennett, an old schoolfellow who had nothing to do, and adored commissions.
10021{_}1435{_}Edith, sitting by the fire or at the 'phone, gave her orders, which were always decisive, short and yet meticulous.
10021{_}1436{_}Miss Bennett was a little late this morning, and Edith had been getting quite anxious to see her.
10021{_}1437{_}When she at last arrived--she was a nondescript-looking girl, with a small hat squashed on her head, a serge coat and skirt, black gloves and shoes with spats--Edith greeted her rather reproachfully with:  'You're late, Grace.'
10021{_}1438{_}'Sorry,' said Grace.
10021{_}1439{_}The name suited her singularly badly.
10021{_}1440{_}She was plain, but had a pleasant face, a pink complexion, small bright eyes, protruding teeth and a scenario for a figure, merely a collection of bones on which a dress could be hung.
10021{_}1441{_}She was devoted to Edith, and to a few other friends of both sexes, of whom she made idols.
10021{_}1442{_}She was hard, abrupt, enthusiastic, ignorant and humorous.
10021{_}1443{_}'Sorry, but I had to do a lot of--'  'All right,' interrupted Edith.
10021{_}1444{_}'You couldn't help it.
10021{_}1445{_}Listen' to what I want you to do.'
10021{_}1446{_}'Go ahead,' said Miss Bennett, taking out a note-book and pencil.
10021{_}1447{_}Edith spoke in her low, soft, impressive voice, rather slowly.
10021{_}1448{_}'Go anywhere you like and bring me back two or three perfectly simple tea-gowns--you know the sort of shape, rather like evening cloaks--straight lines--none of the new draperies and curves--in red, blue and black.'
10021{_}1449{_}'On appro.?'

10021{_}1497{_}Vincy had brought it to convert her to Post-Impressionism.
10021{_}1498{_}When Archie and Dilly were out, and Edith, who always got up rather early, was alone, she often passed her morning hours in reading, dreaming, playing the piano, or even in thinking.
10021{_}1499{_}She was one of the few women who really can think, and enjoy it.

10021{_}1501{_}Considering she was usually the most reposeful woman in London, she was rather restless today.

10021{_}1546{_}CHAPTER XI  P.P.C.
10021{_}1547{_}'It always seems to me so unlike you,' Aylmer said (he had arrived punctually at twenty minutes to four)--'your extreme fondness for newspapers.

10021{_}1652{_}'Mr Vincy,' announced the servant.
10021{_}1653{_}CHAPTER XII  'The Moonshine Girl'  The next evening Bruce and Edith were going to the Society Theatre with Aylmer.
10021{_}1654{_}It was their last meeting before he was to go away, Edith half expected that he would put it off, but there was no change made in the plans, and they met in the box as arranged.
10021{_}1655{_}Aylmer had expected during the whole day to hear that she had managed to postpone the party.
10021{_}1656{_}At one moment he was frightened and rather horrified when he thought of what he had done.
10021{_}1657{_}At another he was delighted and enchanted about it, and told himself that it was absolutely justified.
10021{_}1658{_}After all, he couldn't do more than go away if he found he was too fond of her.
10021{_}1659{_}No hero of romance could be expected to do more than that, and he wasn't a hero of romance; he didn't pretend to be.
10021{_}1660{_}But he _was_ a good fellow--and though Bruce's absurdities irritated him a great deal he had a feeling of delicacy towards him, and a scrupulousness that is not to be found every day.
10021{_}1661{_}At other moments Aylmer swore to himself, cursing his impulsiveness, fearing she now would really not ever think of him as he wished, but as a hustling sort of brute.
10021{_}1662{_}But no--he didn't care.
10021{_}1663{_}He had come at last to close quarters with her.
10021{_}1664{_}He had kissed the pretty little mouth that he had so often watched with longing.
10021{_}1665{_}He admitted to himself that he had really wished to pose a little in her eyes: to be the noble hero in the third act who goes away from temptation.
10021{_}1666{_}But who does not wish for the _beau role_ before one's idol?
10021{_}1667{_}*       *       *       *       *  This meeting at the play tonight was the sort of anti-climax that is almost invariable in a London romance.
10021{_}1668{_}How he looked forward to it!
10021{_}1669{_}For after Vincy came in only a few banalities had been said.
10021{_}1670{_}He was to see her now for the last time--the first time since he had given himself away to her.
10021{_}1671{_}Probably it was only her usual kindness to others that prevented her getting out of the evening plans, he thought.
10021{_}1672{_}Or--did she want to see him once more?
10021{_}1673{_}At dinner before the play Edith was very bright, and particularly pretty.
10021{_}1674{_}Bruce, too, was in good spirits.
10021{_}1675{_}'It's rather sickening,' he remarked, 'Aylmer going away like this; we shall miss him horribly, sha'n't we?
10021{_}1676{_}And then, where's the sense, Edith, in a chap leaving London where he's been the whole of the awful winter, just as it begins to be pleasant here?
10021{_}1677{_}Pass the salt; don't spill it--that's unlucky.
10021{_}1678{_}Not that I believe in any superstitious rot.
10021{_}1679{_}I can see the charm of the quaint old ideas about black cats and so forth, but I don't for one moment attach any importance to them, nor to the number thirteen, nor any of that sort of bosh.
10021{_}1680{_}Indeed as a matter of fact, I walked round a ladder only today rather than go under it.
10021{_}1681{_}But that's simply because I don't go in for trying to be especially original.'
10021{_}1682{_}'No, dear.
10021{_}1683{_}I think you're quite right.'
10021{_}1684{_}'And oddly enough--as I was trying to tell you just now, only you didn't seem to be listening--a black cat ran across my path only this afternoon.'
10021{_}1685{_}He smiled, gratified at the recollection.

10021{_}1691{_}Yes, Aylmer is a thoroughly good chap, and he and I have enormous sympathy.

10021{_}1731{_}One can't get in a word edgeways, but _when_ one does--well, she listens, and laughs at one's jokes, and that sort of thing.

10021{_}1739{_}No, Edith dear, I should tell you, if you would only listen to me, to keep up your music, but you won't and there's an end of it...That souffle was really very good.

10021{_}1742{_}'Oh yes, she's not bad,' said Edith.
10021{_}1743{_}She knew that if Bruce had been aware the cook's remuneration was adequate he would not have enjoyed his dinner.
10021{_}1744{_}*       *       *       *       *  They were in the box in the pretty theatre.
10021{_}1745{_}Lady Everard, very smart in black, sparkling with diamonds, was already there with Aylmer.
10021{_}1746{_}Vincy had not arrived.
10021{_}1747{_}The house was crammed to the ceiling.
10021{_}1748{_}Gay, electrical music of exhilarating futility was being played by the orchestra.
10021{_}1749{_}The scene consisted of model cottages; a chorus of pretty girls in striped cotton were singing.
10021{_}1750{_}The heroine came on; she was well known for her smile, which had become public property on picture post-cards and the Obosh bottles.
10021{_}1751{_}She was dressed as a work-girl also, but in striped silk with a real lace apron and a few diamonds.
10021{_}1752{_}Then the hero arrived.
10021{_}1753{_}He wore a red shirt, brown boots, and had a tenor voice.
10021{_}1754{_}He explained an interesting little bit of the plot, which included an eccentric will and other novelties.
10021{_}1755{_}The humorous dandy of the play was greeted with shouts of joy by the chorus and equal enthusiasm by the audience.
10021{_}1756{_}He agreed to change places with the hero, who wished to give up one hundred and forty thousand pounds a year to marry the heroine.
10021{_}1757{_}'Very disinterested,' murmured Lady Everard.
10021{_}1758{_}'Very nice of him, I'm sure.
10021{_}1759{_}It isn't many people that would do a thing like that.
10021{_}1760{_}A nice voice, too.
10021{_}1761{_}Of course, this is not what _I_ call good music, but it's very bright in its way, and the words--I always think these words are so clever.
10021{_}1762{_}So witty.
10021{_}1763{_}Listen to them--do listen to them, dear Mrs Ottley.'
10021{_}1764{_}They listened to the beautiful words sung, of which the refrain ran as follows:--    'The Author told the Actor,   (The Actor had a fit).
10021{_}1765{_}The Box Office man told the Programme-girl,   The Theatre all was in quite a whirl.
10021{_}1766{_}The call-boy told the Chorus.
10021{_}1767{_}(Whatever could it be?)
10021{_}1768{_}The super asked the Manager,   What did the Censor see?'
10021{_}1769{_}'Charming,' murmured Lady Everard; 'brilliant--I know his father so well.'
10021{_}1770{_}'Whose father--the censor's?'
10021{_}1771{_}'Oh, the father of the composer--a very charming man.
10021{_}1772{_}When he was young he used to come to my parties--my Wednesdays.
10021{_}1773{_}I used to have Wednesdays then.
10021{_}1774{_}I don't have Wednesdays now.
10021{_}1775{_}I think it better to telephone at the last minute any particular day for my afternoons because, after all, you never know when the artists one wants are disengaged, does one?
10021{_}1776{_}You're coming on Wednesday to hear Paul La France sing, dear Mrs Ottley?'
10021{_}1777{_}Edith smiled and nodded assent, trying to stop the incessant trickle of Lady Everard's leaking conversation.
10021{_}1778{_}She loved theatres, and she enjoyed hearing every word, which was impossible while there was more dialogue in the box than on the stage; also, Aylmer was sitting behind her.
10021{_}1779{_}The comic lady now came on; there were shrieks of laughter at her unnecessary and irrelevant green boots and crinoline and Cockney accent.
10021{_}1780{_}She proposed to marry the hero, who ran away from her.
10021{_}1781{_}There was more chorus; and the curtain fell.
10021{_}1782{_}In the interval Vincy arrived.
10021{_}1783{_}He and Bruce went into the little salon behind the box.
10021{_}1784{_}Lady Everard joined them there.
10021{_}1785{_}Edith and Aylmer looked round the house.
10021{_}1786{_}The audience at the Society Theatre is a special one; as at the plays in which the favourite actor-managers and _jeunes premiers_ perform there are always far more women than men, at this theatre there are always far more men than women.
10021{_}1787{_}The stage box opposite our friends was filled with a party of about ten men.
10021{_}1788{_}'It looks like a jury,' said Edith.
10021{_}1789{_}'Perhaps it is.'
10021{_}1790{_}'Probably a board of directors,' said Aylmer.
10021{_}1791{_}The first two rows of the stalls were principally occupied by middle-aged and rather elderly gentlemen.
10021{_}1792{_}Many had grey moustaches and a military bearing.
10021{_}1793{_}Others were inclined to be stout, with brilliant exuberant manners and very dark hair that simply wouldn't lie flat.
10021{_}1794{_}There were a great many parties made up like those of our friends--of somebody in love with somebody, surrounded by chaperons.
10021{_}1795{_}These were the social people, and also there were a certain number of young men with pretty women who were too fashionably dressed, too much made up, and who were looking forward too much to supper.
10021{_}1796{_}These ladies seemed inclined to crab the play, and to find unimportant little faults with the unimportant actresses.
10021{_}1797{_}There were many Americans--who took it seriously; and altogether one could see it was an immense success; in other words everyone had paid for their seats...        *       *       *       *       *  The play was over; Aylmer had not had a word with Edith.
10021{_}1798{_}He was going away the next day, and he asked them all to supper.
10021{_}1799{_}Of course he drove Edith, and Lady Everard took the other two in her motor....  'You're an angel if you've forgiven me,' he said, as they went out.
10021{_}1800{_}CHAPTER XIII  The Supper-party  'Have you forgiven me?'
10021{_}1801{_}he asked anxiously, as soon as they were in the dark shelter of the cab.
10021{_}1802{_}'Yes, oh yes.
10021{_}1803{_}Please don't let's talk about it any more... What time do you start tomorrow?'
10021{_}1804{_}'You think I ought to go then?'
10021{_}1805{_}'You say so.'
10021{_}1806{_}'But you'd rather I remained here; rather we should go on as we are--wouldn't you?'
10021{_}1807{_}'Well, you know I should never have dreamt of suggesting you should go away.
10021{_}1808{_}I like you to be here.'
10021{_}1809{_}'At any cost to me?
10021{_}1810{_}No, Edith; I can't stand it.
10021{_}1811{_}And since I've told you it's harder.
10021{_}1812{_}Your knowing makes it harder.'
10021{_}1813{_}'I should have thought that if you liked anyone so _very_ much, you would want to see them all the time, as much as possible, always--even with other people...anything rather than not see them--be away altogether.
10021{_}1814{_}At least, that's how I should feel.'
10021{_}1815{_}'No doubt you would; that's a woman's view.
10021{_}1816{_}And besides, you see, you don't care!'
10021{_}1817{_}'The more I cared, the less I should go away, I think.'
10021{_}1818{_}'But, haven't I tried?
10021{_}1819{_}And I can't bear it.
10021{_}1820{_}You don't know how cruel you are with your sweetness, Edith...Oh, put yourself in my place!
10021{_}1821{_}How do you suppose I feel when I've been with you like this, near you, looking at you, delighting in you the whole evening--and then, after supper, you go away with Bruce?
10021{_}1822{_}_You've_ had a very pleasant evening, no doubt; it's all right for you to feel you've got me, as you know you have--and with no fear, no danger.
10021{_}1823{_}Yes, you enjoy it!'
10021{_}1824{_}'Oh, Aylmer!'
10021{_}1825{_}He saw in the half-darkness that her eyes looked reproachful.
10021{_}1826{_}'I didn't mean it.
10021{_}1827{_}I'm sorry--I'm always being sorry.'
10021{_}1828{_}His bitter tone changed to gentleness.
10021{_}1829{_}'I want to speak to you now, Edith.
10021{_}1830{_}We haven't much time.
10021{_}1831{_}Don't take away your hand a minute....I always told you, didn't I, that the atmosphere round you is so clear that I feel with you I'm in the Palace of Truth?
10021{_}1832{_}You're so _real_.
10021{_}1833{_}You're the only woman I ever met who really cared for truth.
10021{_}1834{_}You're not afraid of it; and you're as straight and honourable as a man!
10021{_}1835{_}I don't mean you can't diplomatise if you choose, of course, and better than anyone; but it isn't your nature to deceive yourself, nor anyone else.
10021{_}1836{_}I recognise that in you.
10021{_}1837{_}I love it.
10021{_}1838{_}And that's why I can't pretend or act with you; I must be frank.'
10021{_}1839{_}'Please, do be frank.'
10021{_}1840{_}'I love you.
10021{_}1841{_}I'm madly in love with you.
10021{_}1842{_}I adore you.'
10021{_}1843{_}Aylmer stopped, deeply moved at the sound of his own words.
10021{_}1844{_}Few people realise the effect such words have on the speaker.
10021{_}1845{_}Saying them to her was a great joy, and an indulgence, but it increased painfully his passionate feeling, making it more accentuated and acute.
10021{_}1846{_}To let himself go verbally was a wild, bitter pleasure.
10021{_}1847{_}It hurt him, and he enjoyed it.
10021{_}1848{_}'And I'd do anything in the world to get you.
10021{_}1849{_}And I'd do anything in the world for you, too.
10021{_}1850{_}And if you cared for me I'd go away all the same.
10021{_}1851{_}At least, I believe I should...We shall be there in a minute.
10021{_}1852{_}'Listen, dear.
10021{_}1853{_}I want you, occasionally, to write to me; there's no earthly reason why you shouldn't.
10021{_}1854{_}I'll let you know my address.
10021{_}1855{_}It will prevent my being too miserable, or rushing back.
10021{_}1856{_}And will you do something else for me?'
10021{_}1857{_}'Anything.'
10021{_}1858{_}'Angel!
10021{_}1859{_}Well, when you write, call me Aylmer.
10021{_}1860{_}You never have yet, in a letter.
10021{_}1861{_}Treat me just like a friend--as you treat Vincy.
10021{_}1862{_}Tell me what you're doing, where you're going, who you see; about Archie and Dilly; about your new dresses and hats; what you're reading--any little thing, so that I'm still in touch with you.'
10021{_}1863{_}'Yes, I will; I shall like to.
10021{_}1864{_}And don't be depressed, Aylmer.
10021{_}1865{_}Do enjoy your journey; write to me, too.'
10021{_}1866{_}'Yes, I'm going to write to you, but only in an official way, only for Bruce.
10021{_}1867{_}And, listen.
10021{_}1868{_}Take care of yourself.
10021{_}1869{_}You're too unselfish.
10021{_}1870{_}Do what you want sometimes, not what other people want all the time.
10021{_}1871{_}Don't read too much by electric light and try your eyes.
10021{_}1872{_}And don't go out in these thin shoes in damp weather--promise!'

10021{_}1874{_}'Be a great deal with the children.
10021{_}1875{_}I like to think of you with them.
10021{_}1876{_}And I hope you won't be always going out,' he continued, in a tone of unconscious command, which she enjoyed....'Please don't be continually at Lady Everard's, or at the Mitchells', or anywhere.
10021{_}1877{_}I hate you to be admired--how I hate it!'
10021{_}1878{_}'Fancy!
10021{_}1879{_}And I was always brought up to believe people are proud of what's called the 'success' of the people that they--like.'
10021{_}1880{_}'Don't you believe it, Edith!
10021{_}1881{_}That's all bosh--vanity and nonsense.
10021{_}1882{_}At any rate, I know I'm not.
10021{_}1883{_}In fact, as I can't have you myself, I would really like you to be shut up.
10021{_}1884{_}Very happy, very well, with everything in the world you like, even thinking of me a little, but absolutely shut up!
10021{_}1885{_}And if you did go out, for a breath of air, I should like no-one to see you.
10021{_}1886{_}I'd like you to cover up your head--wear a thick veil--and a thick loose dress!'
10021{_}1887{_}'You're very Oriental!'
10021{_}1888{_}she laughed.
10021{_}1889{_}'I'm not a bit Oriental; I'm human.
10021{_}1890{_}It's selfish, I suppose, you think?
10021{_}1891{_}Well, let me tell you, if you care to know, that it's _love_, and nothing else, Edith....Now, is there anything in the world I can do for you while I'm away?
10021{_}1892{_}It would be kind to ask me.
10021{_}1893{_}Remember I shan't see you for three months.
10021{_}1894{_}I may come back in September.
10021{_}1895{_}Can't I send you something--do something that you'd like?
10021{_}1896{_}I count on you to ask me at any time if there's anything in the world I could do for you, no matter what!'
10021{_}1897{_}No woman could help being really pleased at such whole-hearted devotion and such Bluebeard-like views--especially when they were not going to be carried out.
10021{_}1898{_}Edith was thrilled by the passionate emotion she felt near her.
10021{_}1899{_}How cold it would be when he had gone!
10021{_}1900{_}He _was_ nice, handsome, clever--a darling!
10021{_}1901{_}'Don't forget me, Aylmer.
10021{_}1902{_}I don't want you to forget me.
10021{_}1903{_}Later on we'll have a real friendship.'
10021{_}1904{_}'_Friendship!_ Don't use that word.
10021{_}1905{_}It's so false--such humbug--for _me_ at any rate.
10021{_}1906{_}To say I could care for you as a friend is simply blasphemy!
10021{_}1907{_}How can it be possible for _me_?
10021{_}1908{_}But I'll try.
10021{_}1909{_}Thanks for _any_thing!
10021{_}1910{_}You're an angel--I'll try.'
10021{_}1911{_}'And it's horribly inconsistent, and no doubt very wicked of me, but, do you know, I should be rather pained if I heard you had fallen in love with someone else.'
10021{_}1912{_}'Ah, that would be impossible!'
10021{_}1913{_}he cried.
10021{_}1914{_}'Never--never!
10021{_}1915{_}It's the real thing; there never was anyone like you, there never will be.
10021{_}1916{_}Let me look at you once more....Oh, Edith!
10021{_}1917{_}And now--here we are.'
10021{_}1918{_}Edith took away her hand.
10021{_}1919{_}'Your scarf's coming off, you'll catch cold,' said Aylmer, and as he was trying, rather awkwardly, to put the piece of blue chiffon round her head he drew the dear head to him and kissed her harshly.
10021{_}1920{_}She could not protest; it was too final; besides, they were arriving; the cab stopped.
10021{_}1921{_}Vincy came to the door.
10021{_}1922{_}'Welcome to Normanhurst!'
10021{_}1923{_}cried Vincy, with unnecessary facetiousness, giving them a slightly anxious glance.
10021{_}1924{_}He thought Edith had more colour than usual.
10021{_}1925{_}Aylmer was pale.
10021{_}1926{_}*       *       *       *       *  The supper was an absolute and complete failure; the guests displayed the forced gaiety and real depression, and constrained absentmindedness, of genuine and hopeless boredom.
10021{_}1927{_}Except for Lady Everard's ceaseless flow of empty prattle the pauses would have been too obvious.
10021{_}1928{_}Edith, for whom it was a dreary anti-climax, was rather silent.
10021{_}1929{_}Aylmer talked more, and a little more loudly, than usual, and looked worn.
10021{_}1930{_}Bruce, whom champagne quickly saddened, became vaguely reminiscent and communicative about old, dead, forgotten grievances of the past, while Vincy, who was a little shocked at what he saw (and he always saw everything), did his very best, just saving the entertainment from being a too disastrous frost.
10021{_}1931{_}'Well!
10021{_}1932{_}Good luck!'
10021{_}1933{_}said Aylmer, lifting his glass with sham conviviality.'
10021{_}1934{_}I start tomorrow morning by the Orient Express.'
10021{_}1935{_}'Hooray!'
10021{_}1936{_}whispered Vincy primly.
10021{_}1937{_}'Doesn't it sound romantic and exciting?'
10021{_}1938{_}Edith said.
10021{_}1939{_}'The two words together are so delightfully adventurous.
10021{_}1940{_}Orient--the languid East, and yet express--quickness, speed.
10021{_}1941{_}It's a fascinating blend of ideas.'

10021{_}1943{_}I suppose perhaps we may take the children to Westgate for Whitsuntide, and that's about all.
10021{_}1944{_}Not that there isn't quite a good hotel there, and of course it's all right for me, because I shall play golf all day and run up to town when I want to.
10021{_}1945{_}Still, it's very different from one of these jolly long journeys that you gay bachelors can indulge in.'
10021{_}1946{_}'But I'm not a gay bachelor.
10021{_}1947{_}My boy is coming to join me in the summer holidays, wherever I am,' said Aylmer.
10021{_}1948{_}'Ah, but that's not the point.
10021{_}1949{_}I should like to go with you now--at once.
10021{_}1950{_}Don't you wish we were both going, Edith?
10021{_}1951{_}Why aren't we going with him tomorrow?'
10021{_}1952{_}'Surely June's just the nice time in London, Bruce,' said Vincy, in his demure voice.
10021{_}1953{_}'Won't it be terribly hot?'
10021{_}1954{_}said Lady Everard vaguely.
10021{_}1955{_}She always thought every place must be terribly hot.
10021{_}1956{_}'Venice?
10021{_}1957{_}Are you going to Venice?
10021{_}1958{_}Delightful!
10021{_}1959{_}The Viennese are so charming, and the Austrian officers--Oh, you're going to Sicily first?
10021{_}1960{_}Far too hot.
10021{_}1961{_}Paul La France--the young singer, you know--told me that when he was in Sicily his voice completely altered; the heat quite affected the _veloute_ of his voice, as the French call it--and what a voice it is at its best!
10021{_}1962{_}It's not the _highest_ tenor, of course, but the medium is so wonderfully soft and well developed.
10021{_}1963{_}I don't say for a moment that he will ever be a Caruso, but as far as he goes--and he goes pretty far, mind--it's really wonderful.

10021{_}1971{_}Wagner's practically gone out (at least what _I_ call out), and I always said Debussy wouldn't last.

10021{_}1975{_}'I'm coming to see you so soon,' said Vincy.
10021{_}1976{_}CHAPTER XIV  The Letter  It was about a fortnight later.
10021{_}1977{_}Edith and Bruce, from different directions, arrived at the same moment at their door, and went up together in the lift.
10021{_}1978{_}On the little hall-table was a letter addressed to Edith.
10021{_}1979{_}She took it up rather quickly, and went into the drawing-room.
10021{_}1980{_}Bruce followed her.
10021{_}1981{_}'That a letter, Edith?'

10021{_}1983{_}'What _could_ I have supposed it was, Edith?
10021{_}1984{_}A plum pudding?'
10021{_}1985{_}He laughed very much.
10021{_}1986{_}'You are very humorous today, Bruce.'
10021{_}1987{_}She sat down with her hat, veil and gloves on, holding the letter.
10021{_}1988{_}She did not go to her room, because that would leave her no further retreat.
10021{_}1989{_}Bruce sat down exactly opposite to her, with his coat and gloves on.
10021{_}1990{_}He slowly drew off one glove, folded it carefully, and put it down.

10021{_}1995{_}She started.
10021{_}1996{_}'Oh yes!
10021{_}1997{_}I heard this time.'

10021{_}1999{_}I wish you'd go and see him.
10021{_}2000{_}I'll give you the address.'

10021{_}2002{_}I think I would go and have some lessons, if I were you, Bruce; the summer classes are just beginning.
10021{_}2003{_}They teach you to speak so clearly, to get your voice over the footlights, as it were.
10021{_}2004{_}I think all men require to study oratory and elocution.
10021{_}2005{_}It comes in so useful!'
10021{_}2006{_}Bruce lowered his voice almost to a whisper.

10021{_}2008{_}She nodded amiably in the manner of a person perfectly deaf, but who is pretending to hear.

10021{_}2031{_}'What letter?'
10021{_}2032{_}'Don't prevaricate with me.'
10021{_}2033{_}Bruce had now definitely lost his temper.
10021{_}2034{_}'I can stand anything except prevarication.
10021{_}2035{_}Anything in the world, but prevarication, I can endure, with patience.

10021{_}2037{_}As if you didn't know perfectly well there's only one letter I want to see.'

10021{_}2049{_}Edith quickly looked at the letter.
10021{_}2050{_}Until this moment she had had an unreasonable and nervous terror that Aylmer might have forgotten his intention of writing what he called officially, and might have written her what she now inwardly termed a lot of nonsense.
