10021{_}2057{_}What next!
10021{_}2058{_}I suppose the next thing you'll wish is to be a suffragette.'
10021{_}2059{_}'The question,' said Edith, in the most cool, high, irritating voice she could command, 'really, of votes for women hardly enters into our argument here.
10021{_}2060{_}As a matter of fact, I take no interest in any kind of politics, and, I may be entirely wrong, but if I were compelled to take sides on the subject, I should be an anti-suffragist.'
10021{_}2061{_}'Oh, you would, would you?
10021{_}2062{_}That's as well to know!
10021{_}2063{_}That's interesting.
10021{_}2064{_}Give me that letter.'
10021{_}2065{_}'Do you think you have the right to speak to me like that?'
10021{_}2066{_}'Edith,' he said rather pathetically, trying to control himself.
10021{_}2067{_}'I beg you, I _implore_ you to let me see the letter!
10021{_}2068{_}Hang it all!
10021{_}2069{_}You know perfectly well, old girl, how fond I am of you.
10021{_}2070{_}I may worry you a bit sometimes, but you know my heart's all right.'
10021{_}2071{_}'Of course, Bruce; I'm not finding fault with you.
10021{_}2072{_}I only want to read my own letter, that's all.'
10021{_}2073{_}'But if I let you out of this room without having shown it me, then if there's something you don't want me to see, you'll tear it up or chuck it in the fire.'
10021{_}2074{_}Edith was quite impressed at this flash of prophetic insight.
10021{_}2075{_}She admitted to herself he was right.
10021{_}2076{_}'It's entirely a matter of principle,' she said after another reassuring look at the envelope.
10021{_}2077{_}'It's only a matter of principle, dear, I'm twenty-eight years old, we've been married eight years; you leave the housekeeping, the whole ordering of the children's education, and heaps of other quite important things, entirely to me; in fact, you lead almost the life of a schoolboy, without any of the tiresome part, and with freedom, going to school in the day and amusing yourself in the evening, while everything disagreeable and important is thought of and seen to for you.
10021{_}2078{_}You only have the children with you when they amuse you.
10021{_}2079{_}I have all the responsibility; I have to be patient, thoughtful--in fact, you leave things to me more than most men do to their wives, Bruce.
10021{_}2080{_}You won't be bothered even to look at an account--to do a thing.
10021{_}2081{_}But I'm not complaining.'
10021{_}2082{_}'Oh, you're not!
10021{_}2083{_}It sounded a little like it.'
10021{_}2084{_}'But it isn't.
10021{_}2085{_}I don't _mind_ all this responsibility, but I ought, at least, to be allowed to read my letters.'
10021{_}2086{_}'Well, darling, you shall, as a rule.
10021{_}2087{_}Look here, old girl, you shall.
10021{_}2088{_}I promise you, faithfully, dear.
10021{_}2089{_}Oh, Edith, you're looking awfully pretty; I like that hat.
10021{_}2090{_}Look here, I promise you, dear, I'll _never_ ask you again, never as long as I live.
10021{_}2091{_}But I've a fancy to read this particular letter.
10021{_}2092{_}Why not just gratify it?
10021{_}2093{_}It's a very harmless whim.'
10021{_}2094{_}His tone suddenly changed.
10021{_}2095{_}'What do you suppose there's _in_ the damned letter?
10021{_}2096{_}Something you're jolly well anxious I shouldn't see.'
10021{_}2097{_}She made a step forward.
10021{_}2098{_}He rushed at her, snatched the letter out of her hand, and went to the window with it.
10021{_}2099{_}She went into her own room, shut the door, and threw herself on the bed, her whole frame shaking with suppressed laughter.
10021{_}2100{_}*       *       *       *       *  Bruce, alone, with trembling fingers tore open the envelope.
10021{_}2101{_}Never in his life had he been opposed by Edith before in this way.
10021{_}2102{_}He read these words in stereotyped writing:  _'Van will call on receipt of post-card.
10021{_}2103{_}The Lavender Laundry hopes that you will give them a trial, as their terms are extremely mod--'_  Bruce rushed to the door and called out:  'Edith!
10021{_}2104{_}Sorry!
10021{_}2105{_}Edie, I say, I'm sorry.
10021{_}2106{_}Come back.'
10021{_}2107{_}There was no answer.
10021{_}2108{_}He pushed the letter under the door of her room, and said through the keyhole:  'Edith, look here, I'm just going for a little walk.
10021{_}2109{_}I'll be back to dinner.
10021{_}2110{_}Don't be angry.'
10021{_}2111{_}Bruce brought her home a large bunch of Parma violets.
10021{_}2112{_}But neither of them ever referred to the question again, and for some time there was a little less of the refrain of 'Am I master in my own house, or am I not?'
10021{_}2113{_}The next morning, when a long letter came from Aylmer, from Spain, Edith read it at breakfast and Bruce didn't ask a single question.
10021{_}2114{_}However, she left it on his plate, as if by mistake.
10021{_}2115{_}He might just as well read it.
10021{_}2116{_}CHAPTER XV  Mavis Argles  Vincy had the reputation of spending his fortune with elaborate yet careful lavishness, buying nothing that he did not enjoy, and giving away everything he did not want.
10021{_}2117{_}At the same time his friends occasionally wondered on what he _did_ spend both his time and his money.
10021{_}2118{_}He was immensely popular, quite sought after socially; but he declined half his invitations and lived a rather quiet existence in the small flat, with its Oriental decorations and violent post-impressions and fierce Chinese weapons, high up in Victoria Street.
10021{_}2119{_}Vincy really concealed under an amiable and gentle exterior the kindest heart of any man in London.
10021{_}2120{_}There was 'more in him than met the eye,' as people say, and, frank and confidential as he was to his really intimate friends, at least one side of his life was lived in shadow.
10021{_}2121{_}It was his secret romance with a certain young girl artist, whom he saw rarely, for sufficient reasons.
10021{_}2122{_}He was not devoted to her in the way that he was to Edith, for whom he had the wholehearted enthusiasm of a loyal friend, and the idolising worship of a fanatic admirer.
10021{_}2123{_}It was perhaps Vincy's nature, a little, to sacrifice himself for anyone he was fond of.
10021{_}2124{_}He spent a great deal of time thinking out means of helping materially the young art-student, and always he succeeded in this object by his elaborate and tactful care.
10021{_}2125{_}For he knew she was very, very poor, and that her pride was of an old-fashioned order--she never said she was hard up, as every modern person does, whether rich or poor, but he knew that she really lacked what he considered very nearly--if not quite--the necessities of life.
10021{_}2126{_}Vincy's feeling for her was a curious one.
10021{_}2127{_}He had known her since she was sixteen (she was now twenty-four).
10021{_}2128{_}Yet he did not trust her, and she troubled him.
10021{_}2129{_}He had met her at a studio at a time when he had thought of studying art seriously.
10021{_}2130{_}Sometimes, something about her worried and wearied him, yet he couldn't do without her for long.
10021{_}2131{_}The fact that he knew he was of great help to her fascinated him; he often thought that if she had been rich and he poor he would never wish to see her again.
10021{_}2132{_}Certainly it was the touch of pathos in her life that held him; also, of course, she was pretty, with a pale thin face, deep blue eyes, and rich dark red frizzy hair that was always coming down--the untidy hair of the art-student.
10021{_}2133{_}He was very much afraid of compromising her, and _she_ was very much afraid of the elderly aunt with whom she lived.
10021{_}2134{_}She had no parents, which made her more pathetic, but no more free.
10021{_}2135{_}He could not go and see her, with any satisfaction to either of them, at _her_ home, though he did so occasionally.
10021{_}2136{_}This was why she first went to see him at his flat.
10021{_}2137{_}But these visits, as they were both placed, could, of course, happen rarely.
10021{_}2138{_}Mavis Argles--this was the girl's extraordinary name--had a curious fascination for him.
10021{_}2139{_}He was rather fond of her, yet the greatest wish he had in the world was to break it off.
10021{_}2140{_}When with her he felt himself to be at once a criminal and a benefactor, a sinner and a saint.
10021{_}2141{_}Theoretically, theatrically, and perhaps conventionally, his relations with her constituted him the villain of the piece.
10021{_}2142{_}Yet he behaved to her more like Don Quixote than Don Juan....        *       *       *       *       *  One afternoon about four o'clock--he was expecting her--Vincy had arranged an elaborate tea on his little green marble dining-table.
10021{_}2143{_}Everything was there that she liked.
10021{_}2144{_}She was particularly attached to scones; he also had cream-cakes, sandwiches, sweets, chocolate and strawberries.
10021{_}2145{_}As he heard the well-known slightly creaking step, his heart began to beat loudly--quick beats.
10021{_}2146{_}He changed colour, smiled, and nervously went to the door.
10021{_}2147{_}'Here you are, Mavis!'
10021{_}2148{_}He calmed her and himself by this banal welcome.
10021{_}2149{_}He made a movement to help her off with her coat, but she stopped him, and he didn't insist, guessing that she supposed her blouse to be unfit for publication.
10021{_}2150{_}She sat down on the sofa, and leaned back, looking at him with her pretty, weary, dreary, young, blue eyes.
10021{_}2151{_}'It seems such a long time since I saw you,' said Vincy.
10021{_}2152{_}'You're tired; I wish I had a lift.'
10021{_}2153{_}'I am tired,' she spoke in rather a hoarse voice always.
10021{_}2154{_}'And I ought not to stop long.'
10021{_}2155{_}'Oh, stay a minute longer, won't you?'
10021{_}2156{_}he asked.
10021{_}2157{_}'Well, I like that!
10021{_}2158{_}I've only just this moment arrived!'
10021{_}2159{_}'Oh, Mavis, don't say that!
10021{_}2160{_}Have some tea.'
10021{_}2161{_}He waited on her till she looked brighter.
10021{_}2162{_}'How is Aunt Jessie?'
10021{_}2163{_}'Aunt Jessie's been rather ill.'  'Still that nasty pain?'
10021{_}2164{_}asked Vincy.
10021{_}2165{_}She stared at him, then laughed.
10021{_}2166{_}'As if you remember anything about it.'
10021{_}2167{_}'Oh, Mavis!
10021{_}2168{_}I do remember it.
10021{_}2169{_}I remember what was the matter with her quite well.'
10021{_}2170{_}'I bet you don't.
10021{_}2171{_}What was it?'
10021{_}2172{_}she asked, with childish eagerness.
10021{_}2173{_}'It was that wind round the heart that she gets sometimes.
10021{_}2174{_}She told me about it.
10021{_}2175{_}Nothing seems to shift it, either.'
10021{_}2176{_}Mavis laughed--hoarse, childlike laughter that brought tears to her eyes.
10021{_}2177{_}'It's a shame to make fun of Aunt Jessie; she's a very, very good sort.'
10021{_}2178{_}'Oh, good gracious, Mavis, if it comes to sorts, I'm sure she's quite at the top of the tree.
10021{_}2179{_}But don't let's bother about her now.'
10021{_}2180{_}'What _do_ you want to bother about?'
10021{_}2181{_}'Couldn't you come out and dine with me, Mavis?
10021{_}2182{_}It would be a change'--he was going to say 'for you', but altered it--'for me.'
10021{_}2183{_}'Oh no, Vincy; you can't take me out to dinner.
10021{_}2184{_}I don't look up to the mark.'
10021{_}2185{_}She looked in a glass.
10021{_}2186{_}'My hat--it's a very good hat--it cost more than you'd think--but it shows signs of wear.'
10021{_}2187{_}'Oh, that reminds me,' began Vincy.
10021{_}2188{_}'What _do_ you think happened the other day?
10021{_}2189{_}A cousin of mine who was up in London a little while bought a hat--it didn't suit her, and she insisted on giving it to me!
10021{_}2190{_}She didn't know what to do to get rid of it!
10021{_}2191{_}I'd given her something or other, for her birthday, and _she_ declared she would give this to _me_ for _my_ birthday, and so--I've got it on my hands.'
10021{_}2192{_}'What a very queer thing!
10021{_}2193{_}It doesn't sound true.'
10021{_}2194{_}'No; does it?
10021{_}2195{_}Do have some more tea, Mavis darling.'
10021{_}2196{_}'No, thanks; I'll have another cake.'
10021{_}2197{_}'May I smoke?'
10021{_}2198{_}She laughed.
10021{_}2199{_}'Asking _me_!
10021{_}2200{_}You do what you like in your own house.'
10021{_}2201{_}'It's yours,' he answered, 'when you're here.
10021{_}2202{_}And when you're not, even more,' he added as an afterthought.
10021{_}2203{_}He struck a match; she laughed and said: 'I don't believe I understand you a bit.'
10021{_}2204{_}'Oh--I went to the play last night,' said Vincy.
10021{_}2205{_}'Oh, Mavis, it was such a wearing play.'
10021{_}2206{_}'All about nothing, I suppose?
10021{_}2207{_}They always are, now.'
10021{_}2208{_}'Oh no.
10021{_}2209{_}It was all about everything.
10021{_}2210{_}The people were _so_ clever; it was something cruel how clever they were.
10021{_}2211{_}One man _did_ lay down the law!
10021{_}2212{_}Oh, didn't he though!
10021{_}2213{_}I don't hold with being bullied and lectured from the stage, do you, Mavis?
10021{_}2214{_}It seems so unfair when you can't answer back.'
10021{_}2215{_}'Was it Bernard Shaw?'
10021{_}2216{_}she asked.
10021{_}2217{_}'No; it wasn't; not this time; it was someone else.
10021{_}2218{_}Oh, I do feel sometimes when I'm sitting in my stall, so good and quiet, holding my programme nicely and sitting up straight to the table, as it were, and then a fellow lets me have it, tells me where I'm wrong and all that; I _should_ like to stand up and give a back answer, wouldn't you?'
10021{_}2219{_}'No; I'd like to see _you_ do it!
10021{_}2220{_}Er--what colour is that hat that your cousin gave you?'
10021{_}2221{_}'Oh, colour?'
10021{_}2222{_}he said thoughtfully, smoking.
10021{_}2223{_}'Let me see--what colour was it?
10021{_}2224{_}It doesn't seem to me that it was any particular colour.
10021{_}2225{_}It was a very curious colour.
10021{_}2226{_}Sort of mole-colour.
10021{_}2227{_}Or was it cerise?
10021{_}2228{_}Or violet?...

10021{_}2253{_}'I mean what I told you--how my cousin, Cissie Cavanack,' he smiled a little as he invented this name, 'came up to town, chose the wrong hat, didn't know what to do with it--and, you know!'
10021{_}2254{_}'I could tell her all that, of course.'
10021{_}2255{_}'All right,' said Vincy, putting the other hat--the old one--in the box.'
10021{_}2256{_}Where shall we dine?'
10021{_}2257{_}'Oh, Vincy, I think you're very sweet to me, but how late dare I get back to Ravenscourt Park?'
10021{_}2258{_}'Why not miss the eight-five train?--then you'll catch the quarter to ten and get back at about eleven.'
10021{_}2259{_}'Which would you _rather_ I did?'
10021{_}2260{_}'Well, need you ask?'
10021{_}2261{_}'I don't know, Vincy.
10021{_}2262{_}I have a curious feeling sometimes.
10021{_}2263{_}I believe you're rather glad when I've gone--relieved!'
10021{_}2264{_}'Well, my dear,' he answered, 'look how you worry all the time!
10021{_}2265{_}If you'd only have what I call a quiet set-down and a chat, without being always on the fidget, always looking either at the glass or at the clock, one might _not_ have that feeling.'

10021{_}2267{_}'Oh, then you _are_ glad when I'm gone!'
10021{_}2268{_}She pouted.

10021{_}2271{_}'Oh, give over, Mavis, do give over!

10021{_}2273{_}Some days you go on something cruel, you do really.
10021{_}2274{_}Reely, I mean.
10021{_}2275{_}Now, cheer up and be jolly.

10021{_}2287{_}He smiled.

10021{_}2299{_}No price.

10021{_}2310{_}'I like your hair, Mavis.'
10021{_}2311{_}*       *       *       *       *  'It seems too awful I should have been with you such a long time this afternoon,' she exclaimed.
10021{_}2312{_}'It _isn't_ long.'
10021{_}2313{_}'And sometimes it seems so dreadful to think I can't be with you always.'
10021{_}2314{_}'Yes, doesn't it?
10021{_}2315{_}Mavis dear, will you do up your hair and come out to dinner?'
10021{_}2316{_}'Vincy dear, I think I'd better not, because of Aunt Jessie.'
10021{_}2317{_}'Oh, very well; all right.
10021{_}2318{_}Then you will another time?'
10021{_}2319{_}'Oh, you don't want me to stay?'

10021{_}2321{_}'No, next time--next Tuesday.'
10021{_}2322{_}'Very well, very well.'
10021{_}2323{_}He took a dark red carnation out of one of the vases and pinned it on to her coat.
10021{_}2324{_}'The next time I see you,' she said, 'I want to have a long, _long_ talk.'
10021{_}2325{_}'Oh yes; we must, mustn't we?'
10021{_}2326{_}He took her downstairs, put her into a cab.
10021{_}2327{_}It was half-past six.
10021{_}2328{_}He felt something false, worrying, unreliable and incalculable in Mavis.
10021{_}2329{_}She didn't seem real....
10021{_}2330{_}He wished she were fortunate and happy; but he wished even more that he were never going to see her again.
10021{_}2331{_}And still!...
10021{_}2332{_}He walked a little way, then got into a taxi and drove to see Edith.
10021{_}2333{_}When he was in this peculiar condition of mind--the odd mixture of self-reproach, satisfaction, amusement and boredom that he felt now --he always went to see Edith, throwing himself into the little affairs of her life as if he had nothing else on his mind.
10021{_}2334{_}He was a little anxious about Edith.
10021{_}2335{_}It seemed to him that since Aylmer had been away she had altered a little.
10021{_}2336{_}CHAPTER XVI  More of the Mitchells  Edith had become an immense favourite with the Mitchells.
10021{_}2337{_}They hardly ever had any entertainment without her.
10021{_}2338{_}Her success with their friends delighted Mrs Mitchell, who was not capable of commonplace feminine jealousy, and who regarded Edith as a find of her own.
10021{_}2339{_}She often reproached Winthrop, her husband, for having known Bruce eight years without discovering his charming wife.
10021{_}2340{_}One evening they had a particularly gay party.
10021{_}2341{_}Immediately after dinner Mitchell had insisted on dressing up, and was solemnly announced in his own house as Prince Gonoff, a Russian noble.
10021{_}2342{_}He had a mania for disguising himself.
10021{_}2343{_}He had once travelled five hundred miles under the name of Prince Gotoffski, in a fur coat, a foreign accent, a false moustache and a special saloon carriage.
10021{_}2344{_}Indeed, only his wife knew all the secrets of Mitchell's wild early career of unpractical jokes, to some of which he still clung.
10021{_}2345{_}When he was younger he had carried it pretty far.
10021{_}2346{_}She encouraged him, yet at the same time she acted as ballast, and was always explaining his jokes; sometimes she was in danger of explaining him entirely away.
10021{_}2347{_}She loved to tell of his earlier exploits.
10021{_}2348{_}How often, when younger, he had collected money for charities (particularly for the Deaf and Dumb Cats' League, in which he took special interest), by painting halves of salmon and ships on fire on the cold grey pavement!
10021{_}2349{_}Armed with an accordion, and masked to the eyes, he had appeared at Eastbourne, and also at the Henley Regatta, as a Mysterious Musician.
10021{_}2350{_}At the regatta he had been warned off the course, to his great pride and joy.
10021{_}2351{_}Mrs Mitchell assured Edith that his bath-chair race with a few choice spirits was still talked of at St Leonard's (bath-chairmen, of course, are put in the chairs, and you pull them along).
10021{_}2352{_}Mr Mitchell was beaten by a short head, but that, Mrs Mitchell declared, was really most unfair, because he was so handicapped--his man was much stouter than any of the others--and the race, by rights, should have been run again.
10021{_}2353{_}When he was at Oxford he had been well known for concealing under a slightly rowdy exterior the highest spirits of any of the undergraduates.
10021{_}2354{_}He was looked upon as the most fascinating of _farceurs_.
10021{_}2355{_}It seems that he had distinguished himself there less for writing Greek verse, though he was good at it, than for the wonderful variety of fireworks that he persistently used to let off under the dean's window.
10021{_}2356{_}It was this fancy of his that led, first, to his popularity, and afterwards to the unfortunate episode of his being sent down; soon after which he had married privately, chiefly in order to send his parents an announcement of his wedding in _The Morning Post_, as a surprise.
10021{_}2357{_}Some people had come in after dinner--for there was going to be a little _sauterie intime_, as Mrs Mitchell called it, speaking in an accent of her own, so appalling that, as Vincy observed, it made it sound quite improper.
10021{_}2358{_}Edith watched, intensely amused, as she saw that there were really one or two people present who, never having seen Mitchell before, naturally did not recognise him now, so that the disguise was considered a triumph.
10021{_}2359{_}There was something truly agreeable in the deference he was showing to a peculiarly yellow lady in red, adorned with ugly real lace, and beautiful false hair.
10021{_}2360{_}She was obviously delighted with the Russian prince.
10021{_}2361{_}'Winthrop is a wonderful man!'
10021{_}2362{_}said Mrs Mitchell to Edith, as she watched her husband proudly.
10021{_}2363{_}'Who would dream he was clean-shaven!
10021{_}2364{_}Look at that moustache!
10021{_}2365{_}Look at the wonderful way his coat doesn't fit; he's got just that Russian touch with his clothes; I don't know how he's done it, I'm sure.
10021{_}2366{_}How I wish dear Aylmer Ross was here; he _would_ appreciate it so much.'
10021{_}2367{_}'Yes, I wish he were,' said Edith.
10021{_}2368{_}'I can't think what he went away for.
10021{_}2369{_}I suppose he heard the East a-calling, and all that sort of thing.
10021{_}2370{_}The old wandering craving you read of came over him again, I suppose.
10021{_}2371{_}Well, let's hope he'll meet some charming girl and bring her back as his bride.
10021{_}2372{_}Where is he now, do you know, Mrs Ottley?'
10021{_}2373{_}'In Armenia, I fancy,' said Edith.
10021{_}2374{_}'Oh, well, we don't want him to bring home an Armenian, do we?
10021{_}2375{_}What colour are they?
10021{_}2376{_}Blue, or brown, or what?
10021{_}2377{_}I hope no-one will tell Lady Hartland that is my husband.
10021{_}2378{_}She'll expect to see Winthrop tonight; she never met him, you know; but he really ought to be introduced to her.
10021{_}2379{_}I think I shall tell him to go and undress, when they've had a little dancing and she's been down to supper.'
10021{_}2380{_}Lady Hartland was the yellow lady in red, who thought she was flirting with a fascinating Slav.
10021{_}2381{_}'She's a sort of celebrity,' continued Mrs Mitchell.
10021{_}2382{_}'She was an American once, and she married Sir Charles Hartland for her money.
10021{_}2383{_}I hate these interested marriages, don't you?--especially when they're international.
10021{_}2384{_}Sir Charles isn't here; he's such a sweet boy.
10021{_}2385{_}He's a friend of Mr Cricker; it's through Mr Cricker I know them, really.
10021{_}2386{_}Lady Everard has taken _such_ a fancy to young Cricker; she won't leave him alone.
10021{_}2387{_}After all he's _my_ friend, and as he's not musical I don't see that she has any special right to him; but he's there every Wednesday now, and does his dances on their Sunday evenings too.
10021{_}2388{_}He's got a new one--lovely, quite lovely--an imitation of Lydia Kyasht as a water-nymph.
10021{_}2389{_}I wanted him to do it here tonight, but Lady Everard has taken him to the opera.
10021{_}2390{_}Now, won't you dance?
10021{_}2391{_}Your husband promised he would.
10021{_}2392{_}You both look so young!'
10021{_}2393{_}Edith refused to dance.
10021{_}2394{_}She sat in a corner with Vincy and watched the dancers.
10021{_}2395{_}By special permission, as it was so _intime_, the Turkey Trot was allowed.
10021{_}2396{_}Bruce wanted to attempt it with Myra Mooney, but she was horrified, and insisted on dancing the 1880 _trois-temps_ to a jerky American two-step.
10021{_}2397{_}'Edith,' said Vincy; 'I think you're quieter than you used to be.
10021{_}2398{_}Sometimes you seem rather absent-minded.'
10021{_}2399{_}'Am I?
10021{_}2400{_}I'm sorry; there's nothing so tedious to other people.
10021{_}2401{_}Why do you think I'm more serious?'
10021{_}2402{_}'I think you miss Aylmer.'
10021{_}2403{_}'Yes, I do.
10021{_}2404{_}He gave a sort of meaning to everything.
10021{_}2405{_}He's always interesting.
10021{_}2406{_}And there's something about him--I don't know what it is.
10021{_}2407{_}Oh, don't be frightened, Vincy, I'm not going to use the word personality.
10021{_}2408{_}Isn't that one of the words that ought to be forbidden altogether?
10021{_}2409{_}In all novels and newspapers that poor, tired word is always cropping up.'
10021{_}2410{_}'Yes, that and magnetism, and temperament, and technique.
10021{_}2411{_}Let's cut out technique altogether.
10021{_}2412{_}Don't let there be any, that's the best way; then no-one can say anything about it.
10021{_}2413{_}I'm fed up with it.
10021{_}2414{_}Aren't you?'
10021{_}2415{_}'Oh, I don't agree with you at all.
10021{_}2416{_}I think there ought to be any amount of technique, and personality, and magnetism, and temperament.
10021{_}2417{_}I don't mind _how_ much technique there is, as long as nobody talks about it.
10021{_}2418{_}But neither of these expressions is quite so bad as that dreadful thing you always find in American books, and that lots of people have caught up--especially palmists and manicures--mentality.'
10021{_}2419{_}'Yes, mentality's very depressing,' said Vincy.
10021{_}2420{_}'I could get along nicely without it, I think....
10021{_}2421{_}I had a long letter from Aylmer today.
10021{_}2422{_}He seemed unhappy.'
10021{_}2423{_}'I had a few lines yesterday,' said Edith.
10021{_}2424{_}'He said he was having a very good time.
10021{_}2425{_}What did he say to you?'
10021{_}2426{_}'Oh, he wrote, frankly to _me_.'
10021{_}2427{_}'Bored, is he?'
10021{_}2428{_}'Miserable; enamoured of sorrow; got the hump; frightfully off colour; wants to come back to London.
10021{_}2429{_}He misses the Mitchells.
10021{_}2430{_}I suppose it's the Mitchells.'
10021{_}2431{_}Edith smiled and looked pleased.
10021{_}2432{_}'He asked me not to come here much.'
10021{_}2433{_}'Ah!
10021{_}2434{_}But he wouldn't want you to go anywhere.
10021{_}2435{_}That is so like Aylmer.
10021{_}2436{_}He's not jealous; of course.
10021{_}2437{_}How could he be?
10021{_}2438{_}It's only a little exclusiveness.... And how delightfully rare that is, Edith dear.
10021{_}2439{_}I admire him for it.
10021{_}2440{_}Most people now seem to treasure anything they value in proportion to the extent that it's followed about and surrounded by the vulgar public.
10021{_}2441{_}I sympathise with that feeling of wishing to keep--anything of that sort--to oneself.'
10021{_}2442{_}'You are more secretive than jealous, yourself.
10021{_}2443{_}But I have very much the same feeling,' Edith said.
10021{_}2444{_}'Many women I know think the ideal of happiness is to be in love with a great man, or to be the wife of a great public success; to share his triumph!
10021{_}2445{_}They forget you share the man as well!'
10021{_}2446{_}'I suppose the idea is that, after the publicity and the acclamation and the fame and the public glory and the shouting, you take the person home, and feel he is only yours, really.'
10021{_}2447{_}'But, can a famous person be only yours?
10021{_}2448{_}No.
10021{_}2449{_}I shouldn't like it.
10021{_}2450{_}It isn't that I don't _like_ cleverness and brilliance, but I don't care for the public glory.'
10021{_}2451{_}'I see; you don't mind how great a genius he is, as long as he isn't appreciated,' replied Vincy.
10021{_}2452{_}'Well, then, in heaven's name let us stick to our obscurities!'
10021{_}2453{_}CHAPTER XVII  The Agonies of Aylmer  In the fresh cheerfulness of the early morning, after sleep, with the hot June sun shining in at the window, Aylmer used to think he was better.
10021{_}2454{_}He would read his letters and papers, dress slowly, look out of the window at the crowds on the pavement--he had come back to Paris--feel the infectious cheeriness and sense of adventure of the city; then he would say to himself that his trip had been successful.
10021{_}2455{_}He _was_ better.
10021{_}2456{_}When he went out his heart began to sink a little already, but he fought it off; there would be a glimpse of an English face flashing past in a carriage--he thought of Edith, but he put it aside.
10021{_}2457{_}Then came lunch.
10021{_}2458{_}For some reason, immediately after lunch his malady--for, of course, such love is a malady--incongruously attacked him in an acute form.
10021{_}2459{_}'Why after lunch?'
10021{_}2460{_}he asked himself.
10021{_}2461{_}Could it be that only when he was absolutely rested, before he had had any sort of fatigue, that the deceptive improvement would show itself?
10021{_}2462{_}He felt a wondering humiliation at his own narrow grief.
10021{_}2463{_}However, this was the hour that it recurred; he didn't know why.
10021{_}2464{_}He had tried all sorts of physical cures--for there is no disguising the fact that such suffering is physical, and so why should the cure not be, also?
10021{_}2465{_}He had tried wine, no wine, exercise, distraction, everything--and especially a constant change of scene.
10021{_}2466{_}This last was the worst of all.
10021{_}2467{_}He felt so exiled in Sicily, and in Spain--so terribly far away--it was unbearable.
10021{_}2468{_}He was happier directly he got to Paris, because he seemed more in touch with England and her.
10021{_}2469{_}Yes; the pain had begun again....  Aylmer went and sat alone outside the cafe.
10021{_}2470{_}It was not his nature to dwell on his own sensations.
10021{_}2471{_}He would diagnose them quickly and acutely, and then throw them aside.
10021{_}2472{_}He was quickly bored with himself; he was no egotist.
10021{_}2473{_}But today, he thought, he _would_ analyse his state, to see what could be done.
10021{_}2474{_}Six weeks!
10021{_}2475{_}He had not seen her for six weeks.
10021{_}2476{_}The longing was no better.
10021{_}2477{_}The pain seemed to begin at his throat, pressing down gradually on the chest It was that feeling of oppression, he supposed, that makes one sigh; as though there were a weight on the heart.
10021{_}2478{_}And certain little memories made it acute; sudden flashing vivid recollection of that last drive was like a sharp jagged tear.
10021{_}2479{_}Had they ever been on nearer terms, and had she treated him badly, it would not have caused this slow and insidious suffering.
10021{_}2480{_}He was a man of spirit; he was proud and energetic; he would have thrown it off.
10021{_}2481{_}If he could have been angry with her, or despised her, he could have cured himself in time.
10021{_}2482{_}Instead of that, all the recollections were of an almost sickening sweetness; particularly that kiss on the day he went to see her.
10021{_}2483{_}And the other, the _second_, was also the last; so it had a greater bitterness.
10021{_}2484{_}'Rapture sharper than a sword,   Joy like o sudden spear.'
10021{_}2485{_}These words, casually read somewhere, came back to him whenever he remembered her!
10021{_}2486{_}Aylmer had read, heard of these obsessions, but never believed in them.
10021{_}2487{_}It was folly, madness!
10021{_}2488{_}He stood up, tossing his head as though to throw it off.
10021{_}2489{_}He went to fetch some friends, went with them to see pictures, to have tea, and to drive in the Bois, accepting also an invitation to dine with a man--a nice boy--a fellow who had been at Oxford with him, and was at the embassy here, a young attache.
10021{_}2490{_}He was quite nice: a little dull, and a little too fond of talking about his chief.
10021{_}2491{_}Aylmer got home at about half-past six to dress for dinner.
10021{_}2492{_}Then the torture began again.
10021{_}2493{_}It was always worse towards evening--an agony of longing, regret, fury, vague jealousy and desire.
10021{_}2494{_}He stood and looked out of the window again at the crowd, hurrying along now to their pleasures or their happy homes.
10021{_}2495{_}So many people in the world, like stars in the sky--why want the one star only?
10021{_}2496{_}Why cry for the moon?
10021{_}2497{_}He had no photograph of her, but he still thought she was like his mother's miniature, and often looked at it.
10021{_}2498{_}He wished he wasn't going to dine with that young man tonight.
10021{_}2499{_}Aylmer was the most genial and sociable of men; he usually disliked being alone; yet just now being with people bored him; it seemed an interruption.
10021{_}2500{_}He was going through a crisis.
10021{_}2501{_}Yes; he could not stand anyone this evening.
10021{_}2502{_}He rang the bell and sent a _petit bleu_ to say he was prevented from dining with his friend.
10021{_}2503{_}What a relief when he had sent this--now he could think of her alone in peace....  She had never asked him to go away.
10021{_}2504{_}It was his own idea.
10021{_}2505{_}He had come away to get over it.
10021{_}2506{_}Well, he hadn't got over it.
10021{_}2507{_}He was worse.
10021{_}2508{_}But it wasn't because he didn't see her; no, he didn't deceive himself.
10021{_}2509{_}The more he saw of her the worse he would be.
10021{_}2510{_}Not one man in a thousand was capable of feeling so intensely and deeply as Aylmer felt, and never in his life before had he felt anything like it.
10021{_}2511{_}And now it came on again with the ebb and flow of passion, like an illness.
10021{_}2512{_}Why was he so miserable--why would nothing else do?
10021{_}2513{_}He suddenly remembered with a smile that when he was five years old he had adored a certain nurse, and for some reason or other his mother sent her away.
10021{_}2514{_}He had cried and cried for her to come back.
10021{_}2515{_}He remembered even now how people had said: 'Oh, the child will soon forget.'
10021{_}2516{_}But he wore out their patience; he cried himself to sleep every night.
10021{_}2517{_}And his perseverance had at last been rewarded.
10021{_}2518{_}After six weeks the nurse came back.
10021{_}2519{_}His mother sent for her in despair at the boy's misery.
10021{_}2520{_}How well he remembered that evening and her plain brown face, with the twinkling eyes.
10021{_}2521{_}How he kissed his mother, and thanked her!
10021{_}2522{_}The nurse stayed till he went to school and then he soon forgot all about her.
10021{_}2523{_}Perhaps it was in his nature at rare intervals to want one particular person so terribly, to pine and die for someone!
10021{_}2524{_}That was a recollection of babyhood, and yet he remembered even now that obstinate, aching longing....
10021{_}2525{_}He suddenly felt angry, furious.
10021{_}2526{_}What was Edith doing now?
10021{_}2527{_}Saying good-night to Archie and Dilly?
10021{_}2528{_}They certainly did look, as she had said, heavenly angels in their night attire (he had been privileged to see them).
10021{_}2529{_}Then she was dressing for dinner and going out with Bruce.
10021{_}2530{_}Good heavens!
10021{_}2531{_}what noble action had Bruce ever done for _him_ that he should go away?
10021{_}2532{_}Why make such a sacrifice--for Bruce?
10021{_}2533{_}Perhaps, sometimes, she really missed him a little.
10021{_}2534{_}They had had great fun together; she looked upon him as a friend; not only that, but he knew that he amused her, that she liked him, thought him clever, and--admired him even.
10021{_}2535{_}But that was all.
10021{_}2536{_}Yet she _could_ have cared for him.
10021{_}2537{_}He knew that.
10021{_}2538{_}And not only in one way, but in every way.
10021{_}2539{_}They could have been comrades interested in the same things; they had the same sense of humour, much the same point of view.
10021{_}2540{_}She would have made him, probably, self-restrained and patient as she was, in certain things.
10021{_}2541{_}But, in others, wouldn't he have fired her with his own ideas and feelings, and violent passions and enthusiasms!
10021{_}2542{_}She was to be always with Bruce!
10021{_}2543{_}That was to be her life!--Bruce, who was almost indescribable because he was neither bad, nor stupid, nor bad-looking.
10021{_}2544{_}He had only one fault.
10021{_}2545{_}_'Il n'a qu'un defaut--il est impossible,'_ said Aylmer aloud to himself.
10021{_}2546{_}He took up a book--of course one of _her_ books, something she had lent him.
10021{_}2547{_}*       *       *       *       *  Now it was time to go out again--to dinner.
10021{_}2548{_}He couldn't; it was too much effort.
10021{_}2549{_}Tonight he would give way, and suffer grief and desire and longing like a physical pain.
10021{_}2550{_}He hadn't heard from her lately.
10021{_}2551{_}Suppose she should be ill?
10021{_}2552{_}Suppose she was forgetting him entirely?
10021{_}2553{_}Soon they would be going away to some summer place with the children.
10021{_}2554{_}He stamped his foot like an angry child as he imagined her in her thin summer clothes.
10021{_}2555{_}How people would admire her!
10021{_}2556{_}How young she would look!
10021{_}2557{_}Why couldn't he find some fault with her?--imagine her cold, priggish, dull, too cautious.
10021{_}2558{_}But he could only think of her as lovely, as beyond expression attractive, drawing him like a magnet, as marvellously kind, gentle, graceful, and clever.
10021{_}2559{_}He was obliged to use the stupid word clever, as there was no other.
10021{_}2560{_}He suddenly remembered her teeth when she smiled, and a certain slight wave in her thick hair that was a natural one.
10021{_}2561{_}It is really barely decent to write about poor Aylmer as he is alone, suffering, thinking himself unwatched.
10021{_}2562{_}He suddenly threw himself on his bed and gave way to a crisis of despair.
10021{_}2563{_}*       *       *       *       *  About an hour later, when the pain had somehow become stupefied, he lit a cigarette, ashamed of his emotion even to himself, and rang.
10021{_}2564{_}The servant brought him a letter--the English post.
10021{_}2565{_}He had thought so much of her, felt her so deeply the last few days that he fancied it must somehow have reached her.
10021{_}2566{_}He read:  'My Dear Aylmer,  'I'm glad you are in Paris; it seems nearer home.
10021{_}2567{_}Last night I went to the Mitchells' and Mr Mitchell disguised himself as a Russian Count.
10021{_}2568{_}Nobody worried about it, and then he went and undisguised himself again.
10021{_}2569{_}But Lady Hartland worried about it, and as she didn't know the Mitchells before, when he was introduced to her properly she begged him to give her the address of that charming Russian.
10021{_}2570{_}And Vincy was there, and darling Vincy told me you'd written him a letter saying you weren't so very happy.
10021{_}2571{_}And oh, Aylmer, I don't see the point of your waiting till September to come back.
10021{_}2572{_}Why don't you come _now_?
10021{_}2573{_}'We're going away for Archie's holidays.
10021{_}2574{_}Come back and see us and take Freddie with us somewhere in England.
10021{_}2575{_}You told me to ask you when I wanted you--ask you anything I wanted.
10021{_}2576{_}Well, I want to see you.
10021{_}2577{_}I miss you too much.
10021{_}2578{_}You arrived in Paris last night.
10021{_}2579{_}Let me knew when you can come.
10021{_}2580{_}I want you.
10021{_}2581{_}Edith.'
10021{_}2582{_}The bell was rung violently.
10021{_}2583{_}Orders were given, arrangements made, packing was done.
10021{_}2584{_}Aylmer was suddenly quite well, quite happy.
10021{_}2585{_}In a few hours he was in the midnight express due to arrive in London at six in the morning--happy beyond expression.
10021{_}2586{_}By ten o'clock in the morning he would hear her voice on the telephone.
10021{_}2587{_}He met a poor man just outside the hotel selling matches, in rags.
10021{_}2588{_}Aylmer gave him three hundred francs.
10021{_}2589{_}He pretended to himself that he didn't want any more French money.
10021{_}2590{_}He felt he wanted someone else to be happy too.
10021{_}2591{_}CHAPTER XVIII  A Contretemps  Edith did not know, herself, what had induced her to write that letter to Paris.
10021{_}2592{_}Some gradual obscure influence, in an impulsive moment of weakness, a conventional dread of Paris for one's idol.
10021{_}2593{_}Then, what Vincy told her had convinced her Aylmer was unhappy.
10021{_}2594{_}She thought that surely there might be some compromise; that matters could be adjusted.
10021{_}2595{_}Couldn't they go on seeing each other just as friends?
10021{_}2596{_}Surely both would be happier than separated?
10021{_}2597{_}For, yes--there was no doubt she missed him, and longed to see him.
10021{_}2598{_}Is there any woman in the world on whom a sincere declaration from a charming, interesting person doesn't make an impression, and particularly if that person goes away practically the next day, leaving a blank?
10021{_}2599{_}Edith had a high opinion of her own strength of will.
10021{_}2600{_}When she appeared weak it was on some subject about which she was indifferent.
10021{_}2601{_}She took a great pride in her own self-poise; her self-control, which was neither coldness nor density.
10021{_}2602{_}She had made up her mind to bear always with the little irritations Bruce caused her; to guide him in the right direction; keep her influence with him in order to be able to arrange everything about the children just as she wished.
10021{_}2603{_}The children were a deep and intense preoccupation.
10021{_}2604{_}To say she adored them is insufficient.
10021{_}2605{_}Archie she regarded almost as her greatest friend, Dilly as a pet; for both she had the strongest feeling that a mother could have.
10021{_}2606{_}And yet the fact remained that they did not nearly fill her life.
10021{_}2607{_}With Edith's intellect and temperament they could only fill a part.
10021{_}2608{_}Bending down to a lower stature of intelligence all day long would make one's head ache; standing on tiptoe and stretching up would do the same; one needs a contemporary and a comrade.
10021{_}2609{_}Perhaps till Edith met Aylmer she had not quite realised what such real comradeship might mean, coupled with another feeling--not the intellectual sympathy she had for Vincy, but something quite different.
10021{_}2610{_}When she recollected their last drive her heart beat quickly, and the little memories of the few weeks of their friend-ship gave her unwonted moments of sentiment.
10021{_}2611{_}Above all, it was a real, solid happiness--an uplifting pleasure, to believe he was utterly devoted to her.
10021{_}2612{_}And so, in a moment of depression, a feeling of the sense of the futility of her life, she had, perhaps a little wantonly, written to ask him to come back.
10021{_}2613{_}It is human to play with what one loves.
10021{_}2614{_}She thought she had a soft, tender admiration for him, that he had a charm for her; that she admired him.
10021{_}2615{_}But she had not the slightest idea that on her side there was anything that could disturb her in any way.
10021{_}2616{_}And so that his sentiment, which she had found to be rather infectious, should never carry her away, she meant only to see him now and then; to meet again and be friends.
10021{_}2617{_}As soon as she had written the letter and sent it she felt again a cheerful excitement.
10021{_}2618{_}She felt sure he would come in a day or two.
10021{_}2619{_}Aylmer arrived, as I have said, eight hours after he received the letter.
10021{_}2620{_}His first intention was to ring her up, or to speak to Bruce on the telephone.
10021{_}2621{_}But it so happened that it was engaged.
10021{_}2622{_}This decided him to have a short rest, and then go and surprise her with a visit.
10021{_}2623{_}He thought he would have lunch at one (he knew she always lunched with the children at this hour), and would call on her unexpectedly at two, before she would have time to go out.
10021{_}2624{_}They might have a long talk; he would give her the books and things he had bought for her, and he would have the pleasure of surprising her and seeing on her face that first look that no-one can disguise, the look of real welcome.
10021{_}2625{_}Merely to be back in the same town made him nearly wild with joy.
10021{_}2626{_}How jolly London looked at the beginning of July!
10021{_}2627{_}So gay, so full of life.
10021{_}2628{_}And then he read a letter in a writing he didn't know; it was from Mavis Argles, the friend of Vincy--the young art-student: Vincy had given her his address some time ago--asking him for some special privilege which he possessed, to see some of the Chinese pictures in the British Museum.
10021{_}2629{_}He was to oblige her with a letter to the museum.
10021{_}2630{_}She would call for it.
10021{_}2631{_}Vincy was away, and evidently she had by accident chosen the day of Aylmer's return without knowing anything of his absence.
10021{_}2632{_}She had never seen him in her life.
10021{_}2633{_}Aylmer was wandering about the half-dismantled house _desoeuvre_, with nothing to do, restlessly counting the minutes till two in the afternoon.
10021{_}2634{_}He remembered the very little that Vincy had told him of Mavis; how proud she was and how hard up.
10021{_}2635{_}He saw her through the window.
10021{_}2636{_}She looked pale and rather shabby.
10021{_}2637{_}He told the servant to show her in.
10021{_}2638{_}'I've just this moment got your letter, Miss Argles.
10021{_}2639{_}But, of course, I'm only too delighted.'
10021{_}2640{_}'Thank you.
10021{_}2641{_}Mr Vincy said you'd give me the letter.'
10021{_}2642{_}The girl sat down stiffly on the edge of a chair.
10021{_}2643{_}Vincy had said she was pretty.
10021{_}2644{_}Aylmer could not see it.
10021{_}2645{_}But he felt brimming over with sympathy and kindness for her--for everyone, in fact.
10021{_}2646{_}She wore a thin light grey cotton dress, and a small grey hat; her hair looked rich, red, and fluffy as ever; her face white and rather thin.
10021{_}2647{_}She looked about seventeen.
10021{_}2648{_}When she smiled she was pretty; she had a Rossetti mouth; that must have been what Vincy admired.
10021{_}2649{_}Aylmer had no idea that Vincy did more than admire her very mildly.
10021{_}2650{_}'Won't you let me take you there?'
10021{_}2651{_}suggested Aylmer suddenly.
10021{_}2652{_}He had nothing on earth to do, and thought it would fill up the time.
10021{_}2653{_}'Yes!
10021{_}2654{_}I'll drive you there and show you the pictures.
10021{_}2655{_}And then, wouldn't you come and have lunch?
10021{_}2656{_}I've got an appointment at two.'
10021{_}2657{_}She firmly declined lunch, but consented that he should drive her, and they went.
10021{_}2658{_}Aylmer talked with the eagerness produced by his restless excitement and she listened with interest, somewhat fascinated, as people always were, with his warmth and vitality.
10021{_}2659{_}As they were driving along Oxford Street Edith, walking with Archie, saw them clearly.
10021{_}2660{_}She had been taking him on some mission of clothes.
10021{_}2661{_}(For the children only she went into shops.)
10021{_}2662{_}He was talking with such animation that he did not see her, to a pale young girl with bright red hair.
10021{_}2663{_}Edith knew the girl by sight, knew perfectly well that she was Vincy's friend--there was a photograph of her at his rooms.
10021{_}2664{_}Aylmer did not see her.
10021{_}2665{_}After a start she kept it to herself.
10021{_}2666{_}She walked a few steps, then got into a cab.
10021{_}2667{_}She felt ill.
10021{_}2668{_}So Aylmer had never got her letter?
10021{_}2669{_}He had been in London without telling her.
10021{_}2670{_}He had forgotten her.
10021{_}2671{_}Perhaps he was deceiving her?
10021{_}2672{_}And he was making love obviously to that sickening, irritating red-haired fool (so Edith thought of her), Vincy's silly, affected art-student.
10021{_}2673{_}When Edith went home she had a bad quarter of an hour.
10021{_}2674{_}She never even asked herself what right she had to mind so much; she only knew it hurt.
10021{_}2675{_}A messenger boy at once, of course.
10021{_}2676{_}'Dear Mr Ross,  I saw you this morning.
10021{_}2677{_}I wrote you a line to Paris, not knowing you had returned.
10021{_}2678{_}When you get the note forwarded, will you do me the little favour to tear it up unopened?
10021{_}2679{_}I'm sure you will do this to please me.
10021{_}2680{_}'We are going away in a day or two, but I don't know where.
10021{_}2681{_}Please don't trouble to come and see me.
10021{_}2682{_}'Good-bye.
10021{_}2683{_}'EDITH OTTLEY.'
10021{_}2684{_}Aylmer left Miss Argles at the British Museum.
10021{_}2685{_}When he went back, he found this letter.
10021{_}2686{_}CHAPTER XIX  An Extraordinary Afternoon  Aylmer guessed at once she had seen him driving.
10021{_}2687{_}Being a man of sense, and not an impossible hero in a feuilleton, instead of going away again and leaving the misunderstanding to ripen, he went to the telephone, endeavoured to get on, and to explain, in few words, what had obviously happened.
10021{_}2688{_}To follow the explanation by an immediate visit was his plan.
10021{_}2689{_}Though, of course, slightly irritated that she had seen him under circumstances conveying a false impression, on the other hand he was delighted at the pique her letter showed, especially coming immediately after the almost tender letter in Paris.
10021{_}2690{_}He rang and rang (and used language), and after much difficulty getting an answer he asked, '_Why he could not get on_' a pathetic question asked plaintively by many people (not only on the telephone).
10021{_}2691{_}'The line is out of order.'
10021{_}2692{_}In about twenty minutes he was at her door.
10021{_}2693{_}The lift seemed to him preternaturally slow.
10021{_}2694{_}'Mrs Ottley?'
10021{_}2695{_}'Mrs Ottley is not at home, sir.'
10021{_}2696{_}At his blank expression the servant, who knew him, and of course liked him, as they always did, offered the further information that Mrs Ottley had gone out for the whole afternoon.
10021{_}2697{_}'Are the children at home, or out with Miss Townsend?'
10021{_}2698{_}'The children are out, sir, but not with Miss Townsend.
10021{_}2699{_}They are spending the day with their grandmother.'
10021{_}2700{_}'Oh!
10021{_}2701{_}Do you happen to know if Mr and Mrs Ottley will be at home to dinner?'
10021{_}2702{_}'I've heard nothing to the contrary, sir.'
10021{_}2703{_}'May I come in and write a note?'
10021{_}2704{_}He went into the little drawing-room.
10021{_}2705{_}It was intensely associated with her.
10021{_}2706{_}He felt a little emu....
10021{_}2707{_}There was the writing-table, there the bookcase, the few chairs, the grey walls; some pale roses fading in a pewter vase....
10021{_}2708{_}The restfulness of the surroundings filled him, and feeling happier he wrote on the grey notepaper:  'DEAR MRS OTTLEY,  I arrived early this morning.
10021{_}2709{_}I started, in fact, from Paris immediately after receiving a few lines you very kindly sent me there.
10021{_}2710{_}I'm so disappointed not to see you.
10021{_}2711{_}Unless I hear to the contrary--and even if I do, I think!--I propose to come round this evening about nine, and tell you and Bruce all about my travels.
10021{_}2712{_}'Excuse my country manners in thus inviting myself.
10021{_}2713{_}But I know you will say no if you don't want me.
10021{_}2714{_}And in that case I shall have to come another time, very soon, instead, as I really must see you and show you something I've got for Archie.

10021{_}2716{_}He meant to go in the evening, even if she put him off again; and, if they were out, to wait until they returned, pretending he had not heard from her again.
10021{_}2717{_}He was no better.
10021{_}2718{_}He had been away six weeks and was rather more in love than ever.
10021{_}2719{_}He would only see her--she _did_ want to see him before they all separated for the summer!
10021{_}2720{_}He could not think further than of the immediate future; he would see her; they could make plans afterwards.
10021{_}2721{_}Of course, her letter was simply pique!
10021{_}2722{_}She had given herself away--twice--once in the angry letter, also in the previous one to Paris.
10021{_}2723{_}Where was she now?
10021{_}2724{_}What did it mean?
10021{_}2725{_}Why did she go out for the whole afternoon?
10021{_}2726{_}Where was she?
10021{_}2727{_}*       *       *       *       *  After Edith had written and sent her letter to Aylmer in the morning, Mrs Ottley the elder came to fetch the children to dine, and Edith told Miss Townsend to go for the afternoon.
10021{_}2728{_}She was glad she would be absolutely alone.
10021{_}2729{_}'Aren't you very well, dear Mrs Ottley?'
10021{_}2730{_}asked this young lady, in her sweet, sympathetic way.
10021{_}2731{_}Edith was fond of her, and, by implication only, occasionally confided in her on other subjects than the children.
10021{_}2732{_}Today, however, Edith answered that she was _very_ well _indeed_, but was going to see about things before they went away.
10021{_}2733{_}'I don't know how we shall manage without you for the holidays, Miss Townsend.
10021{_}2734{_}I think you had better come with us for the first fortnight, if you don't mind much.'
10021{_}2735{_}Miss Townsend said she would do whatever Edith liked.
10021{_}2736{_}She could easily arrange to go with them at once.
10021{_}2737{_}This was a relief, for just at this moment Edith felt as if even the children would be a burden.
10021{_}2738{_}Sweet, gentle Miss Townsend went away.
10021{_}2739{_}She was dressed rather like herself, Edith observed; she imitated Edith.
10021{_}2740{_}She had the soft, graceful manner and sweet voice of her employer.
10021{_}2741{_}She was slim and had a pretty figure, but was entirely without Edith's charm or beauty.
10021{_}2742{_}Vaguely Edith wondered if she would ever have a love affair, ever marry.
10021{_}2743{_}She hoped so, but (selfishly) not till Archie went to Eton.
10021{_}2744{_}Then she found herself looking at her lonely lunch; she tried to eat, gave it up, asked for a cup of tea.
10021{_}2745{_}At last, she could bear the flat no longer.
10021{_}2746{_}It was a glorious day, very hot, Edith felt peculiar.
10021{_}2747{_}She thought that if she spent all the afternoon out and alone, it would comfort her, and she would think it out.
10021{_}2748{_}Trees and sky and sun had always a soothing effect on her.
10021{_}2749{_}She went out, walked a little, felt worried by the crowd of shoppers swarming to Sloane Street and the Brompton Road, got into a taxi and drove to the gate of Kensington Gardens, opposite Kensington Gore.
10021{_}2750{_}Here she soon found a seat.
10021{_}2751{_}At this time of the day the gardens were rather unoccupied, and in the burning July afternoon she felt almost as if in the country.
10021{_}2752{_}She took off her gloves--a gesture habitual with her whenever possible.
10021{_}2753{_}She looked utterly restful.
10021{_}2754{_}She had nothing in her hands, for she never carried either a parasol or a bag, nor even in winter a muff or in the evening a fan.
10021{_}2755{_}All these little accessories seemed unnecessary to her.
10021{_}2756{_}She liked to simplify.
10021{_}2757{_}She hated fuss, anything worrying, agitating.
10021{_}2758{_}... And now she felt deeply miserable, perturbed and agitated.
10021{_}2759{_}What a punishment for giving way to that half-coquettish, half self-indulgent impulse that had made her write to Paris!
10021{_}2760{_}She had begged him to come back; while, really, he was here, and had not even let her know.
10021{_}2761{_}She had never liked what she had heard of Mavis Argles, but had vaguely pitied her, wondering what Vincy saw in her, and wishing to believe the best.
10021{_}2762{_}Now, she assumed the worst!
10021{_}2763{_}As soon as Vincy had gone out of town--he was staying in Surrey with some of his relatives--she, the minx, began flirting or carrying on with Aylmer.
10021{_}2764{_}How far had it gone?
10021{_}2765{_}she wondered jealously.
10021{_}2766{_}She did not believe Aylmer's love-making to be harmless.
10021{_}2767{_}He was so easily carried away.
10021{_}2768{_}His feelings were impulsive.
10021{_}2769{_}Yet it was only a very short time since Vincy had told her of Aylmer's miserable letter.
10021{_}2770{_}Edith was not interested in herself, and seldom thought much of her own feelings, but she hated self-deception; and now she faced facts.
10021{_}2771{_}She adored Aylmer!
10021{_}2772{_}It had been purely jealousy that made her write to Paris so touchingly, asking him to come back--vague fears that, if he were so depressed in Spain, perhaps he might try by amusements to forget her in Paris.
10021{_}2773{_}He had once said to her that, of all places, he thought Paris the least attractive for a romance, because it was all so obvious, so prepared, so professional.
10021{_}2774{_}He liked the unexpected, the veiled and somewhat more hypocritical atmosphere, and in the fogs of London, he had said, were more romantic mysteries than in any other city.
10021{_}2775{_}Still, she had feared.
10021{_}2776{_}And besides she longed to see him.
10021{_}2777{_}So she had unbent and thought herself soon after somewhat reckless; it was a little wanton and unfair to bring him back.
10021{_}2778{_}But she was not a saint; she was a woman; and sometimes Bruce was trying....  Edith belonged to the superior class of human being whom jealousy chills and cures, and does not stimulate to further efforts.
10021{_}2779{_}It was not in her to go in for competition.
10021{_}2780{_}The moment she believed someone else took her place she relaxed her hold.
10021{_}2781{_}This is the finer temperament, but it suffers most.
10021{_}2782{_}She would not try to take Aylmer away.
10021{_}2783{_}Let him remain with his red-haired Miss Argles!
10021{_}2784{_}He might even marry her.
10021{_}2785{_}He deserved it.
10021{_}2786{_}She meant to tell Vincy, of course.
10021{_}2787{_}Poor Vincy, _he_ didn't know of the treachery.
10021{_}2788{_}Now she must devote herself to the children, and be good and kind to Bruce.
10021{_}2789{_}At least, Bruce was _true_ to her in his way.
10021{_}2790{_}He had been in love when they married, but Edith shrewdly suspected he was not capable of very much more than a weak rather fatuous sentiment for any woman.
10021{_}2791{_}And anyone but herself would have lost him many years ago, would very likely have given him up.
10021{_}2792{_}But she had kept it all together, had really helped him, and was touched when she remembered that jealous scene he made about the letter.
10021{_}2793{_}The letter she wouldn't at first let him see.
10021{_}2794{_}Poor Bruce!
10021{_}2795{_}Well, they were linked together.
10021{_}2796{_}There were Archie, the angel, and Dilly, the pet.... She was twenty-eight and Aylmer forty.
10021{_}2797{_}He ought not to hold so strong a position in her mind.
10021{_}2798{_}But he did.
10021{_}2799{_}Yes, she was in love with him in a way--it was a mania, an obsession.
10021{_}2800{_}But she would now soon wrestle with it and conquer it.
10021{_}2801{_}The great charm had been his exclusive devotion--but also his appearance, his figure, his voice.
10021{_}2802{_}He looked sunburnt and handsome.
10021{_}2803{_}He was laughing as he talked to the miserable creature (so Edith called her in her own mind).
10021{_}2804{_}Then Edith had a reaction.
10021{_}2805{_}She would cure herself today!
10021{_}2806{_}No more flirtation, no more amitie amoureuse.
10021{_}2807{_}They were going away.
10021{_}2808{_}The children, darlings, how they loved her!
10021{_}2809{_}And Bruce.
10021{_}2810{_}She was reminding herself she must be gentle, good, to Bruce.
10021{_}2811{_}He had at least never deceived her!

10021{_}2813{_}It was about five o'clock now.
10021{_}2814{_}As she walked, she thought how fortunate she was in Miss Townsend; what a nice girl she was, what a good friend to her and the children.
10021{_}2815{_}She had a sort of intuition that made her always have the right word, the right manner.
10021{_}2816{_}She had seemed a little odd lately, but she was quite pleased to come with them to the country.
10021{_}2817{_}What made her think of Miss Townsend?
10021{_}2818{_}Some way off was a girl, with her back to Edith, walking with a man.
10021{_}2819{_}Her figure was like Miss Townsend's, and she wore a dress like the one copied from Edith's.
10021{_}2820{_}Edith walked more quickly, it was the retired part of the gardens on the way towards the Bayswater Road.
10021{_}2821{_}The two figures turned down a flowery path....
10021{_}2822{_}It was Miss Townsend!
10021{_}2823{_}She had turned her face.
10021{_}2824{_}Edith was surprised, was interested, and walked on a few steps.
10021{_}2825{_}She had not seen the man clearly.
10021{_}2826{_}Then they both sat down on a seat.
10021{_}2827{_}He took her hand.
10021{_}2828{_}She left it in his.
10021{_}2829{_}There was something familiar in his figure and clothes, and Edith saw his face.
10021{_}2830{_}Yes, it was Bruce.
10021{_}2831{_}Edith turned round and went home.
10021{_}2832{_}CHAPTER XX  Journeys End  So that was how Bruce behaved to her!
10021{_}2833{_}The deceit of both of them hurt her immensely.
10021{_}2834{_}But she pulled herself together.
10021{_}2835{_}It was a case for action.
10021{_}2836{_}She felt a bitter, amused contempt, but she felt it half-urgent _not_ to do anything that would lead to a life of miserable bickering and mutual harm.
10021{_}2837{_}It must be stopped.
10021{_}2838{_}And without making Bruce hate her.
10021{_}2839{_}She wrote the second note of this strange day and sent it by a messenger.

10021{_}2884{_}She had not shown him Aylmer's letter.

10021{_}2891{_}Bruce had tears in his eyes as he took it.

10021{_}2894{_}And look here, Bruce, leave it to me to tell the children.
10021{_}2895{_}They'll forget after the holidays.
10021{_}2896{_}Archie must not be upset.'

10021{_}2899{_}Wasn't it natural?
10021{_}2900{_}She wanted to hear no more about it.
10021{_}2901{_}There was a ring.
10021{_}2902{_}It was nine o'clock.
10021{_}2903{_}It was Aylmer's voice.
10021{_}2904{_}CHAPTER XXI  The Great Exception  The absurdly simple explanation, made almost in dumb show, by action rather than in dialogue, was soon given.
10021{_}2905{_}He was surprised, simply enchanted, at the entire frankness of her recognition; she acknowledged openly that it mattered to her tremendously whether or not he was on intimate terms or flirting with little Miss Argles, or with little Miss anybody.
10021{_}2906{_}He was not even to look at any woman except herself, that was arranged between them now and understood.
10021{_}2907{_}They were side by side, with hands clasped as a matter of course, things taken for granted that he formerly never dreamt of.
10021{_}2908{_}The signs of emotion in her face he attributed of course to the morning's contretemps, knowing nothing of the other trouble.
10021{_}2909{_}'It's heavenly being here again.
10021{_}2910{_}You're prettier than ever, Edith; sweeter than ever.
10021{_}2911{_}What a time I had away.
10021{_}2912{_}It got worse and worse.'
10021{_}2913{_}'Dear Aylmer!'
10021{_}2914{_}'You're far too good and kind to me.
10021{_}2915{_}But I _have_ suffered--awfully.'
10021{_}2916{_}'So have I, since this morning.
10021{_}2917{_}I felt--'  'What did you feel?
10021{_}2918{_}Tell me!'
10021{_}2919{_}'Must I?'
10021{_}2920{_}'Yes!'
10021{_}2921{_}'I felt, when I saw you with her, as if I hadn't got a friend in the world.
10021{_}2922{_}I felt quite alone.
10021{_}2923{_}I felt as if the ground were going to open and swallow me up.
10021{_}2924{_}I relied on you so much, far more than I knew!

10021{_}3000{_}There is, of course, no joy so great as the cessation of pain; in fact all joy, active or passive, is the cessation of some pain, since it must be the satisfaction of a longing, even perhaps an unconscious longing.
10021{_}3001{_}A desire is a sort of pain, even with hope, without it is despair.
10021{_}3002{_}When, for example, one takes artistic pleasure in looking at something beautiful, that is a cessation of the pain of having been deprived of it until then, since what one enjoys one must have longed for even without knowing it.

10021{_}3024{_}'I have a lot of worries.'
10021{_}3025{_}'I never heard you say that before.

10021{_}3028{_}'No; I shall give nothing more to you.

10021{_}3032{_}She now felt sorry for Bruce, utterly and completely indifferent about 'the Townsend case', as she already humorously called it to herself.
10021{_}3033{_}But, she thought, she _must_ be strong!
10021{_}3034{_}She was not prepared to lose her dignity, nor to allow the children to be educated by a woman whose faith at least with them and in their home was unreliable; their surroundings must be crystal-clear.
10021{_}3035{_}It would make a certain difference to them, she thought.
10021{_}3036{_}How could it not?
10021{_}3037{_}There were so many little ways in which she might spoil them or tease them, scamp things, or rush them, or be nicer to one of them, or less nice, if she had any sort of concealed relation with their father.
10021{_}3038{_}And as she had been treated absolutely as a confidante by Edith, the girl had certainly shown herself treacherous, and rather too clearly capable of dissimulation.
10021{_}3039{_}Edith thought this must have a bad effect on the children.
10021{_}3040{_}Edith was essentially a very feminine woman though she had a mental attitude rightly held to be more characteristic of men.
10021{_}3041{_}Being so feminine, so enraged under her calm and ease, she was, of course, not completely consistent.
10021{_}3042{_}She was still angry, and very scornful of Miss Townsend.
10021{_}3043{_}She was hurt with her; she felt a friend had played her false--a friend, too, in the position of deepest trust, of grave responsibility.
10021{_}3044{_}Miss Townsend knew perfectly well what the children were to Edith, and, for all she knew, there was no-one in Edith's life except Bruce; so that it was rather cruel.
10021{_}3045{_}Edith intended to keep up her dignity so absolutely that Miss Townsend could never see her again, that she could never speak to Edith on the subject.
10021{_}3046{_}She wished also, _very_ much, that Bruce should never see her again, but didn't know how to encompass this.
10021{_}3047{_}She must find a way.
10021{_}3048{_}On the other hand, after the first shock and disgust at seeing him, Edith's anger with Bruce himself had entirely passed.
10021{_}3049{_}Had she not known, for years, that he was a little weak, a little fatuous?
10021{_}3050{_}He was just as good a sort now as he had ever been, and as she was not blinded by the resentment and fury of the real jealousy of passion, Edith saw clearly, and knew that Bruce cared far more for _her_ than for anybody else; that in so far as he could love anybody he loved her in his way.
10021{_}3051{_}And she wanted to keep the whole thing together on account of Archie, and for Dilly's sake.
10021{_}3052{_}She must be so kind, yet so strong that Bruce would be at once grateful for her forbearance and afraid to take advantage of it.
10021{_}3053{_}Rather a difficult undertaking!...
10021{_}3054{_}And she had seen Aylmer again!
10021{_}3055{_}There was nothing in it about Miss Argles.
10021{_}3056{_}What happiness!
10021{_}3057{_}She ought to have trusted him.
10021{_}3058{_}He cared for her.
10021{_}3059{_}He loved her.
10021{_}3060{_}His sentiment was worth having.
10021{_}3061{_}And she cared for him too; how much she didn't quite know.
10021{_}3062{_}She admired him; he fascinated her, and she also felt a deep gratitude because he gave her just the sort of passionate worship that she must have always unconsciously craved for.
10021{_}3063{_}Certainly the two little events of today had drawn her nearer to him.
10021{_}3064{_}She had been far less reserved that evening.
10021{_}3065{_}She closed her eyes and smiled to herself.
10021{_}3066{_}But this mustn't happen again.
10021{_}3067{_}With a strong effort of self-coercion she banished all delightful recollections as she heard Bruce come up in the lift.
10021{_}3068{_}He came in with a slightly shy, uncomfortable manner.
10021{_}3069{_}Again, she felt sorry for him.

10021{_}3073{_}Then he said:  'I've just been down to the club.
10021{_}3074{_}What have you been doing?'
10021{_}3075{_}'Aylmer's been here.'
10021{_}3076{_}'Didn't know he was in town.'

10021{_}3080{_}'Yes.'

10021{_}3082{_}'Edith!'

10021{_}3100{_}'Edith, you're a brick.

10021{_}3102{_}She looked down and away without answering.

10021{_}3105{_}'What?

10021{_}3113{_}He didn't answer.

10021{_}3115{_}Edith thought one might be unfaithful without being disloyal, and she believed Bruce now.
10021{_}3116{_}She was too sensible to ask him never to write a line, never to telephone, never to do anything else; besides, it was beneath her dignity to go into these details, and common-sense told her that one or the other must write or communicate if the thing was to be stopped.
10021{_}3117{_}If Miss Townsend wrote to him to the club, he would have to answer.
10021{_}3118{_}Bruce meant not to see her again, and that was enough.
10021{_}3119{_}'Then you're not cross, Edith--not depressed?'
10021{_}3120{_}She gave her sweetest smile.
10021{_}3121{_}She looked brilliantly happy and particularly pretty.
10021{_}3122{_}'Edith!'

10021{_}3124{_}She moved away.

10021{_}3126{_}'Yes, I _entirely_ forgive you.
10021{_}3127{_}The incident is closed.'

10021{_}3129{_}'Absolutely.
10021{_}3130{_}And I've had a tiring day and I'm going to sleep.
10021{_}3131{_}Good night.'
10021{_}3132{_}With a kind little nod she left him standing in the middle of the room with that air of stupid distinction that he generally assumed when in a lift with other people, and that came to his rescue at awkward moments--a dull, aloof, rather haughty expression.
10021{_}3133{_}But it was no use to him now.
10021{_}3134{_}He had considerable difficulty in refraining from venting his temper on the poor, dumb furniture; in fact, he did give a kick to a pretty little writing-table.
10021{_}3135{_}It made no sound, but its curved shoulder looked resentful.
10021{_}3136{_}'What a day!'
10021{_}3137{_}said Bruce to himself.
10021{_}3138{_}He went to his room, pouting like Archie.
10021{_}3139{_}But he knew he had got off cheaply.
10021{_}3140{_}CHAPTER XXII  Another Side of Bruce  Ever since his earliest youth, Bruce had always had, at intervals, some vague, vain, half-hearted entanglement with a woman.
10021{_}3141{_}The slightest interest, practically even common civility, shown him by anyone of the feminine sex between the ages of sixteen and sixty, flattered his vanity to such an extraordinary extent that he immediately thought these ladies were in love with him, and it didn't take much more for him to be in love with them.
10021{_}3142{_}And yet he didn't really care for women.
10021{_}3143{_}With regard to them his point of view was entirely that of vanity, and in fact he only liked both men or women who made up to him, or who gave him the impression that they did.
10021{_}3144{_}Edith was really the only woman for whom his weak and flickering passion had lingered at all long; and in addition to that (the first glamour of which had faded) she had a real hold over him.
10021{_}3145{_}He felt for her the most genuine fondness of which he was capable, besides trust and a certain admiration.
10021{_}3146{_}A sort of respect underlay all his patronising good-nature or caprices with her.
10021{_}3147{_}But still he had got into the habit of some feeble flirtation, a little affair, and at first he missed it very much.
10021{_}3148{_}He didn't care a straw for Miss Townsend; he never had.
10021{_}3149{_}He thought her plain and tedious; she bored him more than any woman he had ever met, and yet he had slipped into a silly sort of intrigue, beginning by a few words of pity or sympathy to her, and by the idea that she looked up to him in admiration.
10021{_}3150{_}He was very much ashamed of it and of the circumstances; he was not proud of his conquest with her, as he generally was.
10021{_}3151{_}He felt that on account of the children, and altogether, he had been playing it a bit low down.
10021{_}3152{_}He was not incapable, either, of appreciating Edith's attitude.
10021{_}3153{_}She had never cross-questioned him, never asked him for a single detail, never laboured the subject, nor driven the point home, nor condescended even to try to find out how far things had really gone.
10021{_}3154{_}She hadn't even told him how she knew; he was ashamed to ask.
10021{_}3155{_}And, after that promise of forgiveness, she never referred to it; there was never the slightest innuendo, teasing, reproach.
10021{_}3156{_}Yes, by Jove!
10021{_}3157{_}Edith was wonderful!
10021{_}3158{_}And so Bruce meant to play the game too.
10021{_}3159{_}For several days he asked the porter at the club if there were any letters, receiving the usual reply, 'None, sir.'
10021{_}3160{_}The third day he received the following note, and took it to read with enjoyment of the secrecy combined with a sort of self-important shame.
10021{_}3161{_}Until now he hadn't communicated with her:--  'Dear Mr Ottley,  Of course you know I'm not returning to the children after the holidays, nor am I going with you to Westgate.
10021{_}3162{_}I'm very unhappy, for I fear I have offended Mrs Ottley.
10021{_}3163{_}She has always been very kind to me till now; but I shall let the matter rest.
10021{_}3164{_}Under the circumstances I suppose I shall not see you any more.
10021{_}3165{_}May I ask that you should not call or write.
10021{_}3166{_}I and mother are going to spend the summer at Bexhill with some friends.
10021{_}3167{_}Our address will be Sandringham, Seaview Road, Bexhill, if you like to write just one line to say good-bye.
10021{_}3168{_}I fear I have been rather to blame in seeing you without Mrs Ottley's knowledge, but you know how one's feelings sometimes lead one to do what one knows one ought not to ...'  'Sandringham, indeed!
10021{_}3169{_}Some boarding house, I suppose,' said Bruce to himself.
10021{_}3170{_}'What a lot of 'ones'!...
10021{_}3171{_}Fine grammar for a governess.'
10021{_}3172{_}'... Wishing you every happiness (I _shall_ miss the children!).
10021{_}3173{_}Yours sincerely,  Margaret Townsend  '_P.S._--I shall never forget how happy I was with you and Mrs Ottley.'
10021{_}3174{_}Bruce's expression as he read the last line was rather funny.
10021{_}3175{_}'She's a silly little fool, and I shan't answer,' he reflected.
10021{_}3176{_}Re-reading the letter, he found it more unsatisfactory still, and destroyed it.
10021{_}3177{_}The thought of Miss Townsend bored him unutterably; and indeed he was incapable of caring for any woman (however feebly) for more than two or three weeks.
10021{_}3178{_}He was particularly fickle, vague, and scrappy in his emotions.
10021{_}3179{_}Edith was the only woman for whom even a little affection could last, and he would have long tired of her but for her exceptional character and the extraordinary trouble and tact she used with him.
10021{_}3180{_}He didn't appreciate her fine shades, he was not in love with her, didn't value her as another man might have done.
10021{_}3181{_}But he was always coming back to a certain steady, renewed feeling of tenderness for her.
10021{_}3182{_}With the curious blindness common to all married people (and indeed to any people who live together), clever Edith had been entirely taken in, in a certain sense; she had always felt (until the 'Townsend case') half disdainfully but satisfactorily certain of Bruce's fidelity.
10021{_}3183{_}She knew that he had little sham flirtations, but she had never imagined his going anywhere near an intrigue.
10021{_}3184{_}She saw now that in that she had been duped, and that if he didn't do more it was not from loyalty to her.
10021{_}3185{_}Still, she now felt convinced that it wouldn't occur again.
10021{_}3186{_}She had treated him well; she had spared him in the matter.
10021{_}3187{_}He was a little grateful, and she believed he would be straight now, though her opinion of him had rather gone down.
10021{_}3188{_}Edith always felt that she must go to the very extreme of loyalty to anyone who was faithful to her; she valued fidelity so deeply, and now this feeling was naturally relaxed a little.
10021{_}3189{_}She hadn't the slightest desire for revenge, but she felt she had a slightly freer hand.
10021{_}3190{_}She didn't see why she should, for instance, deprive herself of the pleasure of seeing Aylmer; she had not told him anything about it.
10021{_}3191{_}That day at the club, Bruce in his depression had a chat with Goldthorpe, his golfing companion and sometime confidant.
10021{_}3192{_}Over a cigarette and other refreshments, Bruce murmured how he had put an end to the little affair for the sake of his wife.
10021{_}3193{_}'Rather jolly little girl, she was.'
10021{_}3194{_}'Oh yes,' said Goldthorpe indifferently.
10021{_}3195{_}He thought Edith very attractive, and would have liked to have the duty of consoling her.
10021{_}3196{_}'One of those girls that sort of _get round_ you, and appeal to you--_you_ know.'
10021{_}3197{_}'Oh yes.'
10021{_}3198{_}'Grey eyes--no, by Jove!
10021{_}3199{_}I should call them hazel, with black lashes, no, not exactly black--brown.
10021{_}3200{_}Nice, white teeth, slim figure--perhaps a bit too straight.
10021{_}3201{_}Brownish hair with a tinge of gold in the sun.'
10021{_}3202{_}'Oh yes.'
10021{_}3203{_}'About twenty,' continued Bruce dreamily.
10021{_}3204{_}He knew that Miss Townsend was thirty-two, but suspected Goldthorpe of admiring flappers, and so, with a subconscious desire to impress him, rearranged the lady's age.
10021{_}3205{_}'About twenty--if that.
10021{_}3206{_}Rather long, thin hands--the hands of a lady.
10021{_}3207{_}Well, it's all over now.'
10021{_}3208{_}'That's all right,' said Goldthorpe.
10021{_}3209{_}He seemed to have had enough of this retrospective inventory.
10021{_}3210{_}He looked at his watch and found he had an appointment.
10021{_}3211{_}Bruce, thinking he seemed jealous, smiled to himself.
10021{_}3212{_}For a few days after what had passed there was a happy reaction in the house.
10021{_}3213{_}Everyone was almost unnaturally sweet and polite and unselfish about trifles to everybody else.
10021{_}3214{_}Edith was devoting herself to the children, Bruce had less of her society than usual.
10021{_}3215{_}She seemed to assume they were to be like brother and sister.
10021{_}3216{_}He wouldn't at present raise the question; thinking she would soon get over such a rotten idea.
10021{_}3217{_}Besides, a great many people had left town; and they were, themselves, in the rather unsettled state of intending to go away in a fortnight.
10021{_}3218{_}Though happy at getting off so easily, Bruce was really missing the meetings and notes (rather than the girl).
10021{_}3219{_}Fortunately, Vincy now returned; he was looking sunburnt and happy.
10021{_}3220{_}He had been having a good time.
10021{_}3221{_}Yet he looked a little anxious occasionally, as if perplexed.

10021{_}3223{_}'I guess who she is.

10021{_}3227{_}'Yes.'
