10021{_}3343{_}*       *       *       *       *  Before the end of the drive, which for him was a sort of adventure, Mavis had promised to meet Bruce when she left her Art School next Tuesday at a certain tea-shop in Bond Street.
10021{_}3344{_}Bruce went home happy and in good spirits again.
10021{_}3345{_}There was no earthly harm in being kind to a poor little girl like this.
10021{_}3346{_}He might do a great deal of good.
10021{_}3347{_}She seemed to admire him.
10021{_}3348{_}She thought him so clever.
10021{_}3349{_}Funny thing, there was no doubt he had the gift; women liked him, and there you are.
10021{_}3350{_}Look at Miss Mooney at the Mitchells' the other day, why, she was ever so nice to him; went for him like one o'clock; but he gave her no encouragement.
10021{_}3351{_}Edith was there.

10021{_}3357{_}She was going to tell him all about it on Tuesday.
10021{_}3358{_}That man must have been a fiend!...
10021{_}3359{_}'Holloa, Vincy!
10021{_}3360{_}So glad you're still here.
10021{_}3361{_}Let's have dinner, Edie.'
10021{_}3362{_}CHAPTER XXIII  At Lady Everard's  Lady Everard was sitting in her favourite attitude at her writing-table, with her face turned to the door.
10021{_}3363{_}She had once been photographed at her writing-table, with a curtain behind her, and her face turned to the door.
10021{_}3364{_}The photograph had appeared in _The Queen, The Ladies' Field, The Sketch, The Taller, The Bystander, Home Chat, Home Notes, The Woman at Home_, and _Our Stately Homes of England_.
10021{_}3365{_}It was a favourite photograph of hers; she had taken a fancy to it, and therefore she always liked to be found in this position.
10021{_}3366{_}The photo had been called: 'Lady Everard at work in her Music-Room.'
10021{_}3367{_}What she was supposed to be working at, heaven only knew; for she never wrote a line of anything, and even her social notes and invitation cards were always written by her secretary.
10021{_}3368{_}As soon as a visitor came in, she rose from the suspiciously clean writing-table, put down the dry pen on a spotless blotter, went and sat in a large brocaded arm-chair in front of some palms, within view of the piano, and began to talk.
10021{_}3369{_}The music-room was large, splendid and elaborately decorated.
10021{_}3370{_}There was a frieze all round, representing variously coloured and somewhat shapeless creatures playing what were supposed to be musical instruments.
10021{_}3371{_}One, in a short blue skirt, was blowing at something; another in pink drapery (who squinted) was strumming on a lyre; other figures were in white, with their mouths open like young birds preparing to be fed by older birds.
10021{_}3372{_}They represented Harmony in all its forms.
10021{_}3373{_}There were other attempts at the classical in the decoration of the room; but Lady Everard herself had reduced this idea to bathos by huge quantities of signed photographs in silver frames, by large waste-paper baskets, lined with blue satin and trimmed with pink rosettes, by fans which were pockets, stuffed cats which were paperweights, oranges which were pincushions, and other debris from those charitable and social bazaars of which she was a constant patroness.
10021{_}3374{_}With her usual curious combination of weak volubility and decided laying-down of the law, she was preparing to hold forth to young La France (whom she expected), on the subject of Debussy, Edvina, Marcoux, the appalling singing of all his young friends, his own good looks, and other subjects of musical interest, when Mr Cricker was announced.
10021{_}3375{_}She greeted him with less eagerness, if less patronage, than her other protege, but graciously offered him tea and permitted a cigarette.
10021{_}3376{_}Lady Everard went in for being at once _grande dame_ and Bohemian.
10021{_}3377{_}She was truly good-natured and kind, except to rivals in her own sphere, but when jealous she was rather redoubtable.
10021{_}3378{_}'I'm pleased to see you, my dear Willie,' she said; 'all the more because I hear Mrs Mitchell has taken Wednesdays now.
10021{_}3379{_}Not _quite_ a nice thing to do, I think; although, after all, I suppose we could hardly really clash.
10021{_}3380{_}True, we _do_ happen to know a few of the same people.'
10021{_}3381{_}(By that Lady Everard meant she had snatched as many of Mrs Mitchell's friends away as she thought desirable.)
10021{_}3382{_}'But as a general rule I suppose we're not really in the same set.
10021{_}3383{_}But perhaps you're going on there afterwards?'
10021{_}3384{_}That had been Mr Clicker's intention, but he denied it, with surprise and apparent pain at the suspicion.
10021{_}3385{_}She settled down more comfortably.
10021{_}3386{_}'Ah, well, Mrs Mitchell is an extremely nice, hospitable woman, and her parties are, I know, considered _quite_ amusing, but I do think--I really do--that her husband carries his practical jokes and things a _little_ too far.
10021{_}3387{_}It isn't good form, it really isn't, to see a man of his age, with his face blacked, coming in after dinner with a banjo, calling himself the Musical White-eyed Kaffir, as he did the last time I was there.
10021{_}3388{_}I find it _deplace_--that's the word, _deplace_.
10021{_}3389{_}He seemed to think that we were all children at a juvenile party!
10021{_}3390{_}I was saying so to Lord Rye only last night.
10021{_}3391{_}Lord Rye likes it, I think, but he says Mr Mitchell's mad--that's what it is, a little mad.
10021{_}3392{_}Last time Lord Rye was there everybody had a present given them hidden in their table napkins.
10021{_}3393{_}There had been some mistake in the parcels, I believe, and Miss Mooney--you know, the actress, Myra Mooney--received a safety razor, and Lord Rye a vanity bag.
10021{_}3394{_}Everybody screamed with laughter, but I must say it seemed to me rather silly.
10021{_}3395{_}I wasn't there myself.'
10021{_}3396{_}'I was,' said Mr Cricker.
10021{_}3397{_}'I got a very pretty little feather fan.
10021{_}3398{_}I suppose the things really had been mixed up, and after all I was very glad of the fan; I was able to give it to--' He stopped, sighed and looked down on the floor.
10021{_}3399{_}'And is that affair still going on, Willie dear?
10021{_}3400{_}It seems to me _such_ a pity.
10021{_}3401{_}I _do_ wish you would try and give it up.'
10021{_}3402{_}'I know, but she _won't_,' he said in a voice hoarse with anxiety.
10021{_}3403{_}'Dear Lady Everard, you're a woman of the world, and know everything--'  She smiled.
10021{_}3404{_}'Not everything, Willie; a little of music, perhaps.
10021{_}3405{_}I know a good voice when I hear it.
10021{_}3406{_}I have a certain _flair_ for what's going to be a success in that direction, and of course I've been everywhere and seen everything.
10021{_}3407{_}I've a certain natural knowledge of life, too, and keep well up to date with everything that's going on.
10021{_}3408{_}I knew about the Hendon Divorce Case long before anyone else, though it never came off after all, but that's not the point.
10021{_}3409{_}But then I'm so discreet; people tell me things.
10021{_}3410{_}At any rate, I always _know_.'
10021{_}3411{_}Indeed, Lady Everard firmly believed herself to be a great authority on most subjects, but especially on contemporary gossip.
10021{_}3412{_}This was a delusion.
10021{_}3413{_}In reality she had that marvellous talent for not knowing things, that gift for ignorance, and genius for inaccuracy so frequently seen in that cultured section of society of which she was so popular and distinguished a member.
10021{_}3414{_}It is a talent that rarely fails to please, particularly in a case like her own.
10021{_}3415{_}There is always a certain satisfaction in knowing that a woman of position and wealth, who plumes herself on her early knowledge and special information, is absolutely and entirely devoid of the one and incorrect in the other.
10021{_}3416{_}A marked ignorance in a professionally well-informed person has always something touching and appealing to those who are able, if not willing, to set that person right.
10021{_}3417{_}It was taken for granted among her acquaintances, and probably was one of the qualities that endeared her to them most, that dear Lady Everard was generally positive and always wrong.
10021{_}3418{_}'Yes, I do know most things, perhaps,' she said complacently.
10021{_}3419{_}'And one thing I know is that this woman friend of yours is making you perfectly miserable.
10021{_}3420{_}You're longing to shake it off.
10021{_}3421{_}Ah, I know you!
10021{_}3422{_}You've far more real happiness in going to the opera with me than even in seeing her, and the more she pursues you the less you like it.
10021{_}3423{_}Am I not right?'
10021{_}3424{_}'Yes, I suppose so.
10021{_}3425{_}But as a matter of fact, Lady Everard, if she didn't--well--what you might call make a dash for it, I shouldn't worry about her at all.'
10021{_}3426{_}'Men,' continued Lady Everard, not listening, 'only like coldness; coldness, reserve.
10021{_}3427{_}The only way in the world to draw a man on is to be always out to him, or to go away, and never even let him hear your name mentioned.'
10021{_}3428{_}'I daresay there's a lot in that,' said Cricker, wondering why she did not try that plan with young La France.
10021{_}3429{_}'Women of the present day,' she continued, growing animated, 'make such a terrible, terrible mistake I What do they do when they like a young man?
10021{_}3430{_}Oh, I know!
10021{_}3431{_}They write to him at his club; they call at his rooms and leave messages; they telephone whenever they can.
10021{_}3432{_}The more he doesn't answer their invitations the more they invite him.
10021{_}3433{_}It's appalling!
10021{_}3434{_}And what's the result?
10021{_}3435{_}Men are becoming cooler and cooler-- as a class, I mean.
10021{_}3436{_}Of course, there are exceptions.
10021{_}3437{_}But it's such a mistake of women to run after the few young men there are.
10021{_}3438{_}There are such a tremendous lot of girls and married women nowadays, there are so many more of them.'
10021{_}3439{_}'Well, perhaps that's why they do it,' said Cricker rather stupidly.
10021{_}3440{_}'At any rate--oh, well, I know if my friend hadn't been so jolly nice to me at first and kept it up so--oh, well, you know what I mean--kept on keeping on, if I may use the expression, I should have drifted away from her ages ago.
10021{_}3441{_}Because, you see, supposing I'm beginning to think about something else, or somebody else, she doesn't stand it; she won't stand it.
10021{_}3442{_}And the awkward part is, you see, her being _on_ the stage _and_ married makes the whole thing about as awkward as a case of that sort can possibly be.'
10021{_}3443{_}'I would not ask you her name for the world,' said Lady Everard smoothly.
10021{_}3444{_}'Of course I know she's a beautiful young comedy actress, or is it tragedy?
10021{_}3445{_}I wonder if I could guess her first name?
10021{_}3446{_}Will you tell me if I guess right?'
10021{_}3447{_}She looked arch.
10021{_}3448{_}'Oh, I say, I can't tell you who it is, Lady Everard; really not.'
10021{_}3449{_}'Only the first name?

10021{_}3451{_}The Christian name is not Margaretta, is it?
10021{_}3452{_}Ah!
10021{_}3453{_}no, I thought not.
10021{_}3454{_}It's Irene Pettifer!
10021{_}3455{_}There, I've guessed.
10021{_}3456{_}The fact is, I always knew it, my dear boy.
10021{_}3457{_}Your secret is safe with me.
10021{_}3458{_}I'm the tomb!
10021{_}3459{_}I--'  'Excuse me, Lady Everard,' said Cricker, with every sign of annoyance, 'it's no more Irene Pettifer than it's you yourself.
10021{_}3460{_}Please believe me.

10021{_}3462{_}'What did I tell you?
10021{_}3463{_}I knew it wasn't; I only said that to draw you.

10021{_}3475{_}It might give me an idea anyway.'
10021{_}3476{_}'All right.'
10021{_}3477{_}She leant back and half closed her eyes, as if in thought; then started up with one finger out.

10021{_}3481{_}July 7th, 1912.
10021{_}3482{_}Have you got that?'
10021{_}3483{_}'Yes, I've got that.'

10021{_}3485{_}Thursday looks more casual, more full of feeling than the exact date.

10021{_}3500{_}Ever see you or meet you again.

10021{_}3502{_}'Oh yes.'
10021{_}3503{_}'Then say: Duty has to come between us, but God knows I wish you well.'
10021{_}3504{_}Tears were beginning to come to Lady Everard's eyes, and she spoke with a break in her voice.

10021{_}3511{_}How do you sign yourself?'
10021{_}3512{_}'Oh, Willie.'
10021{_}3513{_}'Very well then, be more serious this time: Always your faithful friend, William Stacey Cricker.'
10021{_}3514{_}He glanced over the note, his face falling more and more, while Lady Everard looked more and more satisfied.
10021{_}3515{_}'Copy that out, word for word, the moment you go back, and send it off,' she said, 'and all the worst of your troubles will be over.'
10021{_}3516{_}'I should think the worst is yet to come,' said he ruefully.
10021{_}3517{_}'But you promise to do it, Willie?
10021{_}3518{_}Oh, promise me?'

10021{_}3521{_}'O Lord, yes.

10021{_}3524{_}Ah!
10021{_}3525{_}here you are, cher maitre!'

10021{_}3527{_}'Good heavens!'

10021{_}3529{_}She'd show it to her husband, and he'd come round and knock me into a cocked hat for it.
10021{_}3530{_}Dear Lady Everard--she's a dear, but she doesn't know anything about anything.'
10021{_}3531{_}He tore the pages out of his pocket-book, and called out to the cabman the address of the Mitchells.
10021{_}3532{_}'Ah, chere madame, que je suis fatigue!'
10021{_}3533{_}exclaimed La France, as he threw himself back against the cushions.
10021{_}3534{_}His hair was long and smooth and fair, so fair that he had been spoken of by jealous singers as a peroxide blond.
10021{_}3535{_}His eyes were greenish, and he had dark eyebrows and eyelashes.
10021{_}3536{_}He was good-looking.
10021{_}3537{_}His voice in speaking was harsh, but his manner soft and insidious.
10021{_}3538{_}His talents were cosmopolitan; his tastes international; he had no duties, few pleasures and that entire want of leisure known only to those who have practically nothing whatever to do.
10021{_}3539{_}'Fatigued?
10021{_}3540{_}That's what you always say,' said Lady Everard, laughing.
10021{_}3541{_}'But it is always true,' he said, with a strong French accent.
10021{_}3542{_}'You should take more exercise, Paul.
10021{_}3543{_}Go out more in the air.
10021{_}3544{_}You lead too secluded a life.'
10021{_}3545{_}'What exercises?
10021{_}3546{_}I practise my voice every day, twenty minutes.'
10021{_}3547{_}'Ah, but I didn't mean that.
10021{_}3548{_}I mean in the open air--sport--that sort of thing.'
10021{_}3549{_}'Ah, you wish I go horseback riding.
10021{_}3550{_}Ver' nice, but not for me.
10021{_}3551{_}I have never did it.
10021{_}3552{_}I cannot begun now, Lady Everard.
10021{_}3553{_}I spoil all the _veloute_ of my voice.
10021{_}3554{_}Have you seen again that pretty little lady I met here before?
10021{_}3555{_}Delicious light brown hair, pretty blue eyes, a wonderful blue, a blue that seem to say to everyone something different.'
10021{_}3556{_}'What!'
10021{_}3557{_}exclaimed Lady Everard.

10021{_}3559{_}She calmed down again.
10021{_}3560{_}'Oh yes, she's charming, awfully sweet--devoted to her husband, you know--absolutely devoted to her husband; so rare and delightful nowadays in London.'
10021{_}3561{_}'Oh yes, ver' nice.
10021{_}3562{_}Me, I am devoted to 'er husband too.
10021{_}3563{_}I go to see him.

10021{_}3565{_}'What, without _me_?'
10021{_}3566{_}exclaimed Lady Everard.

10021{_}3568{_}He ask me to come round and sing him a song.

10021{_}3579{_}'He could not sing for nuts.'

10021{_}3581{_}'If you say I was seen, I was.
10021{_}3582{_}You could not know I talk to her unless I was seen.
10021{_}3583{_}You could not know by wireless.'

10021{_}3586{_}'Who did it?

10021{_}3588{_}I didn't do anything.'

10021{_}3590{_}'But what could I say?
10021{_}3591{_}It is ver' difficul.
10021{_}3592{_}I walk through the park; she walk through it with another lady.

10021{_}3599{_}I am not responsible for the persons that walk in their own park in their own country.
10021{_}3600{_}She might answer me to go to the devil!

10021{_}3602{_}What that got to do with it?
10021{_}3603{_}In a case like this, chere madame, I have no legal power.'
10021{_}3604{_}She laughed forgivingly and said:  'Ah, well, one mustn't be too exacting!'
10021{_}3605{_}and as she showed some signs of a desire to pat his hair he rose, sat down to the piano, greatly to her disappointment, and filled up the rest of the time by improvising (from memory).
10021{_}3606{_}It was a little fatiguing, as she thought it her duty to keep up an expression of acute rapture during the whole of the performance, which lasted at least three-quarters of an hour.
10021{_}3607{_}CHAPTER XXIV  Miss Bennett  Since his return Aylmer saw everything through what he called a rose-coloured microscope--that is to say, every detail of his life, and everything connected with it, seemed to him perfect.
10021{_}3608{_}He saw Edith as much as ever, and far less formally than before.
10021{_}3609{_}She treated him with affectionate ease.
10021{_}3610{_}She had admitted by her behaviour on the night he returned that she cared for him, and, for the moment, that was enough.
10021{_}3611{_}A sort of general relaxation of formality, due to the waning of the season, and to people being too busy to bother, or already in thought away, seemed to give a greater freedom.
10021{_}3612{_}Everyone seemed more natural, and more satisfied to follow their own inclinations and let other people follow theirs.
10021{_}3613{_}London was getting stale and tired, and the last feverish flickers of the exhausted season alternated with a kind of languor in which nobody bothered much about anybody else's affairs.
10021{_}3614{_}General interest was exhausted, and only a strong sense of self-preservation seemed to be left; people clung desperately to their last hopes.
10021{_}3615{_}Edith was curiously peaceful and contented.
10021{_}3616{_}She would have had scarcely any leisure but that her mother-in-law sometimes relieved her of the care of the children.
10021{_}3617{_}Being very anxious that they should not lose anything from Miss Townsend's absence, she gave them lessons every day.
10021{_}3618{_}One day, at the end of a history lesson, Archie said:  'Where's Miss Townsend?'
10021{_}3619{_}'She's at Bexhill.'
10021{_}3620{_}'Why is she at Bexhill?'
10021{_}3621{_}'Because she likes it.'
10021{_}3622{_}'Where's Bexhill?'
10021{_}3623{_}'In England.'
10021{_}3624{_}'Why isn't Miss Townsend?'
10021{_}3625{_}'What do you mean, Archie?'
10021{_}3626{_}'Well, why isn't she Miss Townsend any more?'
10021{_}3627{_}'She is.'
10021{_}3628{_}'But she's not our Miss Townsend any more.
10021{_}3629{_}Why isn't she?'
10021{_}3630{_}'She's gone away.'
10021{_}3631{_}'Isn't she coming back?'
10021{_}3632{_}'No.'
10021{_}3633{_}Watching his mother's face he realised that she didn't regret this, so he said:  'Is Miss Townsend teaching anybody else?'
10021{_}3634{_}'I daresay she is, or she will, perhaps.'
10021{_}3635{_}'What are their names?'
10021{_}3636{_}'How should I know?'

10021{_}3638{_}'It's possible.'
10021{_}3639{_}'I wonder if she'll ever be cross with the next boy she teaches.'
10021{_}3640{_}'Miss Townsend was very kind to you,' said Edith.
10021{_}3641{_}'But you need not think about her any more, because you will be going to school when you come back from the holidays.'
10021{_}3642{_}'That's what I told Dilly,' said Archie.
10021{_}3643{_}'But Dilly's not going to school.
10021{_}3644{_}Dilly doesn't mind; she says she likes you better than Miss Townsend.'
10021{_}3645{_}'Very kind of her, I'm sure,' laughed Edith.
10021{_}3646{_}'You see you're not a real governess,' said Archie, putting his arm round her neck.

10021{_}3648{_}Because you're not a real one it's more fun for us.'
10021{_}3649{_}'How do you mean, I'm not a real governess?'
10021{_}3650{_}'Well, I mean we're not _obliged_ to do what you tell us!'
10021{_}3651{_}'Oh, aren't you?
10021{_}3652{_}You've got to; you're to go now because I expect Miss Bennett.'
10021{_}3653{_}'Can't I see Miss Bennett?'

10021{_}3655{_}'I don't want to see her; but she always brings parcels.
10021{_}3656{_}I like to see the parcels.'
10021{_}3657{_}'They are not for you; she brings parcels because I ask her to do shopping for me.
10021{_}3658{_}It's very kind of her.'

10021{_}3660{_}I'll be very useful.
10021{_}3661{_}I can untie parcels with my teeth, like this.

10021{_}3663{_}I throw myself on the parcel just like a dog, and shake it and shake it, and then I untie it with my teeth.
10021{_}3664{_}It would be awfully useful.'
10021{_}3665{_}She refused the kind offer.
10021{_}3666{_}Miss Bennett arrived as usual with the parcels, looking pleasantly business-like and important.

10021{_}3700{_}At this moment he arrived.

10021{_}3703{_}'What sort of shopping?
10021{_}3704{_}I'll tell you what you ought to have--a great cricket match when the shopping season's over, between the Old Selfridgians, and the Old Harrodians,' he said, laughing.
10021{_}3705{_}They walked through acres of oil paintings and dozens of portraits of Chief Justices.
10021{_}3706{_}'I can't imagine anyone but Royalty enjoying these pictures,' said Edith.
10021{_}3707{_}'They don't go to see pictures; they go to view exhibits,' Aylmer answered.
10021{_}3708{_}Declaring they had 'Academy headache' before they had been through the second room, they sat down and watched the people.
10021{_}3709{_}One sees people there that are to be seen nowhere else.
10021{_}3710{_}An extraordinary large number of clergymen, a peculiar kind of provincial, and strange Londoners, almost impossible to place, in surprising clothes.
10021{_}3711{_}Then they gave it up, and Aylmer took them out to lunch at a club almost as huge and noisy and as miscellaneous as the Academy itself.
10021{_}3712{_}However, they thoroughly enjoyed themselves.
10021{_}3713{_}Edith and Bruce were to take up their abode in their little country house at Westgate next day.
10021{_}3714{_}CHAPTER XXV  At Westgate  'I've got to go up to town on special business,' said Bruce, one afternoon, after receiving a telegram which he had rather ostentatiously left about, hoping he would be questioned on the subject.
10021{_}3715{_}It had, however, been persistently disregarded.
10021{_}3716{_}'Oh, have you?'
10021{_}3717{_}'Yes.
10021{_}3718{_}Look at this wire.'
10021{_}3719{_}He read aloud:  '_Wish to see you at once if possible come up today M_.'
10021{_}3720{_}'Who _is_ 'M'?'
10021{_}3721{_}'Mitchell, of course.
10021{_}3722{_}Who should it be?'
10021{_}3723{_}He spoke aggressively, then softened down to explanation, 'Mitchell's in town a few days on business, too.
10021{_}3724{_}I may be detained till Tuesday--or even Wednesday next.'
10021{_}3725{_}Bruce had been to town so often lately, his manner was so vague, he seemed at once so happy and so preoccupied, so excited, so pleased, so worried, and yet so unnaturally good-tempered, that Edith had begun to suspect he was seeing Miss Townsend again.
10021{_}3726{_}The suspicion hurt her, for he had given his word of honour, and had been nice to her ever since, and amiable (though rather absent and bored) with the children.
10021{_}3727{_}She walked down to the station with him, though he wished to go in the cab which took his box and suit-case, but he did not resist her wish.
10021{_}3728{_}On the way he said, looking round as if he had only just arrived and had never seen it before:  'This is a very nice little place.
10021{_}3729{_}It's just the right place for you and the children.
10021{_}3730{_}If I were you, I should stay on here.'
10021{_}3731{_}It struck her he spoke in a very detached way, and some odd foreshadowing came to her.
10021{_}3732{_}'Why--aren't you coming back?'
10021{_}3733{_}she asked jokingly.
10021{_}3734{_}'Me?

10021{_}3736{_}Yes, of course.
10021{_}3737{_}But I've told you--this business of mine--well, it'll take a little time to arrange.
10021{_}3738{_}Still, I expect to be back on Tuesday.
10021{_}3739{_}Or quite on Wednesday--or sooner.'
10021{_}3740{_}They walked on and had nearly reached the station.
10021{_}3741{_}'How funny you are, Bruce!'
10021{_}3742{_}'What do you mean?

10021{_}3745{_}You've got plenty of companions.

10021{_}3753{_}How like a woman!
10021{_}3754{_}It's just business.'
10021{_}3755{_}He waited a minute.
10021{_}3756{_}'Stockbroking; that's what it is.
10021{_}3757{_}Yes, it's stockbroking.
10021{_}3758{_}I want to see a chap who's put me in to a good thing.
10021{_}3759{_}A perfectly safe thing.
10021{_}3760{_}No gambling.
10021{_}3761{_}But one has to see into it, you see.

10021{_}3766{_}Aren't you satisfied with my explanation?'
10021{_}3767{_}'_You_ appear to be--very.
10021{_}3768{_}But I'm not asking you to tell me any details about the business, whatever it may be.'
10021{_}3769{_}They arrived at the station, and Bruce gave her what she thought a very queer look.
10021{_}3770{_}It was a mixture of fear, daring, caution and a sort of bravado.

10021{_}3772{_}'Tell me, frankly, something I'd like to know, Bruce.'

10021{_}3774{_}That's not like you.

10021{_}3782{_}'Thanks.

10021{_}3798{_}She still lingered, seeing something odd in his manner.

10021{_}3800{_}'I say--' Edith.'
10021{_}3801{_}'Yes, dear?'
10021{_}3802{_}'Oh, nothing.'
10021{_}3803{_}She waited on till the train started.
10021{_}3804{_}His manner was alternately peevish and kind, but altogether odd.
10021{_}3805{_}Her last glimpse was a rather pale smile from Bruce as he waved his hand and then turned to his paper....  'Well, what _does_ it matter so long as he _has_ gone!'
10021{_}3806{_}exclaimed Aylmer impatiently, when she expressed her wonder at Bruce's going.
10021{_}3807{_}The tide was low, and they went for a long walk over the hard shining sand, followed by Archie picking up wonderful shells and slipping on the green seaweed.
10021{_}3808{_}Everything seemed fresh, lovely.
10021{_}3809{_}She herself was as fresh as the sea breeze, and Aylmer seemed to her as strong as the sea.
10021{_}3810{_}(Privately, Edith thought him irresistible in country clothes.)
10021{_}3811{_}Edith had everything here to make her happy, including Bruce's mother, who relieved her of the children when she wanted rest and in whose eyes she was perfection.
10021{_}3812{_}She saw restrained adoration in Aylmer's eyes, love and trust in the eyes of the children.
10021{_}3813{_}She had all she wanted.
10021{_}3814{_}And yet--something tugged at her heart, and worried her.
10021{_}3815{_}She had a strange and melancholy presentiment.
10021{_}3816{_}But she threw it off.
10021{_}3817{_}Probably there was nothing really wrong with Bruce; perhaps only one of those little imaginary romances that he liked to fabricate for himself; or, perhaps, it was really business?
10021{_}3818{_}It was all right if Mr Mitchell knew about it.
10021{_}3819{_}Yet she could not believe that 'M' _was_ Mitchell.
10021{_}3820{_}Bruce had repeated it too often; and, why on earth should Mitchell suddenly take to sending Bruce fantastic telegrams and signing them, for no reason, with an initial?...
10021{_}3821{_}CHAPTER XXVI  Goggles  'What divine heavenly pets and ducks of angels they are!'
10021{_}3822{_}exclaimed Lady Everard rather distractedly.
10021{_}3823{_}'Angels!
10021{_}3824{_}Divine!
10021{_}3825{_}And so good, too!
10021{_}3826{_}I never saw such darlings in my life.
10021{_}3827{_}Look at them, Paul.
10021{_}3828{_}Aren't they sweet?'
10021{_}3829{_}Lady Everard with her party (what Aylmer called her performing troupe) had driven over to Westgate, from where she was staying in the neighbourhood, to have tea with Edith.
10021{_}3830{_}She had brought with her a sort of juvenile party, consisting of Mr Cricker, Captain Willis and, of course, Paul La France, the young singer.
10021{_}3831{_}She never moved without him.

10021{_}3957{_}CHAPTER XXVII  The Elopement  The following Tuesday, Edith, Aylmer, Vincy and Mrs Ottley were sitting on the veranda after dinner.
10021{_}3958{_}They had a charming little veranda which led on to a lawn, and from there straight down to the sea.
10021{_}3959{_}It was their custom to sit there in the evening and talk.
10021{_}3960{_}The elder Mrs Ottley enjoyed these evenings, and the most modern conversation never seemed to startle her.
10021{_}3961{_}She would listen impassively, or with a smile, as if in silent approval, to the most monstrous of paradoxes or the most childish chaff.
10021{_}3962{_}Aylmer's attention and kind thought for her had absolutely won her heart.
10021{_}3963{_}She consulted him about everything, and was only thoroughly satisfied when he was there.
10021{_}3964{_}His strong, kind, decided voice, his good looks, his decision, and a sort of responsible impulsiveness, all appealed to her immensely.
10021{_}3965{_}She looked up to him, in a kind of admiring maternal way; Edith often wondered, did she not see Aylmer's devotion?
10021{_}3966{_}But, if she did, Mrs Ottley thought nothing of it.
10021{_}3967{_}Her opinion of Edith was so high that she trusted her in any complications....  'Isn't Bruce coming down tonight?'
10021{_}3968{_}she asked Edith.
10021{_}3969{_}'I'm to have a wire.'
10021{_}3970{_}'Ah, here's the last post.
10021{_}3971{_}Perhaps he's written instead.'
10021{_}3972{_}Vincy fetched the letters.
10021{_}3973{_}There was one from Bruce.
10021{_}3974{_}Edith went into the drawing-room to read it; there was not sufficient light on the veranda....
10021{_}3975{_}In growing amazement she read the following words:--  DEAR EDITH,  'I hope what I am about to tell you will not worry you too much.
10021{_}3976{_}At any rate I do hope you will not allow it to affect your health.
10021{_}3977{_}It is inevitable, and you must make up your mind to it as soon as possible.
10021{_}3978{_}I say this in no spirit of unkindness; far from it.
10021{_}3979{_}It is hard to me to break the news to you, but it must be done.
10021{_}3980{_}'Mavis Argles and I are all in all to each other.
10021{_}3981{_}We have made up our minds on account of certain _circumstances_ to throw in our lot together, and we are starting for Australia today.
10021{_}3982{_}When this reaches you, we shall have started.
10021{_}3983{_}I enclose the address to write to me.
10021{_}3984{_}'In taking this step I have, I am sure, acted for the best.
10021{_}3985{_}It may cause you great surprise and pain.
10021{_}3986{_}I regret it, but we met and became very quickly devoted to one another.
10021{_}3987{_}She cannot live without me.
10021{_}3988{_}What I am doing is my duty.
10021{_}3989{_}I now ask you, and believe you will grant my request, to make arrangements to _give me my freedom as soon as possible_.
10021{_}3990{_}Mind you do this, Edith, for it is really my duty to give my name to Mavis, who, as I have said, is devoted to me heart and soul, and cannot live without me.
10021{_}3991{_}'I shall always have the greatest regard and respect for you, and _wish you well_.
10021{_}3992{_}'I am sorry also about my mother, but you must try and explain that it is for the best.
10021{_}3993{_}You also will know exactly what to do, and how to bring up the children just as well without me as with.
10021{_}3994{_}'Hoping this sudden news will not affect your health in any way, and that you will try and stay on a good while at Westgate, as I am sure the air is doing you good, believe me, yours affectionately as always,  'BRUCE.
10021{_}3995{_}'_P.S._--Mind you don't forget to divorce me as soon as you can for Mavis's sake.
10021{_}3996{_}Vincy will give you all the advice you need.
10021{_}3997{_}Don't think badly of me; I have meant well.
10021{_}3998{_}Try and cheer up.
10021{_}3999{_}I am sorry not to write more fully, but you can imagine how I was rushed to catch today's steamer.'
10021{_}4000{_}She sat alone gazing at the letter under the light.
10021{_}4001{_}She was divided at first between a desire to laugh and cry.
10021{_}4002{_}Bruce had actually eloped!
10021{_}4003{_}His silly weakness had culminated, his vanity had been got hold of.
10021{_}4004{_}Vincy's horrid little art-student had positively led him into running away, and leaving his wife and children.
10021{_}4005{_}Controlling herself, Edith went to the veranda and said to Mrs Ottley that Bruce wasn't coming back for a day or two, that she had neuralgia and was going to retire, but begged Aylmer not to go yet.
10021{_}4006{_}Of course at this he went at once.
10021{_}4007{_}The next morning Aylmer at his hotel received a little note asking him to come round and see Edith, while the others were out.

10021{_}4115{_}She took it out of her bag.
10021{_}4116{_}It was directed to Bruce, at the address he had given her in Australia.

10021{_}4124{_}You shall read it because I trust you, because I really care for you.'

10021{_}4126{_}'No, dear; keep it to yourself.'
10021{_}4127{_}His delicacy had revived and he was ashamed of his jealousy.
10021{_}4128{_}But now she insisted on showing it to him, and he read:  'DEAR BRUCE,  'I'm not going to make any appeal to your feelings with regard to your mother and the children, because if you had thought even of me a little this would not have happened.
10021{_}4129{_}I'm very, very sorry for it.
10021{_}4130{_}I believe it happened from your weakness and foolishness, or you could not have behaved with such irresponsibility, but I'm trying to look at it quite calmly.
10021{_}4131{_}I therefore propose to do nothing at all for three months.
10021{_}4132{_}If I acted on your suggestion you might regret it ever after.

10021{_}4134{_}But in case you change your mind I propose to tell nobody, not even your mother.

10021{_}4148{_}Let me know within three months how you feel about it.
10021{_}4149{_}No-one will suffer except myself during this time, as I shall keep it from your mother, and shall remain here during this time.
10021{_}4150{_}Perhaps you will be very angry with me that I don't wish to take you at your word, Bruce.
10021{_}4151{_}At first I thought I would, but I'm doing what I think right, and one cannot do more.
10021{_}4152{_}'I'm not going to reproach you, for if you don't feel the claims of others on you, my words will make no difference.
10021{_}4153{_}'Think over what I say.
10021{_}4154{_}Should you be unhappy and wish to separate from her without knowing how, and if it becomes a question of money, as so many things do, I would help you.
10021{_}4155{_}I did not remind you about your father's legacy to induce you to come back.
10021{_}4156{_}If you really find happiness in the way you expect, we could arrange it.
10021{_}4157{_}You see, I have thought of everything, in one night.
10021{_}4158{_}But you _won't_ be happy.
10021{_}4159{_}'EDITH OTTLEY.'
10021{_}4160{_}'Remember, whenever you like to come back, you will be welcomed, and nothing shall ever be said about it.'
10021{_}4161{_}Aylmer gave her back the letter.
10021{_}4162{_}He was touched.
10021{_}4163{_}'You see,' she said eagerly, 'I haven't got a grain of jealousy.
10021{_}4164{_}All that part is quite finished.
10021{_}4165{_}That's the very reason why I can judge calmly.'
10021{_}4166{_}She fastened up the letter, and then said with a smile:  'And now, let's be happy the rest of the summer.
10021{_}4167{_}Won't you?'
10021{_}4168{_}He answered that she was _impayable_--marvellous--that he would help her--devote himself to doing whatever she wished.
10021{_}4169{_}On consideration he saw that there was still hope.
10021{_}4170{_}CHAPTER XXVIII  Bruce Returns  'Never, Edith!'
10021{_}4171{_}exclaimed Vincy, fixing his eyeglass in his eye, and opening his mouth in astonishment.
10021{_}4172{_}'Never!
10021{_}4173{_}Well, I'm gormed!'
10021{_}4174{_}A week had passed since the news of Bruce's elopement.
10021{_}4175{_}The little group at Westgate didn't seem to have much been affected by it; and this was the less surprising as Aylmer and Edith had kept it to themselves.
10021{_}4176{_}Mrs Ottley listened imperturbably to Edith's story, a somewhat incoherent concoction, but told with dash and decision, that Bruce had been ordered away for a sea-voyage for fear of a nervous breakdown.
10021{_}4177{_}She cried a little, said nothing, kissed Edith more than usual, and took the children away for longer walks and drives.
10021{_}4178{_}With a mother's flashlight of intuition she felt at once certain there was something wrong, but she didn't wish to probe the subject.
10021{_}4179{_}Her confidence in Edith reached the point of superstition; she would never ask her questions.
10021{_}4180{_}Edith had assured her that Bruce would come back all right, and that was enough.
10021{_}4181{_}Personally, Mrs Ottley much preferred the society of Aylmer to that of her son.
10021{_}4182{_}Aylmer was far more amusing, far more considerate to her, and to everybody else, and he didn't use his natural charm for those who amused him only, as the ordinary fascinating man does.
10021{_}4183{_}Probably there was at the back of his attentions to Mrs Ottley a vague idea that he wanted to get her on his side--that she might be a useful ally; but he was always charming to elderly women, and inclined to be brusque with younger ones, excepting Edith; he remembered his own mother with so great a cult of devotion, and his late wife with such a depressed indifference.
10021{_}4184{_}Edith had asked Aylmer to try and forget what had happened--to make himself believe that Bruce had really only gone away medicinally.
10021{_}4185{_}For the present, he did as she wished, but he was longing to begin talking to her on the subject again, both because it interested him passionately from the psychological point of view, and far more, naturally, because he had hopes of persuading her in time.
10021{_}4186{_}She was not bound by letter; she could change her mind.
10021{_}4187{_}Bruce might and possibly would, insist.
10021{_}4188{_}There was difficulty in keeping the secret from Vincy, who was actually staying in the house, and whose wonderful nerves and whimsical mind were so sensitive to every variation of his surroundings.
10021{_}4189{_}He had the gift of reading people's minds.
10021{_}4190{_}But it never annoyed anyone; one felt he had no illusions; that he sympathised with one's weaknesses and follies and, in a sense, enjoyed them, from a literary point of view.
10021{_}4191{_}Probably his friends forgave his clear vision for the sake of his interest.
10021{_}4192{_}Most people would far rather be seen through than not be seen at all.
10021{_}4193{_}One day Vincy, alone on the beach with Edith, remarked that he wondered what had happened to Mavis.
10021{_}4194{_}Edith told him that she had run away with a married man.
10021{_}4195{_}'Never, Edith!'
10021{_}4196{_}he exclaimed.
10021{_}4197{_}'Who would have thought it!
10021{_}4198{_}It seems almost too good to be true!'
10021{_}4199{_}'Don't say that, Vincy.'
10021{_}4200{_}'But how did you hear it?
10021{_}4201{_}You know everything.'
10021{_}4202{_}'I heard it on good authority.
10021{_}4203{_}I _know_ it's true.'
10021{_}4204{_}'And to think I was passing the remark only the other day that I thought I ought to look her up, in a manner of speaking, or write, _or something_,' continued Vincy; 'and who _is_ the poor dear man?
10021{_}4205{_}Do you know?'
10021{_}4206{_}He looked at her with a sudden vague suspicion of he knew not what.
10021{_}4207{_}'Bruce was always inclined to be romantic, you know,' she said steadily.
10021{_}4208{_}'Oh, give over!'
10021{_}4209{_}'Yes, that's it; I didn't want anyone to know about it.
10021{_}4210{_}I'm so afraid of making Mrs Ottley unhappy.'
10021{_}4211{_}'But you're not serious, Edith?'
10021{_}4212{_}'I suppose I'd better show you his letter.
10021{_}4213{_}He tells me to ask your advice.'
10021{_}4214{_}She gave it to him.
10021{_}4215{_}'There is only one word for what I feel about it,' Vincy said, as he gave it back.
10021{_}4216{_}'I'm gormed!
10021{_}4217{_}Simply gormed!
10021{_}4218{_}Gormed, Edith dear, is really the only word.'
10021{_}4219{_}'I'm not jealous,' said Edith.
10021{_}4220{_}'My last trouble with Bruce seems to have cured me of any feeling of the kind.
10021{_}4221{_}But I have a sort of pity and affection for him still in a way--almost like a mother!
10021{_}4222{_}I'm really afraid he will be miserable with her, and then he'll feel tied to her and be wretched all his life.
10021{_}4223{_}So I'm giving him a chance.'
10021{_}4224{_}He looked at her with admiring sympathy.
10021{_}4225{_}'But what about other friends?'
10021{_}4226{_}'Well--oh, you know--'  'Edith, I'm awfully sorry; I wish I'd married her now, then she wouldn't have bothered about Bruce.'
10021{_}4227{_}'But you can't stand her, Vincy.'
10021{_}4228{_}'I know, Edith dear; but I'd marry any number of people to prevent anything tiresome for you.
10021{_}4229{_}And Aylmer, of course--Edith, really, I think Aylmer ought to go away; I'm sure he ought.
10021{_}4230{_}It is a mistake to let him stay here under these circumstances.'
10021{_}4231{_}'Why?'
10021{_}4232{_}said Edith.

10021{_}4236{_}'How can life be like a play?

10021{_}4240{_}And I don't believe he would ever have gone away.'

10021{_}4242{_}'Why?'
10021{_}4243{_}she repeated.
10021{_}4244{_}'Nothing could be more intensely correct.
10021{_}4245{_}Mrs Ottley's staying with me--why shouldn't I have the pleasure of seeing Aylmer because Bruce is having a heavenly time on board ship?'
10021{_}4246{_}'I suppose there's that point of view,' said Vincy, rather bewildered.
10021{_}4247{_}'I say, Edith!'

10021{_}4251{_}'Yes.

10021{_}4254{_}I think I'm only really unhappy when I'm undecided.
10021{_}4255{_}Once I've taken a line--no matter what it is--I can be happy again.
10021{_}4256{_}I can adjust myself to my good fortune.'
10021{_}4257{_}Curiously, when Edith had once got over the pain and shock that the letter first gave her, she was positively happier now than she ever had been before.
10021{_}4258{_}Bruce really must have been a more formidable bore than she had known, since his absence left such a delicious freedom.
10021{_}4259{_}The certainty of having done the right, the wisest thing, was a support, a proud satisfaction.
10021{_}4260{_}During these summer days Aylmer was not so peacefully happy.
10021{_}4261{_}His devotion was assiduous, silent, discreet, and sometimes his feelings were almost uncontrollable, but he hoped; and he consoled himself by the thought that some day he would really have his wish--anything might happen; the chances were all in his favour.
10021{_}4262{_}What an extraordinary woman she was--and how pretty--how subtle; how perfect their life might be together....
10021{_}4263{_}He implored Vincy to use his influence.
10021{_}4264{_}'I can't see Edith in anything so crude as the--as--that court,' Vincy said.
10021{_}4265{_}'But Bruce begs her to do it.
10021{_}4266{_}What could their life be together afterwards?
10021{_}4267{_}It's simply a deliberate sacrifice.'
10021{_}4268{_}'There's every hope that Miss Argles will never let him go,' said Vincy.
10021{_}4269{_}'One has to be very firm to get away from her.
10021{_}4270{_}Oh, ever so firm, and _obstinate_, you can't think!
10021{_}4271{_}How many times a day she must be reproaching Bruce--that will be rather a change for him.
10021{_}4272{_}However, anything may happen,' said Vincy soothingly.
10021{_}4273{_}He still maintained, for he had a very strong sense of propriety in matters of form, that Aylmer ought to go away.
10021{_}4274{_}But Edith would not agree.
10021{_}4275{_}*       *       *       *       *  So the children played and enjoyed themselves, and sometimes asked after their father, and Mrs Ottley, though a little anxious, enjoyed herself too, and Edith had never been so happy.
10021{_}4276{_}She was having a holiday.
10021{_}4277{_}She dismissed all trouble and lived in a sort of dream.
10021{_}4278{_}*       *       *       *       *  Towards the end of the summer, hearing no more from Bruce, Aylmer grew still more hopeful; he began to regard it as practically settled.
10021{_}4279{_}The next letter in answer to Edith's would doubtless convince her, and he would then persuade her; it was, tacitly, he thought, almost agreed now; it was not spoken of between them, but he believed it was all right....        *       *       *       *       *  Aylmer had come back to London in the early days of September and was wandering through his house thinking how he would have it done up and how he wouldn't leave it when they were married, when a telephone message summoned him to Knightsbridge.
10021{_}4280{_}He went, and found the elder Mrs Ottley just going away.
10021{_}4281{_}He thought she looked at him rather strangely.
10021{_}4282{_}'I think Edith wants to speak to you,' she said, as she left the room.
10021{_}4283{_}'Dear Edith!
10021{_}4284{_}Be nice to her.'
10021{_}4285{_}And she fled.
10021{_}4286{_}*       *       *       *       *  Aylmer waited alone, looking round the room that he loved because he associated it with her.
10021{_}4287{_}It was one of the first cold damp days of the autumn, and there was a fire.
10021{_}4288{_}Edith came in, in a dark dress, looking pale, and different, he thought.
10021{_}4289{_}She had seemed the very spirit of summer only a day or two before.
10021{_}4290{_}A chill presentiment struck to his heart.
10021{_}4291{_}'You've had a letter?

10021{_}4298{_}They quarrelled on board.
10021{_}4299{_}He hates her.
10021{_}4300{_}He says he would rather die than remain with her.

10021{_}4385{_}CHAPTER XXIX  Intellectual Sympathy  'What are you going to wear tonight, Edith?'

10021{_}4388{_}I don't wish you to wear anything.
10021{_}4389{_}I'm anxious you should look your best, really nice, especially as we haven't been to the Mitchells' for so long.
10021{_}4390{_}Wear your new blue dress.'
10021{_}4391{_}'Very well.'
10021{_}4392{_}Bruce got up and walked across the room and looked in the glass.
10021{_}4393{_}'Certainly, I'm a bit sunburnt,' he remarked thoughtfully.
10021{_}4394{_}'But it doesn't suit me badly, not really badly; does it?'
10021{_}4395{_}'Not at all.'
10021{_}4396{_}'Edith.'
10021{_}4397{_}'Yes?'
10021{_}4398{_}'If I've spoken about it once, I've spoken about it forty times.
10021{_}4399{_}This ink-bottle is too full.'
10021{_}4400{_}'I'll see about it.'
10021{_}4401{_}'Don't let me have to speak about it again, will you?

10021{_}4404{_}I hope not!
10021{_}4405{_}I can't stand that woman.
10021{_}4406{_}I think she's absurd; she's a mass of affectation and prudishness.

10021{_}4408{_}'Yes?'
10021{_}4409{_}'I don't want to interfere between mother and daughter--I know you're perfectly capable and thoroughly well suited to bringing up a girl, but I really do think you're encouraging Dilly in too great extravagance.'
10021{_}4410{_}'Oh!
10021{_}4411{_}In what way?'
10021{_}4412{_}'I found her making a pinafore for her doll out of a lace flounce of real old Venetian lace.
10021{_}4413{_}Dilly said she found it on the floor.
10021{_}4414{_}'On the floor, indeed,' I said to her.
10021{_}4415{_}'You mustn't use real lace!'
10021{_}4416{_}She said, 'Why not?
10021{_}4417{_}It's a real doll!'

10021{_}4420{_}'Oh yes, of course I will.'
10021{_}4421{_}'I'm afraid my mother spoils them.
10021{_}4422{_}However, Archie will be going to school soon.
10021{_}4423{_}Of course it isn't for me to interfere.
10021{_}4424{_}I have always made a point of letting you do exactly as you like about the children, haven't I, Edith?
10021{_}4425{_}But I'm beginning to think, really, Dilly ought to have another gov--' He stopped, looking self-conscious.
10021{_}4426{_}'Oh, she's only five, quite a baby,' said Edith.
10021{_}4427{_}'I daresay I can manage her for the present.
10021{_}4428{_}Leave it to me.'
10021{_}4429{_}*       *       *       *       *  Since his return, Edith had never once referred to Bruce's sea-voyage.
10021{_}4430{_}Once or twice he had thanked her with real gratitude, and even remorse, for the line she had taken, but her one revenge had been to change the subject immediately.
10021{_}4431{_}If Bruce wished to discuss the elopement that she had so laboriously concealed, he would have to go elsewhere.
10021{_}4432{_}*       *       *       *       *  A brilliantly coloured version, glittering with success and lurid with melodrama, had been given (greatly against the hearer's will) to Goldthorpe at the club.
10021{_}4433{_}One of the most annoying things to Bruce was that he was perfectly convinced, when he was confessing the exact truth, that Goldthorpe didn't believe a word of it.
10021{_}4434{_}It was unfortunate, too, for Bruce, that he felt it incumbent on him to keep it from Vincy; and not to speak of the affair at all was a real sacrifice on Vincy's part, also.
10021{_}4435{_}For they would both have enjoyed discussing it, while Goldthorpe, the only human being in whom Bruce ever really confided, was not only bored but incredulous.
10021{_}4436{_}He considered Bruce not only tedious to the verge of imbecility, but unreliable beyond the pardonable point of inaccuracy.
10021{_}4437{_}In fact, Bruce was his ideal of the most wearisome of liars and the most untruthful of bores; and here was poor Vincy dying to hear all about his old friend, Mavis (he never knew even whether she had mentioned his name), ready to revel, with his peculiar humour, in every detail of the strange romance, particularly to enjoy her sudden desertion of Bruce for an unmarried commercial traveller who had fallen in love with her on board.--And yet, it had to be withheld!
10021{_}4438{_}Bruce felt it would be disloyal, and he had the decency to be ashamed to speak of his escapade to an intimate friend of his wife.
10021{_}4439{_}*       *       *       *       *  Bruce complained very much of the dullness of the early autumn in London without Aylmer.
10021{_}4440{_}This sudden mania for long journeys on Aylmer's part was a most annoying hobby.
10021{_}4441{_}He would never get such a pleasant friend as Aylmer again.
10021{_}4442{_}Aylmer was his hero.
10021{_}4443{_}'Why do you think he's gone away?'
10021{_}4444{_}he rather irritatingly persisted.
10021{_}4445{_}'I haven't the slightest idea.'
10021{_}4446{_}'Do you know, Edith, it has sometimes occurred to me that if--that, well--well, you know what I mean--if things had turned out differently, and you had done as I asked you--'  'Well?'
10021{_}4447{_}'Why, I have a sort of idea,' he looked away, 'that Aylmer might--well, might have proposed to you!'
10021{_}4448{_}'Oh!
10021{_}4449{_}_What_ an extraordinary idea!'
10021{_}4450{_}'But he never did show any sign whatever, I suppose of--well, of--being more interested in you than he ought to have been?'
10021{_}4451{_}'Good heavens, no!'
10021{_}4452{_}'Oh, of course, I know that--you're not his style.
10021{_}4453{_}You liked him very much, didn't you, Edith?...'
10021{_}4454{_}'I like him very much now.'
10021{_}4455{_}'However, I doubt if you ever quite appreciated him.
10021{_}4456{_}He's so full of ability; such an intellectual chap!
10021{_}4457{_}Aylmer is more a man's man.
10021{_}4458{_}_I_ miss him, of course.
10021{_}4459{_}He was a very great friend of mine.
10021{_}4460{_}And he didn't ever at all, in the least--seem to--'  'Seem to what?'
10021{_}4461{_}'It would have been a very unfair advantage to take of my absence if he had,' continued Bruce.
10021{_}4462{_}'Oh!'
10021{_}4463{_}'But he was incapable of it, of course.'
10021{_}4464{_}'Of course.'
10021{_}4465{_}'He _never_ showed any special interest, then, beyond--'  'Never.'
10021{_}4466{_}'I was right, I suppose, as usual.
10021{_}4467{_}You never appreciated him; he was not the sort of man a woman _would_ appreciate ...
10021{_}4468{_}But he's a great loss to me, Edith.
10021{_}4469{_}I need a man who can understand--Intellectual sympathy--'        *       *       *       *       *  'Mr Vincy!'
10021{_}4470{_}announced the servant.
10021{_}4471{_}Vincy had not lost his extraordinary gift for turning up at the right moment.
10021{_}4472{_}He was more welcome than ever now.
